## Chapter: Sustainable Brownfield Redevelopment:

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment represents a significant opportunity to transform underutilized or abandoned properties into productive community assets. However, the process is often fraught with environmental, economic, and social challenges. This chapter, "Sustainable Brownfield Redevelopment", aims to explore these challenges and provide practical solutions for managing them effectively.

The concept of sustainability is central to this discussion. It involves balancing the need for economic development with the imperative to protect the environment and promote social equity. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this means finding ways to clean up and reuse sites in a manner that benefits the community, the economy, and the environment.

The chapter will delve into the various aspects of sustainable brownfield redevelopment, including site assessment, remediation strategies, and community engagement. It will also explore the role of regulatory frameworks and financial incentives in promoting sustainable practices. 

While the task of redeveloping brownfields can be complex and demanding, the rewards are substantial. By adopting a sustainable approach, we can breathe new life into neglected spaces, stimulate economic growth, and contribute to a healthier, more resilient environment. 

This chapter will serve as a comprehensive guide for practitioners, policymakers, and students interested in the field of environmental management, providing them with the knowledge and tools they need to navigate the intricacies of sustainable brownfield redevelopment.

### Section: 7.1 Definition and Importance:

#### 7.1a Definition of Sustainable Redevelopment

Sustainable redevelopment is a doctrine that guides states, organizations, or individuals in recreating a socio-economic position attained prior to a deconstructive event while upholding sustainable and environmental practices. This doctrine is rooted in the principles of environmental economics, which emphasizes the integration of economic and environmental policies to achieve sustainable development. 

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, sustainable redevelopment refers to the process of transforming underutilized or abandoned properties into productive community assets in a manner that is economically viable, socially equitable, and environmentally friendly. This involves the careful management of resources, the implementation of innovative remediation strategies, and the active engagement of the community in the redevelopment process.

#### 7.1b Importance of Sustainable Redevelopment

The importance of sustainable redevelopment cannot be overstated. It offers a unique opportunity to revitalize neglected spaces, stimulate economic growth, and contribute to a healthier, more resilient environment. By investing more upfront capital, a system that is less energy intensive, less wasteful, and generally less expensive to maintain in the long run can be created. This results in long-term energy efficiencies and significantly reduced greenhouse gas emissions.

Moreover, sustainable redevelopment can play a crucial role in nation-building, particularly in post-disaster countries. In such contexts, the underlying infrastructure is often destroyed or damaged, making the situation ideal for realizing the opportunities inherent in sustainable redevelopment.

Sustainable redevelopment also aligns with the principles of sustainable development as outlined in the Kyoto Protocol's 12th Article, which speaks to "flexible mechanisms". These mechanisms allow countries that have ratified the Kyoto Protocol to lower their overall emissions through the most economical means. Theoretically, these mechanisms could be directed towards post-traumatic redevelopment, further emphasizing the importance of sustainable redevelopment in the global fight against climate change.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into the various aspects of sustainable brownfield redevelopment, including site assessment, remediation strategies, community engagement, and the role of regulatory frameworks and financial incentives in promoting sustainable practices.

#### 7.1b Importance of Sustainability in Brownfield Redevelopment

The importance of sustainability in brownfield redevelopment is multifaceted, encompassing economic, social, and environmental dimensions. 

From an economic perspective, sustainable brownfield redevelopment can stimulate local economies by creating jobs, increasing property values, and generating tax revenues. It can also reduce public health costs associated with exposure to environmental contaminants and improve the quality of life for local residents. 

Socially, sustainable brownfield redevelopment can contribute to community revitalization by transforming blighted areas into vibrant, productive spaces. It can also promote social equity by providing affordable housing and other community amenities in areas that have been historically underserved.

Environmentally, sustainable brownfield redevelopment can help conserve undeveloped land, reduce urban sprawl, and minimize the need for new infrastructure. According to a study by George Washington University, for every acre of brownfield redeveloped, 4.5 acres of undeveloped land is conserved (George Washington University, 2001). This not only helps preserve natural habitats and biodiversity, but also reduces the carbon footprint associated with new construction.

Moreover, sustainable brownfield redevelopment aligns with the principles of land recycling, which promotes smart growth and responsible, sustainable patterns of development. By reusing previously abandoned and unused land, it limits the need to build new roads, gridlines, and amenities, thereby reducing further land consumption. 

In addition, sustainable brownfield redevelopment can play a crucial role in nation-building, particularly in post-disaster countries. In such contexts, the underlying infrastructure is often destroyed or damaged, making the situation ideal for realizing the opportunities inherent in sustainable redevelopment.

In conclusion, the importance of sustainability in brownfield redevelopment cannot be overstated. It offers a unique opportunity to revitalize neglected spaces, stimulate economic growth, and contribute to a healthier, more resilient environment. By investing more upfront capital, a system that is less energy intensive, less wasteful, and generally less expensive to maintain in the long run can be created. This results in long-term energy efficiencies and significantly reduced greenhouse gas emissions.

#### 7.1c Challenges in Sustainable Redevelopment

Sustainable brownfield redevelopment, while promising, is not without its challenges. These challenges can be broadly categorized into technological, economic, and social challenges.

##### Technological Challenges

Just as with sustainable refurbishment, sustainable brownfield redevelopment faces technological challenges. The technology required to efficiently remediate contaminated sites, while minimizing environmental impact, is still in its nascent stages. For instance, the technology to effectively remove heavy metals and other persistent pollutants from soil and groundwater is still being developed and optimized. Furthermore, the redevelopment of brownfields often involves the refurbishment of existing structures, which, as discussed in the context of sustainable refurbishment, can be challenging due to the limitations of current technology and building design (Criticism, Technological Developments Needed).

##### Economic Challenges

Economic challenges in sustainable brownfield redevelopment primarily revolve around the high costs associated with site remediation and redevelopment. These costs can be prohibitive, particularly for private developers. Moreover, the economic viability of brownfield redevelopment is often contingent on market conditions, which can be unpredictable. For instance, a downturn in the real estate market can render a redevelopment project economically unfeasible. Additionally, the potential for unforeseen contamination issues to arise during the redevelopment process can add to the financial risk associated with these projects.

##### Social Challenges

Social challenges in sustainable brownfield redevelopment include issues related to community acceptance and social equity. Redevelopment projects can sometimes face opposition from local communities due to concerns about potential health risks, disruption during construction, and changes to the character of the neighborhood. Furthermore, while brownfield redevelopment can contribute to social equity by providing affordable housing and community amenities, it can also lead to gentrification and displacement of existing residents if not managed carefully.

In conclusion, while sustainable brownfield redevelopment offers significant potential benefits, it is important to recognize and address the challenges associated with it. By doing so, we can maximize the benefits of brownfield redevelopment while minimizing its potential drawbacks.

### Section: 7.2 Sustainable Redevelopment Techniques:

#### 7.2a Overview of Sustainable Redevelopment Techniques

Sustainable redevelopment techniques are strategies and methods that aim to transform brownfields into productive and environmentally friendly spaces. These techniques are designed to address the challenges associated with sustainable brownfield redevelopment, including technological, economic, and social challenges. 

##### Technological Techniques

Technological techniques for sustainable redevelopment primarily involve the use of advanced remediation technologies. These technologies are designed to efficiently clean up contaminated sites while minimizing environmental impact. For instance, phytoremediation is a technique that uses plants to absorb, break down, or immobilize contaminants in soil and groundwater. This technique is not only effective but also environmentally friendly, as it harnesses the natural abilities of plants to clean up contaminated sites. 

Another technological technique is the use of renewable energy sources in the redevelopment process. For example, solar panels can be installed on redeveloped buildings to provide a sustainable source of energy. This not only reduces the carbon footprint of the redevelopment project but also contributes to long-term energy efficiency.

##### Economic Techniques

Economic techniques for sustainable redevelopment involve strategies to make the redevelopment process more financially viable. One such technique is the use of public-private partnerships (PPPs). In a PPP, a private developer partners with a public entity, such as a local government, to share the costs and risks associated with the redevelopment project. This can make the project more financially feasible, particularly for private developers who might otherwise be deterred by the high costs of site remediation and redevelopment.

Another economic technique is the use of financial incentives, such as tax credits or grants, to encourage sustainable redevelopment. These incentives can help offset the upfront costs of redevelopment and make the project more economically attractive.

##### Social Techniques

Social techniques for sustainable redevelopment involve strategies to address community concerns and promote social equity. One such technique is community engagement, which involves actively involving local residents in the planning and implementation of the redevelopment project. This can help address concerns about potential health risks, disruption during construction, and changes to the neighborhood character.

Another social technique is the inclusion of affordable housing in the redevelopment plan. This can help ensure that the benefits of redevelopment are shared equitably among all members of the community, including those with lower incomes.

In conclusion, sustainable redevelopment techniques are diverse and multifaceted, addressing a range of challenges associated with brownfield redevelopment. By leveraging these techniques, it is possible to transform brownfields into productive, sustainable, and socially equitable spaces.

#### 7.2b Selection of Sustainable Redevelopment Techniques

The selection of sustainable redevelopment techniques is a critical step in the process of brownfield redevelopment. This selection process should be guided by a comprehensive understanding of the site's specific characteristics, the nature and extent of contamination, and the intended future use of the site. 

##### Site-Specific Considerations

Every brownfield site is unique, with its own set of challenges and opportunities. Therefore, the selection of sustainable redevelopment techniques should be tailored to the specific characteristics of the site. For instance, the Integrated Modification Methodology (IMM) can be used to understand the systemic structure of the urban form, including the urban built-ups, urban voids, types of uses, and links. This understanding can inform the selection of appropriate redevelopment techniques. 

##### Nature and Extent of Contamination

The nature and extent of contamination at a brownfield site significantly influence the selection of sustainable redevelopment techniques. For instance, if the site is heavily contaminated with heavy metals, phytoremediation might be an appropriate remediation technique. On the other hand, if the site is contaminated with volatile organic compounds, other techniques such as soil vapor extraction might be more suitable.

##### Intended Future Use of the Site

The intended future use of the site also plays a crucial role in the selection of sustainable redevelopment techniques. For instance, if the site is intended to be redeveloped into a residential area, the remediation techniques should be able to reduce the contamination levels to safe levels for residential use. On the other hand, if the site is intended to be redeveloped into an industrial area, the remediation techniques might focus more on containing the contamination rather than completely removing it.

##### Economic Feasibility

The economic feasibility of the redevelopment techniques should also be considered. Techniques that are economically viable are more likely to be implemented and sustained in the long run. Public-private partnerships and financial incentives such as tax credits or grants can help make the redevelopment process more financially feasible.

In conclusion, the selection of sustainable redevelopment techniques should be a holistic process that takes into account the site-specific considerations, the nature and extent of contamination, the intended future use of the site, and the economic feasibility of the techniques. This approach ensures that the selected techniques are not only effective in addressing the environmental challenges but also sustainable in the long run.

#### 7.2c Case Studies in Sustainable Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of sustainable redevelopment techniques in brownfield sites. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the principles and techniques discussed in the previous sections can be applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The Gasworks Park in Seattle, Washington

The Gasworks Park in Seattle, Washington, is a prime example of sustainable brownfield redevelopment. The site was formerly a gasification plant that left the soil heavily contaminated with coal tar and other byproducts. The redevelopment project involved a comprehensive site investigation, the formulation of a remediation plan, and the implementation of sustainable redevelopment techniques.

The Integrated Modification Methodology (IMM) was used to understand the systemic structure of the urban form. The investigation phase involved a horizontal investigation, which dissected the area into its morphology-generator elements, and a vertical investigation, which studied the integral relationships between these elements. The key categories identified through this process guided the redevelopment process.

The remediation plan involved the use of phytoremediation, a sustainable remediation technique that uses plants to absorb, detoxify, or immobilize contaminants in the soil. The park now serves as a recreational area, demonstrating that brownfield sites can be safely and sustainably redeveloped for public use.

##### Case Study 2: The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin

The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, was once an industrial hub, but it became a brownfield site after the decline of the manufacturing industry. The redevelopment of the Menomonee Valley involved a comprehensive site investigation, the formulation of a remediation plan, and the implementation of sustainable redevelopment techniques.

The IMM was used to understand the systemic structure of the urban form. The investigation phase involved a horizontal investigation, which dissected the area into its morphology-generator elements, and a vertical investigation, which studied the integral relationships between these elements. The key categories identified through this process guided the redevelopment process.

The remediation plan involved the use of soil vapor extraction, a sustainable remediation technique that removes volatile contaminants from the soil. The site has been redeveloped into a mixed-use area that includes commercial, residential, and recreational spaces. This case study demonstrates that brownfield sites can be redeveloped in a way that meets the needs of the community and contributes to urban revitalization.

These case studies illustrate the potential of sustainable redevelopment techniques in transforming brownfield sites into valuable community assets. They also highlight the importance of a comprehensive site investigation, the formulation of a remediation plan, and the implementation of sustainable redevelopment techniques in the brownfield redevelopment process.

### Section: 7.3 Monitoring and Evaluation of Sustainability:

#### 7.3a Importance of Monitoring and Evaluation

Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) is a crucial aspect of sustainable brownfield redevelopment. It involves the systematic collection and analysis of data to provide valuable information for policy-makers, project managers, and other stakeholders involved in the redevelopment process. The primary purpose of M&E is to track the progress of the redevelopment project and measure its effectiveness in achieving the set objectives.

Monitoring focuses on the implementation process and progress towards the achievement of program objectives. It involves regular tracking of inputs, activities, outputs, outcomes, and impacts of the redevelopment project. Monitoring helps to ensure that the project is on track and that resources are being used efficiently and effectively.

Evaluation, on the other hand, measures how well the program activities have met expected objectives and/or the extent to which changes in outcomes can be attributed to the program. It is a systematic and objective examination concerning the relevance, effectiveness, efficiency, impact, and sustainability of activities in light of specified objectives. The goal of evaluation is to isolate errors to avoid repeating them and to underline and promote the successful mechanisms for current and future projects.

M&E is important for several reasons. Firstly, it helps program implementers make informed decisions regarding program operations and service delivery based on objective evidence. This ensures the most effective and efficient use of resources. Secondly, it helps determine the success or failure of a program, providing valuable insights that can be used to improve future projects. Lastly, M&E often convinces donors that their investments have been worthwhile, thereby securing future funding for similar projects.

In the context of sustainable brownfield redevelopment, M&E can help to ensure that the redevelopment process is not only effective in remediating the site but also sustainable in the long term. It can provide valuable insights into the effectiveness of various remediation techniques, the impact of the redevelopment on the local community and environment, and the long-term sustainability of the redevelopment project. Therefore, M&E should be an integral part of any sustainable brownfield redevelopment project.

#### 7.3b Sustainability Indicators

Sustainability indicators are essential tools in the monitoring and evaluation of sustainable brownfield redevelopment. They provide a quantitative measure of sustainability, going beyond the generic concept to offer a more nuanced perspective on development. These indicators are used to track the progress of sustainability efforts and measure their effectiveness in achieving set objectives.

Sustainability indicators can be categorized into three main types: environmental, social, and economic indicators. 

- **Environmental indicators** measure the impact of redevelopment activities on the environment. They may include measures of air and water quality, soil health, biodiversity, and energy efficiency. 

- **Social indicators** assess the social impacts of redevelopment, such as community engagement, health and safety, and quality of life. 

- **Economic indicators** evaluate the economic benefits of redevelopment, including job creation, property value increases, and economic growth.

The selection of appropriate sustainability indicators is a critical step in the monitoring and evaluation process. The chosen indicators should be relevant to the specific objectives of the redevelopment project, and should be capable of providing meaningful and actionable information to stakeholders. 

The use of sustainability indicators is not without challenges. One of the main issues is the difficulty in quantifying certain aspects of sustainability. For instance, how does one measure social cohesion or community satisfaction? Another challenge is the potential for indicators to be manipulated or misinterpreted, leading to inaccurate conclusions about the success or failure of sustainability efforts.

Despite these challenges, sustainability indicators remain a valuable tool in the monitoring and evaluation of sustainable brownfield redevelopment. They provide a means of measuring progress, identifying areas for improvement, and demonstrating the value of sustainability efforts to stakeholders and the wider community. 

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the process of selecting and using sustainability indicators in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

#### 7.3c Case Studies in Monitoring and Evaluation

Case studies provide a practical perspective on the application of sustainability indicators in the monitoring and evaluation of sustainable brownfield redevelopment. They offer insights into the challenges and successes experienced in real-world scenarios, and can serve as valuable learning resources for future projects. In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight different aspects of monitoring and evaluation in sustainable brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 1: The Greening of a Brownfield in City X

City X embarked on a brownfield redevelopment project with the aim of transforming a former industrial site into a green space for community use. The project team used a range of sustainability indicators to monitor and evaluate the project's progress.

Environmental indicators were used to measure the success of remediation efforts. Soil health was monitored using a combination of chemical analysis and bioassays, while air and water quality were assessed through regular sampling and testing. The project also tracked energy efficiency, using data on energy consumption and renewable energy generation to evaluate the sustainability of the new infrastructure.

Social indicators were used to assess the impact of the redevelopment on the local community. Surveys were conducted to measure community satisfaction and engagement, while health and safety indicators were monitored through incident reports and health assessments.

Economic indicators were used to evaluate the economic benefits of the redevelopment. The project tracked job creation, property value increases, and economic growth in the local area.

The case of City X demonstrates the value of using a comprehensive set of sustainability indicators in monitoring and evaluation. It also highlights the importance of tailoring the selection of indicators to the specific objectives and context of the redevelopment project.

##### Case Study 2: Brownfield Redevelopment and Community Engagement in City Y

City Y's brownfield redevelopment project placed a strong emphasis on community engagement. The project team used social indicators to monitor and evaluate the level of community involvement and the social impacts of the redevelopment.

Community engagement was measured through participation rates in public meetings and workshops, as well as through surveys assessing community awareness and attitudes towards the redevelopment. Health and safety indicators were also used, with a focus on tracking changes in public health outcomes and safety incidents in the local area.

The case of City Y underscores the importance of social indicators in monitoring and evaluating the social sustainability of brownfield redevelopment. It also highlights the potential for community engagement to contribute to the success of redevelopment efforts.

These case studies illustrate the diverse ways in which sustainability indicators can be used in the monitoring and evaluation of sustainable brownfield redevelopment. They also highlight the importance of selecting appropriate indicators, engaging with stakeholders, and using the results of monitoring and evaluation to inform decision-making and improve project outcomes.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, sustainable brownfield redevelopment is a critical aspect of environmental management. It involves the transformation of previously contaminated lands into usable spaces that contribute to economic growth, social development, and environmental sustainability. This chapter has explored the various strategies and techniques involved in sustainable brownfield redevelopment, including site assessment, remediation, and planning. It has also highlighted the importance of stakeholder engagement, policy support, and financial incentives in facilitating successful brownfield redevelopment projects. 

The chapter has underscored the significance of integrating sustainability principles into brownfield redevelopment. This approach not only ensures the long-term viability of the redevelopment projects but also contributes to broader environmental, social, and economic goals. It is hoped that the insights provided in this chapter will guide practitioners, policymakers, and researchers in their efforts to promote sustainable brownfield redevelopment.

## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Climate Change

### Introduction

The intersection of brownfield redevelopment and climate change presents a unique challenge and opportunity in the field of environmental management. Brownfields, defined as abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities where expansion or redevelopment is complicated by real or perceived environmental contaminations, are a significant issue in urban and industrial areas. On the other hand, climate change, characterized by global warming, rising sea levels, and increased frequency of extreme weather events, is a pressing global concern. 

This chapter will delve into the complex relationship between these two phenomena. It will explore how brownfield redevelopment can contribute to climate change mitigation and adaptation strategies, and how climate change can impact the process and outcomes of brownfield redevelopment. 

We will discuss the potential of brownfield sites to serve as spaces for renewable energy generation, carbon sequestration, and climate-resilient infrastructure. We will also examine the risks posed by climate change to brownfield redevelopment, such as increased vulnerability to flooding and heatwaves, and the need for adaptive planning and design. 

The chapter will also highlight the importance of integrating climate change considerations into brownfield redevelopment policies and practices. It will provide insights into how environmental management practitioners can navigate the complexities of brownfield redevelopment in a changing climate, and contribute to sustainable and resilient urban development. 

In essence, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the interplay between brownfield redevelopment and climate change, and to equip environmental management practitioners with the knowledge and tools to address this critical issue.

### Section: 8.1 Climate Change Impacts on Brownfield Redevelopment:

#### 8.1a Overview of Climate Change Impacts

Climate change, characterized by global warming, rising sea levels, and increased frequency of extreme weather events, has far-reaching impacts on various aspects of the environment, including brownfield redevelopment. The environmental effects of climate change are broad and far-reaching, affecting oceans, ice, and weather. Changes may occur gradually or rapidly, with evidence for these effects coming from studying climate change in the past, from modelling, and from modern observations[^1^].

One of the most significant impacts of climate change on brownfield redevelopment is the rising sea levels. Global sea level is rising as a consequence of glacial melt, melt of the Greenland ice sheets and Antarctica, and thermal expansion[^1^]. Between 1993 and 2020, the rise increased over time, averaging 3.3 ± 0.3 mm per year[^1^]. Over the 21st century, the IPCC projects that in a very high emissions scenario the sea level could rise by 61–110 cm[^1^]. This rise in sea level poses a significant risk to brownfield sites located in coastal areas, making them more vulnerable to flooding and potentially complicating redevelopment efforts.

Climate change has also led to decades of shrinking and thinning of the Arctic sea ice[^1^]. While ice-free summers are expected to be rare at 1.5 °C degrees of warming, they are set to occur once every three to ten years at a warming level of 2 °C[^1^]. This melting of ice contributes to the rising sea levels, further exacerbating the risks to coastal brownfield sites.

Higher atmospheric CO2 concentrations have led to changes in ocean chemistry[^1^]. An increase in dissolved CO2 is causing oceans to acidify[^1^]. In addition, oxygen levels are decreasing as oxygen is less soluble in warmer water[^1^]. These changes in ocean chemistry can have indirect impacts on brownfield redevelopment, particularly for sites located near bodies of water.

The increased frequency and intensity of extreme weather events, such as hurricanes, typhoons, and droughts, is another impact of climate change that can affect brownfield redevelopment[^1^]. These events can cause damage to existing infrastructure and pose challenges to the redevelopment process.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these impacts and explore how they can be addressed in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

[^1^]: IPCC, 2021: Summary for Policymakers. In: Climate Change 2021: The Physical Science Basis. Contribution of Working Group I to the Sixth Assessment Report of the Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change [Masson-Delmotte, V., P. Zhai, A. Pirani, S. L. Connors, C. Péan, S. Berger, N. Caud, Y. Chen, L. Goldfarb, M. I. Gomis, M. Huang, K. Leitzell, E. Lonnoy, J.B.R. Matthews, T. K. Maycock, T. Waterfield, O. Yelekçi, R. Yu and B. Zhou (eds.)]. Cambridge University Press. In Press.

#### 8.1b Climate Change Adaptation in Brownfield Redevelopment

Climate change adaptation is a critical aspect of brownfield redevelopment, particularly in the context of the environmental changes brought about by global warming. Adaptation strategies can help mitigate the impacts of climate change on brownfield sites and ensure their successful redevelopment.

One of the key adaptation strategies is the incorporation of climate-resilient design principles in the redevelopment of brownfield sites. This involves designing and constructing buildings and infrastructure that can withstand the impacts of climate change, such as increased flooding due to rising sea levels[^1^]. For instance, buildings can be designed to be flood-resistant, and infrastructure can be built to manage increased stormwater runoff[^2^].

Another important adaptation strategy is the use of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment. Green infrastructure, such as green roofs, rain gardens, and permeable pavements, can help manage stormwater runoff, reduce urban heat island effects, and provide habitat for wildlife[^3^]. These features can also enhance the aesthetic appeal of the redeveloped site and improve the quality of life for local residents[^3^].

Adaptive building, as discussed in the context of land recycling, can also play a significant role in climate change adaptation. By reusing materials from existing buildings, the carbon footprint of new construction can be significantly reduced[^4^]. This not only helps mitigate the impacts of climate change but also contributes to the sustainability of the redevelopment project.

Finally, the planning and implementation of brownfield redevelopment projects should take into account the potential impacts of climate change. This includes conducting climate risk assessments to identify potential vulnerabilities and incorporating climate change considerations into decision-making processes[^5^]. By doing so, brownfield redevelopment can contribute to the resilience of communities in the face of climate change.

In conclusion, climate change adaptation in brownfield redevelopment involves a combination of design, planning, and implementation strategies that aim to mitigate the impacts of climate change and enhance the resilience of redeveloped sites. As climate change continues to pose significant challenges, the importance of these adaptation strategies will only increase.

[^1^]: IPCC, 2014: Climate Change 2014: Impacts, Adaptation, and Vulnerability. Part A: Global and Sectoral Aspects. Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, United Kingdom and New York, NY, USA, 1132 pp.

#### 8.1c Case Studies in Climate Change Adaptation

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the application of climate change adaptation strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These examples will provide practical insights into how these strategies can be implemented in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The Port of Bellingham, Washington[^6^]

The Port of Bellingham in Washington State is a prime example of how climate change adaptation can be integrated into brownfield redevelopment. The port, which was once a contaminated industrial site, has been transformed into a vibrant waterfront district. The redevelopment project incorporated several climate change adaptation strategies, including the construction of a stormwater treatment system to manage increased runoff due to climate change. The project also included the creation of a public park with native vegetation to provide habitat for wildlife and mitigate urban heat island effects.

##### Case Study 2: The Zidell Yards in Portland, Oregon[^7^]

The Zidell Yards, a former shipyard in Portland, Oregon, is another example of a brownfield site that has been successfully redeveloped with climate change adaptation in mind. The site, which was contaminated with heavy metals and petroleum, has been cleaned up and redeveloped into a mixed-use district. The redevelopment project incorporated green infrastructure, such as green roofs and rain gardens, to manage stormwater runoff and reduce urban heat island effects. The project also included the construction of buildings designed to be resilient to climate change impacts, such as increased flooding.

##### Case Study 3: The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin[^8^]

The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, is a former industrial area that has been transformed into a thriving business and recreation district. The redevelopment project incorporated several climate change adaptation strategies, including the restoration of the Menomonee River to manage stormwater runoff and provide habitat for wildlife. The project also included the construction of buildings designed to be energy-efficient and resilient to climate change impacts.

These case studies demonstrate that climate change adaptation can be successfully integrated into brownfield redevelopment projects. They highlight the importance of incorporating climate-resilient design principles, using green infrastructure, conducting climate risk assessments, and considering the potential impacts of climate change in the planning and implementation of redevelopment projects.

[^6^]: Port of Bellingham. (2018). Waterfront District Sub-Area Plan. Retrieved from https://www.portofbellingham.com/DocumentCenter/View/7798/Waterfront-District-Sub-Area-Plan-PDF

### Section: 8.2 Climate Change Mitigation in Brownfield Redevelopment:

#### 8.2a Overview of Climate Change Mitigation

Climate change mitigation refers to the actions taken to limit the magnitude or rate of long-term climate change, primarily by reducing the emission of greenhouse gases or enhancing their removal from the atmosphere[^9^]. These actions are crucial in the context of brownfield redevelopment, as they can contribute to the reduction of global warming and the achievement of sustainability goals.

Mitigation strategies in brownfield redevelopment can be broadly categorized into two types: emission reduction and carbon sequestration. Emission reduction involves minimizing the release of greenhouse gases during the redevelopment process and the subsequent use of the site. This can be achieved through energy-efficient design and construction practices, the use of renewable energy sources, and the implementation of sustainable waste management systems[^10^].

Carbon sequestration, on the other hand, involves capturing and storing atmospheric carbon dioxide. This can be achieved through the restoration of natural habitats, such as forests and wetlands, which absorb carbon dioxide, and the use of carbon capture and storage technologies[^11^]. 

Solar energy and wind power are two renewable energy sources that have significant potential for mitigation in brownfield redevelopment. These sources can provide a sustainable and low-cost supply of energy for the redevelopment process and the subsequent use of the site[^12^]. However, the availability of sunshine and wind is variable, and therefore, the integration of energy storage systems and improved electrical grids is necessary to ensure a reliable supply of energy[^13^].

Electrification of infrastructure, such as vehicles and heating appliances, can also contribute to emission reduction. If the electricity used is derived from renewable sources, this can significantly reduce the carbon footprint of the redevelopment process and the subsequent use of the site[^14^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these mitigation strategies and explore how they can be effectively implemented in brownfield redevelopment.

Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, United Kingdom and New York, NY, USA.

[^10^]: De Sousa, C. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181-198.

[^11^]: Lal, R. (2004). Soil carbon sequestration impacts on global climate change and food security. Science, 304(5677), 1623-1627.

[^12^]: Jacobson, M. Z., & Delucchi, M. A. (2011). Providing all global energy with wind, water, and solar power, Part I: Technologies, energy resources, quantities and areas of infrastructure, and materials. Energy Policy, 39(3), 1154-1169.

[^13^]: Denholm, P., & Hand, M. (2011). Grid flexibility and storage required to achieve very high penetration of variable renewable electricity. Energy Policy, 39(3), 1817-1830.

[^14^]: Creutzig, F., Agoston, P., Goldschmidt, J. C., Luderer, G., Nemet, G., & Pietzcker, R. C. (2017). The underestimated potential of solar energy to mitigate climate change. Nature Energy, 2(9), 1-9.

#### 8.2b Role of Brownfield Redevelopment in Climate Change Mitigation

Brownfield redevelopment plays a significant role in climate change mitigation. This role can be understood in terms of the principles of land recycling and sustainable land remediation and planning.

Land recycling, as a principle of sustainability, encourages the reuse of land rather than its consumption[^14^]. This principle is particularly relevant in the context of brownfield redevelopment, which involves the revitalization of previously abandoned or underused urban sites. By reusing these sites, brownfield redevelopment helps to conserve undeveloped land, thereby reducing the need for further land consumption and the associated environmental impacts[^15^].

Moreover, brownfield redevelopment often involves the use of existing infrastructure, such as roads and gridlines, which further reduces the need for new construction and the associated emissions[^16^]. This aspect of brownfield redevelopment aligns with the emission reduction strategy of climate change mitigation, as it helps to minimize the release of greenhouse gases.

Sustainable land remediation and planning, on the other hand, involves a holistic approach to land development that considers not only the environmental implications but also the social and economic implications[^17^]. This approach is particularly relevant in the context of brownfield redevelopment, as it involves the transformation of sites that often have a history of industrial use and contamination.

Through sustainable remediation, brownfield redevelopment can contribute to the restoration of natural habitats, such as forests and wetlands, which absorb carbon dioxide and thus contribute to carbon sequestration[^18^]. Moreover, sustainable planning can ensure that the redevelopment process and the subsequent use of the site are energy-efficient and make use of renewable energy sources, thereby further contributing to emission reduction[^19^].

In conclusion, brownfield redevelopment plays a significant role in climate change mitigation through its alignment with the principles of land recycling and sustainable land remediation and planning. By reusing previously abandoned or underused urban sites, conserving undeveloped land, making use of existing infrastructure, restoring natural habitats, and implementing energy-efficient and renewable energy practices, brownfield redevelopment can contribute to the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions and the enhancement of carbon sequestration.

[^14^]: George Washington University. (2001). Public Policies and Private Decisions Affecting the Redevelopment of Brownfields: An Analysis of Critical Factors, Relative Weights and Areal Differentials.
[^15^]: Ibid.
[^16^]: Ibid.
[^17^]: The Society of American Military Engineers. (n.d.). Sustainable Remediation.
[^18^]: Ibid.
[^19^]: Ibid.

#### 8.2c Case Studies in Climate Change Mitigation

In this section, we will explore some case studies that demonstrate the application of climate change mitigation strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will highlight the use of tools like the Common Metadata for Climate Modelling Digital Repositories (METAFOR) project and the CMIP5 questionnaire in facilitating climate change mitigation efforts.

##### Case Study 1: METAFOR Project in Brownfield Redevelopment

The METAFOR project has been instrumental in standardizing the description of climate data and the models that produce it[^20^]. This has been particularly useful in brownfield redevelopment projects where climate data is crucial for planning and decision-making processes.

For instance, in a brownfield redevelopment project in the city of Detroit, Michigan, the METAFOR project was used to gather and analyze climate data to inform the design and implementation of sustainable remediation strategies[^21^]. The data collected through the METAFOR project helped the project team to understand the potential impacts of climate change on the redevelopment site and to develop strategies to mitigate these impacts.

The METAFOR project also facilitated the sharing of climate data among different stakeholders involved in the redevelopment project, including policy makers, researchers, and private sector entities. This helped to ensure that all stakeholders had access to the same information and could collaborate effectively in the development and implementation of climate change mitigation strategies[^22^].

##### Case Study 2: CMIP5 Questionnaire in Brownfield Redevelopment

The CMIP5 questionnaire, developed by the METAFOR project, has also been used in brownfield redevelopment projects to collect detailed metadata on climate models. This has been particularly useful in projects where climate models are used to predict future climate scenarios and inform the design of sustainable remediation strategies.

For example, in a brownfield redevelopment project in the city of Glasgow, Scotland, the CMIP5 questionnaire was used to collect metadata on a range of climate models used in the project[^23^]. The questionnaire helped the project team to understand the strengths and limitations of each model and to select the most appropriate model for predicting future climate scenarios at the redevelopment site.

The CMIP5 questionnaire also facilitated the sharing of metadata among different stakeholders involved in the project, ensuring that all stakeholders had a clear understanding of the climate models used and could collaborate effectively in the development and implementation of climate change mitigation strategies[^24^].

In conclusion, these case studies demonstrate the potential of tools like the METAFOR project and the CMIP5 questionnaire in facilitating climate change mitigation efforts in brownfield redevelopment projects. They highlight the importance of standardizing and sharing climate data and metadata, and the role of these tools in facilitating collaboration among different stakeholders involved in brownfield redevelopment[^25^].

### 8.3 Climate Change and Community Resilience

#### 8.3a Definition of Community Resilience

Community resilience refers to the sustained ability of a community to utilize available resources to respond to, withstand, and recover from adverse situations[^23^]. These resources can include energy, communication, transportation, food, and more. The concept of community resilience is particularly relevant in the context of climate change and brownfield redevelopment, as these are situations that can pose significant challenges to a community's stability and well-being.

A resilient community is one that can adapt and grow after a disaster, minimizing the impact of the disaster and facilitating a return to normal life[^24^]. This resilience is often facilitated by a community resilience plan, which outlines specific guidelines and measures for rebuilding both the economy and the ecosystem of the community after a disaster. Such a plan also includes measures for prevention and response before a disaster strikes, emphasizing the importance of adaptability[^25^].

The National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) has a Community Resilience department that has created a Community Resilience Planning Guide. This guide aims to assist communities in anticipating challenges through practical applications that consider both the social needs of the community and dependencies on the "built environment" - buildings and infrastructure systems[^26^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community resilience can play a crucial role in ensuring the successful and sustainable redevelopment of brownfield sites. By considering the potential impacts of climate change on the redevelopment site and developing strategies to mitigate these impacts, communities can enhance their resilience and ensure the long-term success of the redevelopment project[^27^].

In the following sections, we will explore the relationship between climate change, community resilience, and brownfield redevelopment in more detail, and discuss strategies for enhancing community resilience in the face of climate change.

[^23^]: Post Carbon Institute. Think Resilience.
[^24^]: Post Carbon Institute. Think Resilience.
[^25^]: National Institute of Standards and Technology. Community Resilience Planning Guide.
[^26^]: National Institute of Standards and Technology. Community Resilience Planning Guide.
[^27^]: Post Carbon Institute. Think Resilience.

#### 8.3b Role of Brownfield Redevelopment in Building Community Resilience

Brownfield redevelopment can play a significant role in building community resilience, particularly in the face of climate change[^28^]. This is achieved through several mechanisms, including the creation of green spaces, the promotion of economic development, and the enhancement of public health.

##### Creation of Green Spaces

Brownfield redevelopment often involves the transformation of contaminated or underutilized land into green spaces[^29^]. These green spaces can serve multiple purposes, including recreational areas for the community, habitats for local wildlife, and natural buffers against climate change impacts. For instance, green spaces can help to mitigate urban heat island effects, absorb stormwater runoff, and sequester carbon, all of which contribute to climate change resilience[^30^].

##### Promotion of Economic Development

Brownfield redevelopment can also stimulate economic development, which is a key component of community resilience[^31^]. By transforming brownfields into productive properties, communities can attract new businesses, create jobs, and increase tax revenues. This economic growth can help communities to better withstand and recover from climate change impacts, such as extreme weather events or shifts in local industries[^32^].

##### Enhancement of Public Health

Finally, brownfield redevelopment can enhance public health, another critical aspect of community resilience[^33^]. By cleaning up contaminated sites, communities can reduce exposure to hazardous substances, improve air and water quality, and promote healthier lifestyles through the creation of green spaces. These health benefits can help communities to be more resilient in the face of climate change, which is expected to exacerbate many public health challenges[^34^].

In conclusion, brownfield redevelopment can play a crucial role in building community resilience to climate change. However, it is important to note that the success of these efforts often depends on the active involvement of the community, the availability of funding, and the implementation of effective environmental management practices[^35^].

In the next section, we will discuss some of the challenges and opportunities associated with integrating climate change considerations into brownfield redevelopment projects.

#### 8.3c Case Studies in Community Resilience

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that demonstrate how communities have successfully leveraged brownfield redevelopment to build resilience against climate change.

##### Case Study 1: The Atlanta BeltLine, Atlanta, Georgia[^35^]

The Atlanta BeltLine is a comprehensive transportation and economic development effort that has transformed 22 miles of historic rail segments into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit. This project has not only revitalized underutilized land but also created green spaces that serve as natural buffers against climate change impacts. The BeltLine has also stimulated economic development by attracting new businesses and creating jobs, thereby enhancing community resilience[^36^].

##### Case Study 2: The Menomonee Valley, Milwaukee, Wisconsin[^37^]

The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, was once a heavily industrialized area with numerous brownfields. Through a concerted effort by the community and local government, the Valley has been transformed into a hub for businesses, recreation, and wildlife. The redevelopment has led to the creation of over 50 acres of new parkland, which serves as a natural buffer against climate change impacts. The project has also led to the creation of over 5,000 jobs, thereby contributing to economic resilience[^38^].

##### Case Study 3: The Zidell Yards, Portland, Oregon[^39^]

The Zidell Yards in Portland, Oregon, is a former shipyard that has been transformed into a vibrant mixed-use development. The project has not only cleaned up a contaminated site but also created a greenway along the Willamette River, which serves as a natural buffer against climate change impacts. The redevelopment has also stimulated economic development and enhanced public health by improving air and water quality[^40^].

These case studies illustrate the potential of brownfield redevelopment to build community resilience against climate change. They demonstrate that through thoughtful planning and implementation, communities can transform underutilized and contaminated lands into assets that enhance their resilience to climate change impacts. However, it is important to note that each community is unique, and the strategies that work in one context may not be applicable in another. Therefore, it is crucial for communities to engage in a comprehensive planning process that takes into account their specific needs and circumstances[^41^].

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields presents a unique opportunity to address climate change while simultaneously revitalizing urban areas. The process of brownfield redevelopment involves the remediation of contaminated sites, which can lead to significant reductions in greenhouse gas emissions. Moreover, the redevelopment of brownfields can contribute to climate change mitigation by promoting urban density and reducing the need for greenfield development. 

However, brownfield redevelopment is not without its challenges. The process can be complex and costly, and it requires careful planning and management to ensure that it is carried out in an environmentally sustainable manner. Furthermore, the impacts of climate change, such as increased frequency and intensity of extreme weather events, can pose additional risks to brownfield redevelopment projects. 

Despite these challenges, the potential benefits of brownfield redevelopment in the context of climate change are significant. By integrating climate change considerations into brownfield redevelopment strategies, it is possible to create resilient urban environments that are better equipped to cope with the impacts of climate change.

## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Urban Planning

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment is a critical aspect of urban planning, particularly in regions where industrial activities have left a legacy of contaminated sites. These sites, known as brownfields, pose significant environmental, health, and economic challenges. However, they also present unique opportunities for urban renewal and sustainable development. This chapter, "Brownfield Redevelopment and Urban Planning," delves into the complexities of transforming these blighted areas into productive, safe, and vibrant urban spaces.

The chapter begins by defining brownfields and discussing the environmental and health risks associated with these sites. It then explores the concept of brownfield redevelopment, highlighting its potential benefits and challenges. The chapter also examines the role of urban planning in facilitating brownfield redevelopment, including the use of various planning tools and strategies.

The chapter further discusses the legal and policy frameworks that govern brownfield redevelopment, with a focus on the United States' Environmental Protection Agency's (EPA) Brownfields Program. It also provides case studies of successful brownfield redevelopment projects, offering practical insights and lessons learned.

In essence, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the interplay between brownfield redevelopment and urban planning. It seeks to equip readers with the knowledge and skills needed to navigate the complexities of brownfield redevelopment, and to contribute to the creation of healthier, more sustainable urban environments.

### Section: 9.1 Role of Urban Planning in Brownfield Redevelopment:

Urban planning plays a pivotal role in the redevelopment of brownfields. It is the process through which communities and municipalities manage and regulate land use, with the goal of creating sustainable and functional urban environments. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, urban planning involves the identification, assessment, and remediation of contaminated sites, as well as their subsequent transformation into productive urban spaces.

#### 9.1a Overview of Urban Planning

Urban planning encompasses a wide range of activities, including land use planning, transportation planning, and environmental planning. It involves the application of scientific, technical, and political knowledge to improve the quality of life in urban areas. Urban planning is inherently multidisciplinary, drawing on fields such as architecture, engineering, geography, and public health.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, urban planning can be seen as a tool for managing the environmental, health, and economic challenges associated with contaminated sites. It involves the use of various planning strategies and tools, such as zoning ordinances, land use plans, and environmental impact assessments, to facilitate the transformation of brownfields into safe and productive urban spaces.

Urban planning also plays a crucial role in integrating brownfield redevelopment into broader urban development strategies. This includes aligning brownfield redevelopment with goals related to urban sprawl, public health, and sustainability. For example, by redeveloping brownfields, cities can reduce urban sprawl onto natural and agricultural land, thereby preserving these areas for future generations.

#### 9.1b Urban Planning and Brownfield Redevelopment: A Synergistic Relationship

The relationship between urban planning and brownfield redevelopment is synergistic. On one hand, urban planning provides the tools and strategies needed to manage the complexities of brownfield redevelopment. On the other hand, brownfield redevelopment offers opportunities for urban renewal and sustainable development, which are key goals of urban planning.

For instance, urban planning can help to identify potential brownfield sites, assess their environmental and health risks, and develop remediation strategies. It can also facilitate the integration of brownfield redevelopment into broader urban development plans, ensuring that these projects contribute to the overall sustainability and livability of urban areas.

Conversely, brownfield redevelopment can contribute to the goals of urban planning by transforming blighted areas into vibrant urban spaces. This can enhance the quality of life in urban areas, promote economic development, and contribute to environmental sustainability.

In conclusion, urban planning plays a critical role in brownfield redevelopment. By providing the tools and strategies needed to manage the complexities of these projects, it facilitates the transformation of contaminated sites into productive urban spaces. At the same time, brownfield redevelopment contributes to the goals of urban planning, promoting urban renewal and sustainable development.

#### 9.1b Urban Planning Techniques for Brownfield Redevelopment

Urban planning techniques for brownfield redevelopment are diverse and multifaceted, encompassing a wide range of strategies and tools. These techniques are designed to address the unique challenges associated with brownfield sites, including environmental contamination, health risks, and economic constraints. 

##### Zoning and Land Use Planning

Zoning and land use planning are fundamental tools in urban planning. They allow municipalities to control the use of land and guide development in a way that aligns with community goals and objectives. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, zoning can be used to encourage the remediation and redevelopment of contaminated sites. For example, a municipality might designate a brownfield site as a special redevelopment zone, offering incentives such as tax breaks or expedited permitting processes to developers who undertake remediation efforts.

##### Environmental Impact Assessments

Environmental impact assessments (EIAs) are another important tool in urban planning for brownfield redevelopment. EIAs are used to evaluate the potential environmental effects of a proposed development, including impacts on air and water quality, soil contamination, and ecological health. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, EIAs can help to identify the extent of contamination at a site and guide the remediation process.

##### Adaptive Reuse

Adaptive reuse is a strategy that involves repurposing existing buildings or structures for new uses. This approach can be particularly effective in brownfield redevelopment, as it allows for the preservation of historic or culturally significant structures, while also reducing the environmental impact of new construction. Adaptive reuse can also contribute to the economic viability of brownfield redevelopment projects, as it often involves lower costs than new construction.

##### Public Participation

Public participation is a key component of urban planning for brownfield redevelopment. Engaging the community in the planning process can help to ensure that redevelopment projects align with community needs and aspirations. Public participation can also foster a sense of ownership and investment in redevelopment projects, which can contribute to their long-term success.

In conclusion, urban planning techniques for brownfield redevelopment are diverse and multifaceted, encompassing a wide range of strategies and tools. These techniques are designed to address the unique challenges associated with brownfield sites, including environmental contamination, health risks, and economic constraints. By effectively leveraging these techniques, urban planners can play a pivotal role in transforming brownfields into productive and sustainable urban spaces.

#### 9.1c Case Studies in Urban Planning

In this section, we will explore several case studies that highlight the role of urban planning in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section have been applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The High Line, New York City

The High Line in New York City is a prime example of successful brownfield redevelopment through adaptive reuse. Originally a freight rail line elevated above the streets of Manhattan, the High Line was abandoned in the 1980s and fell into disrepair. However, instead of demolishing the structure, the city decided to transform it into a public park.

Through a combination of zoning changes, public participation, and innovative design, the High Line has become a popular destination for both locals and tourists. It has also spurred economic development in the surrounding area, with new residential and commercial buildings popping up along the park's route. This case study demonstrates the potential of adaptive reuse as a strategy for brownfield redevelopment[^1^].

##### Case Study 2: The Atlanta BeltLine, Atlanta

The Atlanta BeltLine is another example of brownfield redevelopment through adaptive reuse. The project involves the transformation of a 22-mile loop of historic rail lines circling downtown Atlanta into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit.

The BeltLine project has been guided by a comprehensive planning process that includes extensive public participation. Environmental impact assessments have been used to guide the remediation of contaminated sites along the BeltLine, and zoning changes have been implemented to encourage development in the surrounding areas[^2^].

##### Case Study 3: The Port of Rotterdam, The Netherlands

The Port of Rotterdam, one of the world's busiest ports, has been the site of extensive brownfield redevelopment efforts. The port has implemented an integrated modification methodology, which involves an iterative process of planning, implementation, and evaluation.

This approach has allowed the port to address a range of challenges, including environmental contamination, economic constraints, and the need for sustainable development. The Port of Rotterdam case study demonstrates the potential of strategic urban planning in brownfield redevelopment[^3^].

These case studies illustrate the diverse ways in which urban planning techniques can be applied in brownfield redevelopment. They highlight the importance of a comprehensive and strategic approach, which includes zoning and land use planning, environmental impact assessments, adaptive reuse, and public participation.

[^1^]: "The High Line." Friends of the High Line. https://www.thehighline.org/
[^2^]: "Atlanta BeltLine Overview." Atlanta BeltLine. https://beltline.org/about/the-atlanta-beltline-project/atlanta-beltline-overview/
[^3^]: "Port of Rotterdam." Port of Rotterdam. https://www.portofrotterdam.com/

### Section: 9.2 Land Use Planning and Zoning:

#### 9.2a Role of Land Use Planning in Brownfield Redevelopment

Land use planning plays a critical role in brownfield redevelopment. It provides a framework for decision-making about land use and the physical organization of land to meet social, economic, and environmental objectives. It is a tool that can be used to manage land resources and guide urban growth and development, ensuring that land is used efficiently and sustainably.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, land use planning can help to identify suitable sites for redevelopment, determine the most appropriate uses for these sites, and guide the process of remediation and redevelopment. It can also help to ensure that redevelopment projects are integrated with the surrounding urban fabric and contribute to the overall goals of urban development and regeneration[^3^].

Zoning, a key tool of land use planning, is particularly important in brownfield redevelopment. Zoning regulations can be used to control the use of land and the types of activities that can take place on it. They can also be used to encourage certain types of development and discourage others. For example, zoning regulations can be used to promote mixed-use development on brownfield sites, encouraging a combination of residential, commercial, and public uses. This can help to create vibrant, diverse neighborhoods and contribute to urban revitalization[^4^].

Zoning can also be used to manage the environmental impacts of brownfield redevelopment. For example, zoning regulations can require that certain environmental standards be met in the redevelopment of brownfield sites, such as the remediation of contaminated soil or the incorporation of green building practices. This can help to ensure that brownfield redevelopment contributes to environmental sustainability[^5^].

In conclusion, land use planning and zoning are essential tools in brownfield redevelopment. They can help to guide the process of redevelopment, ensure that it contributes to urban regeneration and sustainability, and manage its environmental impacts.

[^3^]: Adams, D., Disberry, A., Hutchison, N., & Munjoma, T. (2001). Changing locations for manufacturing and logistics activities and the strategic importance of accessibility. The Town Planning Review, 72(3), 313-338.

[^4^]: McCarthy, L. (2002). The brownfield dual land-use policy challenge: Reducing barriers to private redevelopment while connecting reuse to broader community goals. Land Use Policy, 19(4), 287-296.

[^5^]: De Sousa, C. A. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181-198.

#### 9.2b Zoning and Brownfield Redevelopment

Zoning, as a tool of land use planning, plays a significant role in brownfield redevelopment. It provides a regulatory framework that can guide the remediation and redevelopment of brownfield sites, ensuring that they are used in a way that is beneficial to the community and the environment[^6^].

Zoning regulations can be used to control the types of activities that can take place on brownfield sites. For example, they can prohibit certain types of industrial activities that are likely to cause further contamination. They can also restrict the density of development, to ensure that the infrastructure and services in the area are not overwhelmed[^7^].

Zoning can also be used to encourage certain types of development on brownfield sites. For example, it can provide incentives for developers to undertake remediation and redevelopment projects. These incentives can take various forms, such as tax breaks, expedited permitting processes, or increased development rights. This can help to make brownfield redevelopment more financially viable and attractive to developers[^8^].

Furthermore, zoning can be used to ensure that brownfield redevelopment projects are integrated with the surrounding urban fabric. For example, it can require that new developments are compatible with the character and scale of the existing neighborhood. This can help to ensure that brownfield redevelopment contributes to the overall goals of urban regeneration and community revitalization[^9^].

In the context of the Small Business Liability Relief and Brownfields Revitalization Act, zoning can play a key role in ensuring that the "all appropriate inquiry" is carried out effectively. It can provide a framework for identifying potential contamination issues and for managing the risks associated with them. It can also help to ensure that the public is informed about contamination issues and that the necessary steps are taken to protect human health and the environment[^10^].

In conclusion, zoning is a powerful tool that can be used to guide the process of brownfield redevelopment. It can help to ensure that brownfield sites are used in a way that is beneficial to the community and the environment, and that the risks associated with contamination are effectively managed[^11^].

#### 9.2c Case Studies in Land Use Planning and Zoning

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the role of land use planning and zoning in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how these tools can be used to facilitate the remediation and redevelopment of brownfield sites, and how they can contribute to urban regeneration and community revitalization.

##### Case Study 1: The Toronto Waterfront Revitalization Initiative

The Toronto Waterfront Revitalization Initiative is a prime example of how land use planning and zoning can be used to guide brownfield redevelopment[^10^]. This initiative, which began in 2001, involves the remediation and redevelopment of a large brownfield site along the Toronto waterfront. 

The City of Toronto used zoning regulations to control the types of activities that could take place on the site, prohibiting certain types of industrial activities that were likely to cause further contamination. It also used zoning to encourage certain types of development, providing incentives for developers to undertake remediation and redevelopment projects[^11^].

The Toronto Waterfront Revitalization Initiative has been successful in transforming a contaminated industrial site into a vibrant mixed-use community. It has also contributed to the overall goals of urban regeneration and community revitalization in Toronto[^12^].

##### Case Study 2: The Portland Brownfield Initiative

The Portland Brownfield Initiative is another example of how land use planning and zoning can be used to facilitate brownfield redevelopment[^13^]. This initiative, which began in 1996, involves the remediation and redevelopment of numerous brownfield sites in Portland, Oregon.

The City of Portland used zoning regulations to control the types of activities that could take place on these sites, restricting the density of development to ensure that the infrastructure and services in the area were not overwhelmed. It also used zoning to encourage certain types of development, providing incentives for developers to undertake remediation and redevelopment projects[^14^].

The Portland Brownfield Initiative has been successful in transforming contaminated industrial sites into productive uses, such as housing, parks, and businesses. It has also contributed to the overall goals of urban regeneration and community revitalization in Portland[^15^].

These case studies demonstrate the potential of land use planning and zoning as tools for brownfield redevelopment. They show how these tools can be used to guide the remediation and redevelopment of brownfield sites, to control the types of activities that can take place on these sites, and to encourage certain types of development. They also show how these tools can contribute to the overall goals of urban regeneration and community revitalization.

### Section: 9.3 Urban Design and Brownfield Redevelopment:

#### 9.3a Role of Urban Design in Brownfield Redevelopment

Urban design plays a crucial role in the redevelopment of brownfield sites. It is the process of designing and shaping the physical layout of cities, towns, and other urban environments. It involves the arrangement and design of buildings, public spaces, transport systems, services, and amenities[^14^]. It is a multidisciplinary field that includes architecture, landscape design, urban planning, and civil engineering[^15^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, urban design can help to transform contaminated and underutilized sites into vibrant, productive parts of the urban fabric. It can help to create attractive, functional, and sustainable urban environments that meet the needs of the community and contribute to urban regeneration[^16^].

Urban design can also help to mitigate some of the challenges associated with brownfield redevelopment. For example, it can help to integrate new developments into the existing urban fabric, ensuring that they are compatible with the surrounding land uses and urban form[^17^]. It can also help to manage the impacts of redevelopment on the local community, such as traffic congestion, noise, and visual impacts[^18^].

Moreover, urban design can contribute to the environmental sustainability of brownfield redevelopment. It can promote the efficient use of resources, reduce the environmental footprint of new developments, and enhance the resilience of urban areas to environmental challenges[^19^]. For instance, adaptive building, a common form of land recycling, involves the redevelopment of abandoned or unused building properties into new uses, which is more environmentally sustainable than building new structures with new materials[^20^].

In conclusion, urban design plays a pivotal role in brownfield redevelopment. It can help to transform contaminated and underutilized sites into vibrant, productive parts of the urban fabric, mitigate the challenges associated with redevelopment, and contribute to the environmental sustainability of urban areas.

#### 9.3b Urban Design Techniques for Brownfield Redevelopment

Urban design techniques for brownfield redevelopment are diverse and multifaceted, encompassing a range of strategies that aim to transform these sites into productive and sustainable urban spaces[^21^]. These techniques often involve a combination of architectural design, landscape design, urban planning, and civil engineering approaches[^22^].

One of the key techniques is adaptive reuse, which involves repurposing existing buildings and structures on brownfield sites for new uses[^23^]. This technique is particularly relevant for brownfield sites that contain abandoned or underutilized buildings. Adaptive reuse can help to preserve the historical and cultural heritage of urban areas, reduce waste and resource consumption, and promote the efficient use of urban land[^24^].

Another important technique is the integration of green infrastructure into the redevelopment of brownfield sites[^25^]. Green infrastructure refers to a network of green spaces and natural systems that provide a range of environmental, social, and economic benefits[^26^]. It can help to manage stormwater, reduce heat island effects, enhance biodiversity, improve air and water quality, and provide recreational opportunities[^27^]. 

In addition, urban design techniques for brownfield redevelopment often involve the use of sustainable building materials and technologies[^28^]. This can include the use of recycled or reclaimed materials, energy-efficient building systems, and renewable energy sources[^29^]. These techniques can help to reduce the environmental footprint of new developments and promote the sustainability of urban areas[^30^].

Furthermore, participatory design processes are often used in the redevelopment of brownfield sites[^31^]. These processes involve engaging the local community and other stakeholders in the design and planning process, which can help to ensure that the redevelopment meets the needs and aspirations of the community[^32^].

In conclusion, urban design techniques for brownfield redevelopment are diverse and multifaceted, encompassing a range of strategies that aim to transform these sites into productive and sustainable urban spaces. These techniques can help to mitigate the challenges associated with brownfield redevelopment and contribute to the environmental, social, and economic sustainability of urban areas[^33^].

[^21^]: Newman, P., & Jennings, I. (2008). Cities as Sustainable Ecosystems: Principles and Practices. Island Press.
[^22^]: Carmona, M., Heath, T., Oc, T., & Tiesdell, S. (2010). Public Places - Urban Spaces: The Dimensions of Urban Design. Routledge.
[^23^]: Bullen, P. A., & Love, P. E. D. (2011). Adaptive reuse of heritage buildings. Structural Survey.
