#### 9.3c Case Studies in Urban Design

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of urban design principles in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be implemented in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The High Line, New York City[^32^]

The High Line in New York City is a prime example of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. Originally a freight rail line elevated above the streets of Manhattan, the High Line was abandoned in the 1980s and slated for demolition[^33^]. However, a community-led effort to preserve and repurpose the structure resulted in its transformation into a public park[^34^].

The design of the High Line integrates green infrastructure, with a variety of plant species that provide habitat for urban wildlife and help to manage stormwater[^35^]. The park also features sustainable building materials and technologies, including energy-efficient lighting and rainwater collection systems[^36^].

The High Line project demonstrates the potential of adaptive reuse and green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment, as well as the importance of community engagement in the design and planning process[^37^].

##### Case Study 2: The Emscher Park, Germany[^38^]

The Emscher Park in Germany is another notable example of brownfield redevelopment. The park was once a heavily industrialized area, with numerous brownfield sites resulting from the decline of the coal and steel industries[^39^].

The redevelopment of Emscher Park involved a comprehensive approach that combined urban planning, landscape design, and architectural design[^40^]. The park now features a network of green spaces, recreational facilities, and cultural institutions, all integrated into the existing urban fabric[^41^].

The Emscher Park project illustrates the potential of integrated modification methodology in brownfield redevelopment. The project involved a thorough investigation of the urban form, the identification of key categories, and the evaluation of the system's performance in terms of ecological sustainability[^42^].

These case studies highlight the potential of urban design techniques in transforming brownfield sites into vibrant, sustainable urban spaces. They also underscore the importance of a comprehensive, integrated approach that considers the unique characteristics and needs of each site[^43^].

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields is a critical aspect of urban planning. It not only provides an opportunity to revitalize underutilized or abandoned areas but also helps in mitigating environmental risks associated with contaminated sites. The process of brownfield redevelopment involves a comprehensive understanding of environmental management practices, regulatory frameworks, and stakeholder engagement. It requires a multidisciplinary approach, integrating environmental science, urban planning, and socio-economic considerations. 

The successful redevelopment of brownfields can lead to numerous benefits, including economic growth, improved public health, and enhanced environmental quality. However, it also presents several challenges, such as the complexity of site remediation, financial constraints, and public acceptance. Therefore, it is essential for urban planners and environmental managers to adopt strategic planning and innovative solutions to overcome these challenges and achieve sustainable brownfield redevelopment.

## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Public Health

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment is a critical aspect of environmental management, particularly in urban areas where industrial or commercial activities have left behind sites that are underutilized due to real or perceived contamination. This chapter, "Brownfield Redevelopment and Public Health," delves into the intersection of these redevelopment projects and their implications on public health.

The process of brownfield redevelopment involves not only the remediation of contaminated sites but also the transformation of these areas into productive community assets. However, this process is not without its challenges. The potential health risks associated with exposure to contaminants present in brownfields are a significant concern. This chapter aims to explore these risks and discuss the strategies employed to mitigate them.

We will examine the role of environmental management in ensuring that brownfield redevelopment projects are carried out in a manner that safeguards public health. This includes the implementation of rigorous site assessments, risk evaluations, and remediation strategies. The chapter will also highlight the importance of community engagement in brownfield redevelopment projects, as public participation can play a crucial role in addressing health concerns and ensuring the success of redevelopment efforts.

In essence, this chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the complex relationship between brownfield redevelopment and public health. It will serve as a guide for environmental managers, urban planners, and public health officials, providing them with the knowledge and tools necessary to navigate the challenges of brownfield redevelopment while prioritizing public health.

### Section: 10.1 Health Impacts of Brownfields:

#### 10.1a Overview of Health Impacts

Brownfields, as previously defined, are underutilized properties that have been contaminated by previous industrial or commercial activities. The health impacts of these sites can be significant and varied, depending on the nature and extent of the contamination. 

The most direct health impacts come from exposure to hazardous substances present in the soil, groundwater, or structures on the site. These substances can include heavy metals, petroleum products, asbestos, and various organic compounds, many of which are known carcinogens or have other harmful effects on human health[^1^]. Exposure can occur through direct contact, inhalation of dust or vapors, or ingestion of contaminated water or food grown on the site.

In addition to these direct health risks, brownfields can also have indirect impacts on public health. For example, the presence of a brownfield can contribute to neighborhood blight, leading to decreased property values and increased stress among residents[^2^]. This can exacerbate health disparities in already vulnerable communities. Furthermore, brownfields can act as barriers to community development, preventing the establishment of amenities such as parks or grocery stores that could improve community health.

However, it's important to note that brownfield redevelopment can also have significant health benefits. By remediating contamination and repurposing these sites, communities can reduce exposure to hazardous substances, improve neighborhood aesthetics, and create new opportunities for healthy activities. For example, a former industrial site could be transformed into a community garden, providing access to fresh produce and opportunities for physical activity. 

In the context of climate change, brownfield redevelopment can also contribute to mitigation efforts. By promoting infill development and reducing the need for greenfield development, these projects can help to reduce greenhouse gas emissions and their associated health impacts[^3^]. 

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these health impacts, exploring the specific risks associated with different types of contaminants and the strategies used to mitigate these risks. We will also discuss the role of community engagement in addressing health concerns and maximizing the health benefits of brownfield redevelopment.

[^1^]: Probst, K. N., & Konisky, D. M. (2015). Superfund's toxic legacy: Assessing the effectiveness of the Superfund program. MIT Press.
[^2^]: Greenberg, M., & Lewis, M. J. (2000). Brownfields redevelopment, preferences and public involvement: A case study of an ethnically mixed neighborhood. Urban Studies, 37(13), 2501-2514.
[^3^]: De Sousa, C. A. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181-198.

#### 10.1b Role of Public Health in Brownfield Redevelopment

Public health plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. It is the responsibility of public health professionals to assess the potential health risks associated with brownfields and to ensure that these risks are adequately addressed during the redevelopment process[^3^]. This involves conducting health impact assessments, monitoring exposure to hazardous substances, and implementing measures to protect the health of the community.

Health impact assessments are a key tool used by public health professionals to evaluate the potential health effects of a proposed redevelopment project[^4^]. These assessments consider a wide range of factors, including the types and levels of contamination present, the proposed use of the site, and the vulnerability of the local population. The results of these assessments can inform decision-making and help to ensure that redevelopment projects do not exacerbate existing health disparities.

Monitoring exposure to hazardous substances is another important aspect of public health's role in brownfield redevelopment. This can involve testing soil, water, and air samples for contaminants, as well as conducting health surveillance to track any changes in the health status of the community[^5^]. If elevated levels of exposure are detected, public health professionals can work with environmental regulators and developers to implement remediation measures.

Public health also plays a role in protecting the health of the community during the redevelopment process. This can involve implementing safety measures to prevent exposure to hazardous substances, such as fencing off contaminated areas or using dust suppression techniques during construction[^6^]. Public health professionals can also provide education and outreach to the community about the risks associated with brownfields and the benefits of redevelopment.

Finally, public health can play a role in ensuring that brownfield redevelopment projects contribute to the overall health and well-being of the community. This can involve advocating for the inclusion of health-promoting features in redevelopment plans, such as green spaces or community gardens[^7^]. These features can provide opportunities for physical activity, improve air quality, and enhance community cohesion, all of which can have positive impacts on public health.

In conclusion, public health plays a vital role in brownfield redevelopment, from assessing and monitoring health risks to implementing protective measures and advocating for health-promoting features. By integrating public health considerations into the redevelopment process, we can ensure that brownfield sites are not only cleaned up, but also transformed into assets that contribute to the health and well-being of our communities.

[^3^]: Frumkin, H. (2002). Urban Sprawl and Public Health. Public Health Reports, 117(3), 201-217.
[^4^]: Bhatia, R., & Wernham, A. (2008). Integrating Human Health into Environmental Impact Assessment: An Unrealized Opportunity for Environmental Health and Justice. Environmental Health Perspectives, 116(8), 991-1000.

#### 10.1c Case Studies in Public Health and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore several case studies that highlight the intersection of public health and brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will illustrate how the principles and practices discussed in the previous sections have been applied in real-world settings, and the impacts they have had on community health.

##### Case Study 1: The Atlanta BeltLine Project

The Atlanta BeltLine project is a comprehensive urban redevelopment initiative in Atlanta, Georgia, that aims to transform a 22-mile loop of historic rail lines into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit[^7^]. The project has been lauded for its focus on sustainability and community engagement, and it provides a compelling example of how brownfield redevelopment can be leveraged to improve public health.

One of the key health-related aspects of the BeltLine project is its emphasis on creating accessible green spaces. As discussed in the related context, access to green spaces has been linked to a range of health benefits, including reductions in chronic disease rates and improvements in mental health[^8^]. By transforming brownfields into parks and trails, the BeltLine project is helping to increase access to green spaces for Atlanta residents.

In addition to creating new green spaces, the BeltLine project has also implemented measures to protect the health of the community during the redevelopment process. For example, the project team has worked closely with public health professionals to monitor exposure to hazardous substances and implement safety measures, such as dust suppression techniques during construction[^9^].

##### Case Study 2: The Chicago Brownfields Initiative

The Chicago Brownfields Initiative is another example of a city-wide effort to address the health impacts of brownfields. Launched in 1993, the initiative has facilitated the cleanup and redevelopment of hundreds of brownfield sites across the city[^10^].

One of the key health-related aspects of the Chicago Brownfields Initiative is its focus on community engagement. The initiative has made a concerted effort to involve local residents in the redevelopment process, including conducting health impact assessments and providing education and outreach about the risks and benefits of brownfield redevelopment[^11^].

The Chicago Brownfields Initiative has also prioritized the creation of urban green spaces as part of its redevelopment efforts. As in the Atlanta BeltLine project, this focus on green space has helped to improve public health by increasing physical activity levels, reducing obesity rates, and improving mental health[^12^].

These case studies illustrate the potential for brownfield redevelopment to improve public health. However, they also highlight the importance of careful planning and community engagement in ensuring that these health benefits are realized. As we continue to explore the intersection of public health and brownfield redevelopment, these lessons will be crucial in guiding our approach.

### Section: 10.2 Health Impact Assessment:

#### 10.2a Definition of Health Impact Assessment

Health Impact Assessment (HIA) is a systematic process that uses an array of data sources and analytic methods, and considers input from stakeholders, to determine the potential effects of a proposed policy, program, project, or event on the health of a population and the distribution of those effects within the population. HIA provides recommendations on monitoring and managing those effects <Harv|ECHP|1999|p=4>.

HIA is a tool that can help decision-makers make choices about alternatives and improvements to prevent disease/injury and to actively promote health. It is used to evaluate the potential health effects of a project or policy before it is built or implemented. An HIA can provide recommendations to increase positive health outcomes and minimize adverse health outcomes <Harv|Taylor|Quigley|2002|p=2>.

HIA is particularly relevant to the field of brownfield redevelopment, as it allows for the assessment of potential health impacts associated with the cleanup and reuse of these sites. This can include both direct health impacts, such as exposure to hazardous substances, and indirect health impacts, such as changes in community access to green space or employment opportunities.

#### 10.2b Procedures of Health Impact Assessment

The procedures of HIA are similar to those used in other forms of impact assessment, such as environmental impact assessment or social impact assessment. The main steps of an HIA include:

1. **Screening:** This step determines whether an HIA is needed and whether it will add value to the decision-making process.
2. **Scoping:** This step determines which potential health effects to consider, the methods for analysis, and the work plan for the assessment.
3. **Assessment:** This step identifies who may be affected and how they may be affected. It also provides an evaluation of the potential health impacts.
4. **Recommendations:** This step provides strategies to manage identified adverse health impacts.
5. **Reporting:** This step involves the presentation of the results to the decision-makers, the stakeholders, and the public.
6. **Monitoring and Evaluation:** This step monitors the changes in health determinants and health status, and evaluates the effectiveness of the measures implemented.

The main objective of HIA is to apply existing knowledge and evidence about health impacts, to specific social and community contexts, to develop evidence-based recommendations that inform decision-making in order to protect and improve community health and wellbeing <Harv|Acheson|1998>.

```
4. **Recommendations:** This step involves the development of practical strategies to manage the identified health impacts. These strategies can include both measures to enhance positive health impacts and mitigate negative ones. The recommendations should be evidence-based and feasible to implement within the context of the proposed policy, program, or project.

5. **Reporting:** This step involves the communication of the findings of the HIA to the decision-makers, stakeholders, and the public. The report should be clear, concise, and accessible to a non-technical audience. It should include a summary of the methods used, the health impacts identified, the recommendations made, and a plan for monitoring and evaluation.

6. **Monitoring and Evaluation:** This step involves tracking the implementation of the recommendations and evaluating the effectiveness of the HIA in influencing decision-making and health outcomes. This can involve both process evaluation (how well the HIA was conducted) and impact evaluation (the extent to which the HIA's recommendations were implemented and the resulting changes in health outcomes).

#### 10.2c Importance of Health Impact Assessment in Brownfield Redevelopment

Health Impact Assessment (HIA) plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. It provides a structured process to identify potential health risks and benefits associated with the redevelopment of brownfield sites, and to develop strategies to manage these impacts. 

The use of HIA in brownfield redevelopment can help to ensure that the redevelopment process contributes to the improvement of public health and wellbeing, and does not inadvertently lead to negative health outcomes. For example, an HIA can help to identify potential risks associated with exposure to residual contamination at the site, and recommend measures to mitigate these risks. 

Furthermore, an HIA can also help to identify opportunities to enhance positive health outcomes through the redevelopment process. For example, the redevelopment of a brownfield site could include the creation of new green spaces or recreational facilities, which can contribute to physical activity and mental wellbeing in the local community.

In conclusion, the use of HIA in brownfield redevelopment is a valuable tool for ensuring that the redevelopment process is conducted in a way that is beneficial to public health and wellbeing. It provides a structured process for identifying and managing health impacts, and for ensuring that these considerations are integrated into the decision-making process. <Harv|Acheson|1998>.
```

#### 10.2c Case Studies in Health Impact Assessment

To further illustrate the importance and application of Health Impact Assessment (HIA) in brownfield redevelopment, let's consider a few case studies.

##### Case Study 1: The Atlanta BeltLine Project

The Atlanta BeltLine project is a comprehensive transportation and economic development effort that includes a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit along a historic 22-mile railroad corridor circling downtown Atlanta. The project aimed to transform the city by spurring economic growth and improving public health outcomes. 

An HIA was conducted to assess the potential health impacts of the project. The HIA identified several potential positive health impacts, including increased physical activity due to improved access to recreational facilities, and improved air quality due to reduced vehicle emissions. However, it also identified potential negative impacts, such as displacement of low-income residents due to rising property values, and increased exposure to noise and dust during construction <Harv|Ross|2012>.

Based on these findings, the HIA made several recommendations, including the provision of affordable housing, measures to mitigate noise and dust during construction, and the inclusion of health-promoting features in the design of the parks and trails. The HIA also recommended ongoing monitoring and evaluation to track the implementation of these recommendations and their impact on health outcomes.

##### Case Study 2: The Hunters Point Shipyard Redevelopment, San Francisco

The Hunters Point Shipyard in San Francisco, a former naval shipyard, is one of the largest brownfield sites in the U.S. The proposed redevelopment of the site included residential, commercial, and recreational uses.

An HIA was conducted to assess the potential health impacts of the redevelopment. The HIA identified several potential health risks, including exposure to residual contamination at the site, and increased traffic and associated air pollution. It also identified potential health benefits, such as increased access to recreational facilities and job opportunities <Harv|Bhatia|2008>.

The HIA made several recommendations to manage these impacts, including measures to ensure safe remediation of the site, traffic management strategies to reduce air pollution, and the provision of affordable housing and job training programs. The HIA also recommended ongoing monitoring and evaluation to track the implementation of these recommendations and their impact on health outcomes.

These case studies illustrate the value of HIA in informing decision-making in brownfield redevelopment, and in identifying and managing potential health impacts. They also highlight the importance of ongoing monitoring and evaluation in ensuring the effectiveness of the HIA process.

### Section: 10.3 Public Health and Community Engagement:

#### 10.3a Role of Community Engagement in Public Health

Community engagement plays a crucial role in public health, especially in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It is a process of involving the community in decisions that affect their health and well-being. This involvement can take many forms, from informing the community about planned activities, consulting with them about their views and preferences, involving them in decision-making processes, collaborating with them in the development of alternatives and solutions, and empowering them to take action and make decisions <Harv|CDC|2011>.

Community engagement is particularly important in brownfield redevelopment because these sites often pose significant health risks to the local community. These risks can include exposure to hazardous substances, increased stress and anxiety due to uncertainty about health risks, and potential displacement due to redevelopment activities. By engaging the community in the redevelopment process, these risks can be identified, understood, and mitigated.

Community engagement can also help to ensure that the benefits of brownfield redevelopment, such as improved access to green space, increased economic activity, and improved public health outcomes, are equitably distributed. This is particularly important in communities that are already disadvantaged or marginalized, where the benefits of redevelopment may not be realized without active engagement and advocacy.

There are several strategies for effective community engagement in brownfield redevelopment. These include:

- **Communication and education**: Providing clear, accurate, and timely information about the redevelopment process, the potential health risks and benefits, and the ways in which the community can get involved.

- **Consultation and participation**: Seeking the community's views and preferences, involving them in decision-making processes, and incorporating their input into redevelopment plans.

- **Collaboration and partnership**: Working with community organizations, local leaders, and other stakeholders to develop and implement redevelopment plans.

- **Empowerment and advocacy**: Supporting the community to take action and make decisions, and advocating for their interests in the redevelopment process.

In conclusion, community engagement is a critical component of public health in brownfield redevelopment. It helps to identify and mitigate health risks, ensure the equitable distribution of benefits, and build trust and collaboration between the community and other stakeholders. By actively engaging the community in the redevelopment process, we can create healthier, more sustainable, and more equitable communities <Harv|EPA|2013>.

#### 10.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Public Health

Engaging the community in public health, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment, requires a multi-faceted approach. Here are some techniques that can be employed:

- **Use of Technology**: With the advent of urban computing, public health organizations can leverage technology to engage the community. Smart devices and social media platforms can be used to disseminate information rapidly about disease outbreaks, medical discoveries, and other news related to public health <Harv|CDC|2011>. For instance, a collaboration between the CDC and Google led to the creation of a map predicting flu outbreaks based on flu-related search queries <Harv|Ginsberg et al.|2009>. Similarly, technology can be used to alert residents about high pollution days, thus preventing adverse health effects <Harv|ANN & CRF Research|2015>.

- **Community Meetings and Workshops**: Regular meetings and workshops can be organized to educate the community about the redevelopment process, potential health risks, and benefits. These platforms also provide an opportunity for the community to voice their concerns and suggestions.

- **Surveys and Polls**: Surveys and polls can be conducted to understand the community's views and preferences. This can help in making informed decisions that align with the community's needs and expectations.

- **Partnerships with Local Organizations**: Collaborating with local organizations can help in reaching out to a larger audience. These organizations often have a deep understanding of the community's needs and can play a crucial role in facilitating communication and participation.

- **Proactive Outreach**: Proactive outreach involves reaching out to community members who may not be actively involved in the process. This can be done through door-to-door visits, phone calls, or even digital platforms.

- **Public Art and Cultural Events**: Public art and cultural events can be used as a platform to engage the community in a more informal and interactive manner. These events can be used to disseminate information, gather feedback, and foster a sense of community ownership and pride in the redevelopment process.

Remember, the goal of community engagement is not just to inform, but to involve the community in the decision-making process. This ensures that the redevelopment process is inclusive, equitable, and responsive to the community's needs and aspirations.

#### 10.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Public Health

In this section, we will explore two case studies that highlight the importance of community engagement in public health, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 1: Community-Based Participatory Research as a Tool for Policy Change

The first case study involves the collaboration between Communities for a Better Environment (CBE), Liberty Hill Foundation, and an academic research team from University of California, Santa Cruz, Brown University, and Occidental College. The collaboration aimed to review Rule 1402, a state goal set in 1994 to reduce public health risk from cancerous and noncancerous emissions by large industries <Harv|CBE|2010>.

The maximum individual cancer risk was initially set at 100 cancer risks per million. However, due to the collaborative efforts of CBE and its partners, the risk was reduced to 25 cancer risks per million six years later, representing a reduction in acceptable risk levels to 75% <Harv|CBE|2010>. This collaboration led to more efforts in the reduction of allowable risk level faced by various communities and encouraged the California Environmental Protection Agency to broaden their view on issues such as these, to put more emphasis on risk exposure when making new policies.

##### Case Study 2: Linking Exposure Assessment Science With Policy

The second case study is still in progress and focuses on linking exposure assessment science with policy. This involves the use of scientific research to understand the direct and indirect consequences of toxicity and chemical secretion. By partnering with health providers and academic institutions, CBE conducts research on human subjects and provides online training for human subject protection via the National Institute of Health website <Harv|CBE|2010>.

These case studies highlight the importance of community engagement in public health. They demonstrate how communities can work together to fight local pollution and achieve healthier living conditions. Furthermore, they show how scientific research can be used to inform policy decisions and reduce health risks associated with environmental pollution.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, these case studies underscore the importance of involving the community in the decision-making process. By doing so, redevelopment projects can better address the community's needs and concerns, ultimately leading to more successful and sustainable outcomes.

### Conclusion

The chapter on Brownfield Redevelopment and Public Health has provided a comprehensive overview of the intersection between environmental management and public health. It has highlighted the importance of redeveloping brownfield sites, not just for economic and environmental reasons, but also for the potential health benefits it can bring to communities. 

The chapter has underscored the significance of conducting thorough environmental assessments to identify potential contaminants and health risks associated with brownfield sites. It has also emphasized the need for effective remediation strategies to mitigate these risks and ensure the safety of the public. 

Moreover, the chapter has discussed the role of public participation in brownfield redevelopment projects. It has stressed the importance of involving the community in decision-making processes to ensure that redevelopment plans align with their needs and aspirations. 

In conclusion, brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires a multidisciplinary approach. It is not just about cleaning up contaminated sites, but also about improving public health, promoting social equity, and fostering sustainable development.

## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Economic Development

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment is a critical aspect of environmental management, particularly in urban areas where industrial or commercial activities have left behind sites that are underutilized due to real or perceived environmental contamination. This chapter, "Brownfield Redevelopment and Economic Development," will delve into the intricate relationship between the remediation of these brownfield sites and the potential for economic development.

The process of brownfield redevelopment is not just about cleaning up contaminated sites. It's about transforming these areas into productive assets that can stimulate economic growth, create jobs, and enhance community vitality. However, this transformation is not without its challenges. The complexities of site assessment, cleanup, and redevelopment planning require a comprehensive understanding of environmental, economic, and regulatory factors.

In this chapter, we will explore the economic implications of brownfield redevelopment, including the potential benefits and costs. We will also discuss the role of various stakeholders, from government agencies to private developers, in facilitating brownfield redevelopment. Furthermore, we will examine case studies that highlight successful brownfield redevelopment projects and the economic development strategies employed.

By the end of this chapter, readers should have a deeper understanding of how brownfield redevelopment can serve as a catalyst for economic development, and the key considerations in planning and implementing such projects. This knowledge will be invaluable for environmental managers, urban planners, and policymakers who are tasked with turning brownfield sites into opportunities for sustainable economic growth.

### Section: 11.1 Economic Impacts of Brownfields

#### 11.1a Overview of Economic Impacts

Brownfield redevelopment can have significant economic impacts, both positive and negative, on local and regional economies. These impacts can be direct, indirect, or induced, and they can vary widely depending on the specific characteristics of the brownfield site and the nature of the redevelopment project.

##### Direct Economic Impacts

Direct economic impacts are the immediate effects of brownfield redevelopment, which are typically measured in terms of job creation, increased property values, and additional tax revenues. For instance, the cleanup and construction activities associated with brownfield redevelopment can create jobs in industries such as environmental consulting, construction, and waste management. Once the redevelopment project is completed, the newly developed property can attract businesses and residents, leading to increased property values and tax revenues.

##### Indirect Economic Impacts

Indirect economic impacts are the secondary effects of brownfield redevelopment, which occur as the direct impacts ripple through the local economy. For example, the jobs created by a brownfield redevelopment project can lead to increased spending on goods and services, which can stimulate additional economic activity and job creation in other sectors of the economy. Similarly, the increase in property values can lead to increased wealth for property owners, which can also stimulate additional spending and economic activity.

##### Induced Economic Impacts

Induced economic impacts are the tertiary effects of brownfield redevelopment, which occur as the indirect impacts further ripple through the local economy. For instance, the additional economic activity and job creation resulting from the direct and indirect impacts can lead to increased demand for public services, which can stimulate further economic activity and job creation in the public sector.

However, it's important to note that brownfield redevelopment can also have negative economic impacts. For instance, the cleanup of contaminated sites can be costly, and these costs can be borne by taxpayers if the responsible parties cannot be identified or are unable to pay. Additionally, the redevelopment of brownfield sites can lead to gentrification and displacement of low-income residents, particularly in urban areas.

In the next sections, we will delve deeper into these economic impacts, examining the factors that influence their magnitude and distribution, and discussing strategies for maximizing the positive impacts and mitigating the negative impacts.

#### 11.1b Role of Economic Development in Brownfield Redevelopment

Economic development plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. It is the driving force that transforms these contaminated sites into productive properties, contributing to the local economy and improving the quality of life in the surrounding communities.

##### Economic Development Strategies

Economic development strategies for brownfield redevelopment typically involve a combination of public and private sector efforts. The public sector, often through the Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) and other federal and state agencies, provides funding and regulatory support for brownfield cleanup and redevelopment. This includes the $250 million per year for brownfields revitalization established by the Small Business Liability Relief and Brownfields Revitalization Act, which is used for brownfields assessment, cleanup, revolving loans, and environmental job training[^1^].

The private sector, on the other hand, brings investment and entrepreneurial activity to the redevelopment process. This can include property developers, businesses, and investors who see potential in the brownfield site and are willing to take on the risks associated with its redevelopment. The involvement of the private sector is often crucial for the economic viability of the redevelopment project, as it can bring the necessary financial resources and market expertise to the table.

##### Economic Development Outcomes

The economic development outcomes of brownfield redevelopment can be significant. As previously discussed, the direct economic impacts include job creation, increased property values, and additional tax revenues. But the benefits of brownfield redevelopment can go beyond these direct impacts.

For instance, brownfield redevelopment can lead to the revitalization of distressed neighborhoods, attracting new businesses and residents and stimulating economic growth in the surrounding area. It can also contribute to the preservation of open space and parks, improving the quality of life in the community and potentially attracting more residents and businesses.

Moreover, brownfield redevelopment can lead to the creation of "green-space" or environmentally friendly developments, which can have additional economic benefits. For example, green developments can attract businesses and residents who value sustainability, potentially leading to higher property values and tax revenues. They can also lead to cost savings in terms of energy use and waste management, further contributing to the economic viability of the redevelopment project[^2^].

In conclusion, economic development plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment, driving the transformation of contaminated sites into productive properties and contributing to local economies and communities.

[^1^]: Small Business Liability Relief and Brownfields Revitalization Act, Section 104(k) and Section 128(a).
[^2^]: United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA). "Brownfields and Land Revitalization."

#### 11.1c Case Studies in Economic Development and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore some case studies that highlight the economic impacts of brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide real-world examples of how brownfield redevelopment can lead to significant economic development outcomes.

##### Case Study 1: The Emeryville Public Market, Emeryville, California

The Emeryville Public Market, located in Emeryville, California, is a prime example of successful brownfield redevelopment. The site, which was once a steel mill and later a trucking facility, was contaminated with heavy metals and petroleum hydrocarbons[^2^]. 

In the late 1980s, the city of Emeryville, with the help of the EPA's Brownfields Program, began the process of cleaning up and redeveloping the site. Today, the Emeryville Public Market is a vibrant mixed-use development that includes retail, office space, and residential units. The redevelopment has led to significant economic benefits, including the creation of over 2,000 jobs and an increase in property values in the surrounding area[^2^].

##### Case Study 2: The Menomonee Valley, Milwaukee, Wisconsin

The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, is another example of successful brownfield redevelopment. The valley, which was once home to heavy industry, was left with contaminated soil and groundwater after the industries closed down[^3^].

In the late 1990s, the city of Milwaukee, with support from the EPA and other federal and state agencies, began the process of cleaning up and redeveloping the valley. Today, the Menomonee Valley is home to a variety of businesses, including manufacturing companies, offices, and retail outlets. The redevelopment has led to the creation of over 5,000 jobs and has generated millions of dollars in tax revenue for the city[^3^].

These case studies highlight the potential economic benefits of brownfield redevelopment. Not only can it lead to job creation and increased property values, but it can also stimulate economic growth in the surrounding area and generate significant tax revenue. However, it's important to note that the success of brownfield redevelopment projects often depends on a combination of public and private sector efforts, as well as the availability of funding and regulatory support.

[^2^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2016). Emeryville Public Market: Emeryville, CA. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2016-06/documents/emeryville-public-market.pdf

[^3^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2016). Menomonee Valley: Milwaukee, WI. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2016-06/documents/menomonee-valley.pdf

### Section: 11.2 Economic Development Strategies:

#### 11.2a Overview of Economic Development Strategies

Economic development strategies are crucial in the process of brownfield redevelopment. These strategies are designed to stimulate economic growth and development, often through the creation of jobs, the attraction of businesses, and the generation of wealth. The strategies are often categorized into five waves, each with its unique characteristics and approaches. 

##### First-wave Economic Development: Growth Promotion Approach

The first wave of economic development, also known as the Growth Promotion Approach, is characterized by the use of financial incentives to attract industries. This approach was first implemented in the United States during the 1930s as a response to the Great Depression[^4^]. The incentives often include tax abatements and loans aimed at reducing the costs associated with land, infrastructure, and labor[^4^].

###### Smokestack-Chasing

A common practice during the first wave of economic development is "smokestack-chasing." This approach involves attracting a major employer to a region, such as a factory, mine, college, or prison[^4^]. The process begins with promoting the location to the potential employer, followed by offering incentives such as cheap land or labor, or lax environmental regulations[^4^].

Critics of smokestack-chasing argue that it is haphazard and likely to prove ineffective. They suggest that while it may result in short-term job growth and per capita income increases, it may not lead to sustainable economic development[^4^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, the first-wave economic development strategies can be useful in attracting businesses to redeveloped sites. However, it is crucial to ensure that these strategies are implemented in a manner that promotes sustainable economic development and does not compromise environmental standards.

In the following sections, we will explore the other waves of economic development and how they can be applied in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

[^4^]: Blakely, E. J., & Leigh, N. G. (2010). Planning local economic development: Theory and practice. Sage.

#### 11.2b Selection of Economic Development Strategies

The selection of economic development strategies for brownfield redevelopment should be guided by a comprehensive understanding of the local economic context, the specific characteristics of the brownfield site, and the broader goals of sustainable development. 

##### Second-wave Economic Development: Human Capital Approach

The second wave of economic development, also known as the Human Capital Approach, emerged in the 1960s and 1970s. This approach emphasizes the importance of investing in human capital - education, skills, and health - as a means to stimulate economic growth[^5^]. 

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this could involve strategies such as job training programs for local residents, partnerships with educational institutions to provide skills training, or health initiatives to address any health impacts associated with the brownfield site[^5^].

##### Third-wave Economic Development: Innovation and Entrepreneurship Approach

The third wave of economic development, which emerged in the 1980s and 1990s, focuses on fostering innovation and entrepreneurship. This approach recognizes the role of knowledge and innovation in driving economic growth and seeks to create an environment that supports the creation and growth of innovative businesses[^6^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this could involve strategies such as creating innovation hubs or business incubators on redeveloped sites, providing support for startups and small businesses, or fostering partnerships between businesses and research institutions[^6^].

##### Fourth-wave Economic Development: Sustainability Approach

The fourth wave of economic development, which emerged in the 2000s, integrates economic development with environmental sustainability. This approach recognizes the interdependence of economic growth and environmental health and seeks to promote development strategies that are both economically viable and environmentally sustainable[^7^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this could involve strategies such as green building practices, renewable energy projects, or the creation of green jobs[^7^].

##### Fifth-wave Economic Development: Place-based Approach

The fifth wave of economic development, which is still emerging, focuses on the unique characteristics and assets of a particular place. This approach recognizes that each place has unique strengths and challenges, and seeks to develop strategies that leverage these unique characteristics[^8^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this could involve strategies such as community-led redevelopment projects, cultural or heritage preservation, or the creation of unique public spaces[^8^].

In conclusion, the selection of economic development strategies for brownfield redevelopment should be guided by a comprehensive understanding of the local economic context, the specific characteristics of the brownfield site, and the broader goals of sustainable development. It is also important to engage with local communities and stakeholders in the process of selecting and implementing these strategies.

In the next section, we will explore the role of policy in supporting the implementation of these strategies.

[^5^]: Hirschman, A.O. (1958). The Strategy of Economic Development. Yale University Press.
[^6^]: Lewis, W.A. (1955). The Theory of Economic Growth. Routledge.
[^7^]: Ottosson, S. (2005). Strategies for International Development. World Bank Development Marketplace Competition.
[^8^]: Rostow, W.W. (1960). The Stages of Economic Growth: A Non-Communist Manifesto. Cambridge University Press.

#### 11.2c Case Studies in Economic Development Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of economic development strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the theoretical concepts discussed in the previous sections have been applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The Pittsburgh Technology Center

The Pittsburgh Technology Center (PTC) is a prime example of the third-wave economic development strategy. Once a brownfield site occupied by the Jones and Laughlin Steel Company, the PTC is now a thriving hub for technology and innovation[^7^].

The redevelopment of the PTC was a collaborative effort involving the University of Pittsburgh, Carnegie Mellon University, and the Urban Redevelopment Authority of Pittsburgh. The project aimed to create an environment conducive to innovation and entrepreneurship, with a focus on attracting technology-based businesses[^7^].

The PTC now houses over 100 companies, including startups, established technology firms, and research institutions. The redevelopment has not only revitalized the site but also contributed to the economic growth of the region[^7^].

##### Case Study 2: The Menomonee Valley Industrial Center

The Menomonee Valley Industrial Center in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, is an example of the fourth-wave economic development strategy. Once a brownfield site with a history of industrial use, the Menomonee Valley has been transformed into a sustainable industrial park[^8^].

The redevelopment project was guided by a comprehensive plan that integrated economic development with environmental sustainability. The plan included strategies such as the use of green building practices, the creation of green jobs, and the restoration of natural habitats[^8^].

The Menomonee Valley Industrial Center now hosts over 40 businesses, providing more than 5,000 jobs. The project has not only led to economic growth but also improved environmental conditions and quality of life in the surrounding communities[^8^].

##### Case Study 3: The Shared Value Approach in Brownfield Redevelopment

The Shared Value Approach, as discussed in the empirical research context, can also be applied to brownfield redevelopment. This approach involves creating economic value in a way that also creates value for society by addressing its needs and challenges[^9^].

A case in point is the redevelopment of a brownfield site in East Baltimore, Maryland. The project, led by a partnership between Johns Hopkins University and the Annie E. Casey Foundation, aimed to create shared value by addressing the economic, social, and environmental needs of the community[^9^].

The project involved the creation of a new mixed-use development, including affordable housing, retail spaces, and a new school. The project also included job training programs for local residents and initiatives to improve health outcomes. This approach has led to significant economic growth while also addressing social and environmental challenges in the community[^9^].

These case studies illustrate the diverse ways in which economic development strategies can be applied in brownfield redevelopment. They highlight the potential for brownfield redevelopment to contribute to economic growth, environmental sustainability, and social well-being.

[^7^]: Pittsburgh Technology Center. (n.d.). About PTC. Retrieved from http://www.ptcpgh.org/about-ptc
[^8^]: Menomonee Valley Partners. (n.d.). Menomonee Valley Industrial Center. Retrieved from http://www.renewthevalley.org/projects/mvic
[^9^]: Porter, M. E., & Kramer, M. R. (2011). Creating Shared Value. Harvard Business Review, 89(1/2), 62–77.

### Section: 11.3 Economic Development and Community Engagement:

#### 11.3a Role of Community Engagement in Economic Development

Community engagement plays a crucial role in the economic development of brownfield redevelopment. It is a process that involves the active participation of community members in decision-making processes and implementation of strategies that affect their living standards[^9^]. This engagement is a key component of Community Economic Development (CED), a field that works with government and private sectors to build strong communities, industries, and markets[^9^].

Community engagement in brownfield redevelopment can take various forms, including public meetings, workshops, surveys, and online platforms. These methods provide opportunities for community members to voice their concerns, share their ideas, and contribute to the development of strategies for brownfield redevelopment[^9^].

Community engagement is not only about involving community members in decision-making processes but also about building their capacity to participate effectively. This is where community capacity building comes into play. It involves increasing the ability and desire of groups, businesses, municipalities, and not-for-profit organizations to engage in community economic development[^10^]. 

Capacity building can be achieved through various means, including training, education, and the provision of resources. For example, stakeholders such as governments can contribute to environmental community capacity building by providing practical support in terms of resource provision and opening up opportunities for participation[^10^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement and capacity building can lead to several benefits. These include improved decision-making, increased community support for redevelopment projects, and enhanced social and economic outcomes for the community[^11^]. 

For instance, community engagement can help to identify local resources that can be utilized for redevelopment, thereby promoting local economic development. It can also help to ensure that redevelopment strategies are aligned with the needs and aspirations of the community, thereby enhancing social conditions in a sustainable way[^11^].

In conclusion, community engagement and capacity building are essential components of economic development in brownfield redevelopment. They not only facilitate the effective exploration and utilization of local resources but also foster the economic, social, ecological, and cultural well-being of communities[^11^].

[^9^]: Shragge, E., & Toye, M. (2006). Community economic development: Building for social change. Fernwood Publishing.

[^10^]: Eade, D. (1997). Capacity-building: An approach to people-centered development. Oxfam.

[^11^]: Chaskin, R. J. (2001). Building community capacity: A definitional framework and case studies from a comprehensive community initiative. Urban Affairs Review, 36(3), 291-323.

#### 11.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Economic Development

Engaging the community in economic development, particularly in brownfield redevelopment, requires a strategic approach that combines various techniques. These techniques should aim to foster open communication, build trust, and promote active participation among community members[^12^].

##### Public Meetings and Workshops

Public meetings and workshops are traditional methods of community engagement. They provide a platform for community members to voice their concerns, share their ideas, and contribute to the development of strategies for brownfield redevelopment[^13^]. These meetings should be organized in a way that encourages open dialogue and constructive feedback. They should also be inclusive, ensuring that all community members, regardless of their socioeconomic status, have an opportunity to participate[^14^].

##### Surveys and Online Platforms

Surveys and online platforms are effective tools for gathering community input. Surveys can be used to collect data on community perceptions, needs, and preferences. They can be administered in person, by mail, or online[^15^]. Online platforms, on the other hand, can facilitate ongoing dialogue and collaboration among community members. They can also provide a space for sharing updates and progress reports on redevelopment projects[^16^].

##### Capacity Building Initiatives

Capacity building initiatives are crucial for enhancing community engagement. They involve increasing the ability and desire of community members to participate in economic development activities. This can be achieved through training, education, and the provision of resources[^17^]. For instance, stakeholders such as governments can contribute to environmental community capacity building by providing practical support in terms of resource provision and opening up opportunities for participation[^18^].

##### Partnerships and Collaborations

Partnerships and collaborations can also enhance community engagement in economic development. They involve working with various stakeholders, including government agencies, private sector entities, non-profit organizations, and community groups. These partnerships can leverage the unique strengths and resources of each stakeholder to achieve common goals[^19^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, partnerships can facilitate the sharing of technical expertise, financial resources, and decision-making power. They can also foster a sense of shared ownership and responsibility among stakeholders, thereby increasing community support for redevelopment projects[^20^].

In conclusion, engaging the community in economic development requires a multifaceted approach that combines various techniques. These techniques should aim to foster open communication, build trust, and promote active participation among community members. They should also be flexible and adaptable, taking into account the unique characteristics and needs of each community[^21^].

#### 11.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Economic Development

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the role of community engagement in economic development, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 1: The WIRED Initiative

The Workforce Innovation in Regional Economic Development (WIRED) initiative is a prime example of a federal program that emphasizes human capital and 'soft' infrastructure in economic development[^19^]. As Renski (2009) notes, the WIRED initiative incorporates well-accepted economic development practices, such as fostering cluster development to stimulate innovation and technology spillover[^20^]. However, the novel aspect of WIRED is its administration and funding by the U.S. federal government, which has had little involvement in regional economic development since the 1970s[^21^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, the WIRED initiative can serve as a model for how federal programs can support regional growth that crosses traditional political and jurisdictional boundaries. By focusing on human capital and 'soft' infrastructure, such programs can help communities develop the organizational know-how and networking skills necessary for successful brownfield redevelopment[^22^].

##### Case Study 2: Smart Cities

Smart cities represent another interesting case study in community engagement and economic development. University research labs have developed prototypes for intelligent cities, which use technology to improve the quality of life for their residents and promote sustainable economic development[^23^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, smart cities can provide valuable insights into how technology can be used to facilitate community engagement. For instance, online platforms can be used to gather community input, share updates on redevelopment projects, and facilitate ongoing dialogue and collaboration among community members[^24^].

##### Case Study 3: Think Resilience

Think Resilience is an online course that teaches individuals and communities how to build resilience in the face of environmental, economic, and social challenges[^25^]. This course emphasizes the importance of community engagement in building resilience and promoting sustainable economic development.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, Think Resilience can serve as a model for how education and training can enhance community engagement. By increasing the ability and desire of community members to participate in economic development activities, such initiatives can contribute to the success of brownfield redevelopment projects[^26^].

In conclusion, these case studies highlight the importance of community engagement in economic development and provide valuable insights into how this engagement can be facilitated in the context of brownfield redevelopment. They underscore the need for strategic approaches that foster open communication, build trust, and promote active participation among community members.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields is a complex, multifaceted process that requires careful planning and management. It is a critical component of sustainable development, contributing to economic growth while also addressing environmental concerns. The process involves various stakeholders, including government agencies, private developers, and community members, all of whom play a crucial role in ensuring successful redevelopment. 

The economic benefits of brownfield redevelopment are significant. By transforming underutilized or abandoned properties into productive land, communities can stimulate local economies, create jobs, and increase tax revenues. However, these benefits must be balanced against the potential environmental and health risks associated with contaminated sites. 

Effective environmental management is therefore essential in brownfield redevelopment. It involves conducting thorough site assessments, implementing appropriate remediation strategies, and monitoring the site to ensure ongoing safety and compliance with environmental regulations. 

Despite the challenges, brownfield redevelopment represents a valuable opportunity to promote sustainable economic development while improving environmental quality. It is a testament to the power of innovation and collaboration in addressing complex environmental and economic issues.
