## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Social Equity

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment is a critical aspect of environmental management, particularly in urban areas where industrial or commercial activities have left behind contaminated sites. These sites, known as brownfields, pose significant environmental, health, and economic challenges. However, they also present opportunities for redevelopment and revitalization. This chapter, "Brownfield Redevelopment and Social Equity", will delve into the intersection of these redevelopment efforts and the concept of social equity.

Social equity is a principle that emphasizes fair distribution of resources, opportunities, and burdens in society. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, it refers to the idea that the benefits of redevelopment - such as improved environmental conditions, job creation, and increased property values - should be equitably distributed among all community members. Conversely, the burdens associated with redevelopment, such as potential displacement or gentrification, should not disproportionately fall on disadvantaged or marginalized groups.

The chapter will explore how brownfield redevelopment can be leveraged to promote social equity, and the challenges that may arise in this process. It will also discuss strategies and best practices for ensuring that brownfield redevelopment contributes to social equity, rather than exacerbating existing inequalities. 

While the task of balancing environmental, economic, and social objectives in brownfield redevelopment is complex, it is crucial for achieving sustainable and inclusive urban development. This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of this issue, and to equip readers with the knowledge and tools to navigate it effectively.

### Section: 12.1 Social Impacts of Brownfields:

#### 12.1a Overview of Social Impacts

Brownfields, as previously defined, are abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities where redevelopment is complicated by environmental contamination. The existence of brownfields in a community can have significant social impacts, affecting the quality of life, economic opportunities, and overall wellbeing of residents. 

The social impacts of brownfields can be both direct and indirect. Direct impacts include health risks associated with exposure to hazardous substances, while indirect impacts can include decreased property values, reduced tax revenues, and the stigma associated with living or working near a contaminated site. 

#### 12.1b Health Risks

Exposure to hazardous substances found in brownfields can lead to a range of health problems, including respiratory diseases, cardiovascular diseases, and cancer. These health risks can disproportionately affect disadvantaged or marginalized groups, who often live in closer proximity to brownfields due to historical patterns of environmental injustice. This can exacerbate existing health disparities and contribute to a cycle of poverty and poor health.

#### 12.1c Economic Impacts

Brownfields can also have significant economic impacts on a community. Property values near brownfields are often lower due to the perceived risks associated with contamination. This can lead to reduced tax revenues for local governments, limiting their ability to provide essential services and invest in community development. 

Furthermore, the presence of brownfields can deter investment and economic development in the surrounding area. This can lead to job loss and increased poverty, particularly in communities that were previously reliant on the industries associated with the brownfield site.

#### 12.1d Social Equity Considerations

The social impacts of brownfields raise important considerations for social equity. As noted in the introduction to this chapter, social equity refers to the fair distribution of resources, opportunities, and burdens in society. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this means ensuring that the benefits of redevelopment are equitably distributed, and that the burdens do not disproportionately fall on disadvantaged or marginalized groups.

However, achieving social equity in brownfield redevelopment is a complex task. It requires careful planning and community engagement to ensure that redevelopment efforts do not lead to displacement or gentrification, and that the needs and interests of all community members are taken into account. 

In the following sections, we will explore strategies and best practices for promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment, and discuss case studies that illustrate these principles in action.

#### 12.1b Role of Social Equity in Brownfield Redevelopment

Social equity plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. It is a principle that ensures fair distribution of benefits and burdens associated with brownfield redevelopment, particularly among marginalized and disadvantaged communities. 

The Small Business Liability Relief and Brownfields Revitalization Act, for instance, stipulates that funding criteria should address "sensitive populations" and involve the local community. This is a clear recognition of the need for social equity in brownfield redevelopment. 

##### 12.1b(i) Involvement of Local Communities

Involvement of local communities in brownfield redevelopment is a key aspect of social equity. This can be achieved through public–private partnerships, as mentioned in the Act. These partnerships can ensure that the interests of the local community are taken into account during the redevelopment process. 

For example, community members can provide valuable input on the types of redevelopment that would be most beneficial to them, such as affordable housing, green spaces, or job-creating businesses. This not only ensures that the redevelopment meets the needs of the community, but also helps to build trust and reduce the stigma associated with brownfields.

##### 12.1b(ii) Addressing Sensitive Populations

Another important aspect of social equity in brownfield redevelopment is addressing the needs of "sensitive populations". These can include low-income communities, minority communities, and other groups that may be disproportionately affected by the presence of brownfields.

For instance, these communities often bear the brunt of the health risks associated with brownfields, as they are more likely to live in close proximity to these sites. Therefore, any redevelopment efforts should prioritize the protection of human health and the environment, particularly in these sensitive areas.

##### 12.1b(iii) Fair Distribution of Benefits

Finally, social equity in brownfield redevelopment involves ensuring a fair distribution of benefits. This means that the economic benefits of redevelopment, such as job creation and increased property values, should be equitably distributed among all members of the community.

This can be achieved through policies that promote inclusive economic development, such as job training programs for local residents or affordable housing requirements for new developments. By ensuring that all community members have an opportunity to benefit from the redevelopment, we can help to break the cycle of poverty and poor health associated with brownfields.

In conclusion, social equity is a critical component of brownfield redevelopment. By involving local communities, addressing the needs of sensitive populations, and ensuring a fair distribution of benefits, we can ensure that brownfield redevelopment contributes to a more equitable and sustainable future.

#### 12.1b(iii) Fair Distribution of Benefits (Continued)

The fair distribution of benefits is a critical component of social equity in brownfield redevelopment. This involves ensuring that the benefits derived from the redevelopment, such as job creation, improved housing, and enhanced public spaces, are equitably distributed among all community members, particularly those who have been historically marginalized or disadvantaged.

##### 12.1c Case Studies in Social Equity and Brownfield Redevelopment

To better understand the role of social equity in brownfield redevelopment, let's examine a few case studies.

##### 12.1c(i) Discovery Green, Houston, Texas

The Discovery Green project in Houston, Texas, is an excellent example of a public-private partnership that prioritized social equity in brownfield redevelopment. The project transformed an underused parking lot with a history of industrial occupants into a vibrant city park. 

The Discovery Green Conservancy, a private nonprofit organization, was formed by the Brown Foundation, the Kinder Foundation, the Wortham Foundation, the Houston Endowment, Inc., and the City of Houston. This group envisioned creating a space that would not only revitalize the area but also provide a much-needed green space for the local community.

The project was successful in achieving its goals. Today, Discovery Green is a 12-acre park that hosts hundreds of events each year and serves as a gathering place for the community. The park has also spurred economic development in the surrounding area, with new residential, commercial, and hospitality projects.

This case demonstrates how brownfield redevelopment can be used as a tool for social equity, providing benefits such as green spaces and economic development to the local community.

##### 12.1c(ii) Voluntary Cleanup Program (VCP), Texas

The Voluntary Cleanup Program (VCP) in Texas is another example of a successful brownfield redevelopment initiative that prioritizes social equity. The program, run by the Texas Commission on Environmental Quality (TCEQ), provides administrative, technical, and legal incentives to participants for the investigation, cleanup, and redevelopment of brownfield sites.

The VCP has been successful in encouraging the revitalization of historic sites with an industrial past, providing benefits such as job creation and improved environmental conditions to local communities. The program also ensures that the interests of the local community are taken into account during the redevelopment process, further emphasizing the importance of social equity in brownfield redevelopment.

These case studies highlight the potential of brownfield redevelopment as a tool for social equity. By involving local communities, addressing the needs of sensitive populations, and ensuring the fair distribution of benefits, brownfield redevelopment can contribute to the creation of more equitable and sustainable communities.

### Section: 12.2 Social Equity Strategies:

#### 12.2a Overview of Social Equity Strategies

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, social equity strategies aim to ensure that the benefits of redevelopment are distributed fairly among all community members, particularly those who have been historically marginalized or disadvantaged. These strategies can take various forms, including community engagement, capacity building, and policy interventions.

##### 12.2a(i) Community Engagement

Community engagement is a critical strategy for promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment. This involves involving local residents and stakeholders in the planning and decision-making processes. By giving community members a voice, we can ensure that their needs and concerns are taken into account, and that the redevelopment projects are designed in a way that benefits them.

Community engagement can take various forms, such as public meetings, workshops, surveys, and participatory planning processes. These methods can help to build trust, foster a sense of ownership, and ensure that the redevelopment projects are responsive to the needs of the community.

##### 12.2a(ii) Capacity Building

Capacity building is another important strategy for promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment. This involves providing community members with the skills, knowledge, and resources they need to participate effectively in the redevelopment process.

Capacity building can involve various activities, such as training programs, educational workshops, and technical assistance. These activities can help to empower community members, enabling them to advocate for their interests and contribute to the planning and implementation of redevelopment projects.

##### 12.2a(iii) Policy Interventions

Policy interventions can also play a key role in promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment. This can involve implementing policies that ensure fair distribution of benefits, protect against displacement, and promote inclusive growth.

For example, policies can be put in place to ensure that a certain percentage of the jobs created by the redevelopment are reserved for local residents, particularly those from disadvantaged backgrounds. Similarly, policies can be implemented to ensure that affordable housing is included in the redevelopment projects, to prevent displacement of low-income residents.

In conclusion, promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment requires a multifaceted approach that involves community engagement, capacity building, and policy interventions. By implementing these strategies, we can ensure that the benefits of redevelopment are shared equitably among all community members, and that the redevelopment process contributes to the creation of more inclusive and equitable communities.

#### 12.2b Selection of Social Equity Strategies

The selection of social equity strategies in brownfield redevelopment is a critical step that requires careful consideration. The choice of strategies should be guided by the specific needs and characteristics of the community, as well as the nature of the redevelopment project. 

##### 12.2b(i) Assessing Community Needs and Characteristics

The first step in selecting social equity strategies is to conduct a thorough assessment of the community's needs and characteristics. This can involve collecting data on various aspects of the community, such as its demographic composition, socio-economic status, and history of environmental exposure. 

This information can help to identify the groups that are most vulnerable or disadvantaged, and to understand their specific needs and concerns. For example, a community with a high proportion of low-income residents may require strategies that focus on economic empowerment and job creation. Similarly, a community with a history of environmental exposure may require strategies that prioritize health and safety.

##### 12.2b(ii) Evaluating the Nature of the Redevelopment Project

The nature of the redevelopment project can also influence the selection of social equity strategies. For instance, a project that involves the construction of residential units may require strategies that ensure affordable housing. On the other hand, a project that involves the development of commercial or industrial facilities may require strategies that promote job creation and economic development.

##### 12.2b(iii) Engaging Stakeholders in the Selection Process

Engaging stakeholders in the selection process is another important aspect of selecting social equity strategies. This can involve conducting consultations or workshops with community members, local organizations, and other stakeholders. 

This participatory approach can help to ensure that the selected strategies are responsive to the needs and concerns of the community. It can also help to build trust and foster a sense of ownership among community members, which can be critical for the success of the redevelopment project.

##### 12.2b(iv) Implementing and Monitoring the Selected Strategies

Once the social equity strategies have been selected, they need to be implemented and monitored. This can involve developing a detailed implementation plan, allocating resources, and establishing mechanisms for monitoring and evaluation. 

Monitoring and evaluation are crucial for assessing the effectiveness of the strategies and making necessary adjustments. They can also provide valuable lessons for future redevelopment projects, contributing to the ongoing improvement of social equity practices in brownfield redevelopment.

#### 12.2c Case Studies in Social Equity Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of social equity strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how these strategies can be implemented and the potential benefits they can bring to communities.

##### 12.2c(i) Case Study: The Greening of Brownfields in Chicago

In Chicago, the city government has been proactive in redeveloping brownfields with a focus on social equity. One of the key strategies they have employed is the creation of green spaces in areas that were previously industrial wastelands. These green spaces not only improve the environmental quality of the area but also provide recreational opportunities for the local community.

In addition, the city has also implemented job training programs for local residents, providing them with the skills needed to participate in the redevelopment process. This not only ensures that the benefits of redevelopment are shared with the local community but also helps to address issues of unemployment and economic inequality.

##### 12.2c(ii) Case Study: Affordable Housing in San Francisco

In San Francisco, the redevelopment of the Hunters Point Shipyard, a former naval shipyard, has been guided by a commitment to social equity. One of the key strategies employed in this project is the provision of affordable housing.

The city has mandated that a significant proportion of the new residential units built on the site be set aside for low-income residents. This ensures that the benefits of redevelopment are shared with those who are most in need and helps to address the city's housing crisis.

##### 12.2c(iii) Case Study: Community Engagement in Detroit

In Detroit, the city government has made community engagement a central part of its brownfield redevelopment strategy. The city has established a community advisory committee for each redevelopment project, which includes representatives from local residents, businesses, and community organizations.

These committees are involved in all stages of the redevelopment process, from planning to implementation. This ensures that the needs and concerns of the local community are taken into account and that the benefits of redevelopment are shared equitably.

These case studies illustrate the potential of social equity strategies in brownfield redevelopment. By focusing on the needs and concerns of local communities, these strategies can help to ensure that the benefits of redevelopment are shared equitably and that the process of redevelopment contributes to the creation of more inclusive and sustainable communities.

### Section: 12.3 Social Equity and Community Engagement:

#### 12.3a Role of Community Engagement in Social Equity

Community engagement plays a pivotal role in promoting social equity during brownfield redevelopment. It is a process that involves the active participation of all stakeholders, including marginalized communities, in the decision-making process. This engagement ensures that the benefits of redevelopment are equitably distributed and that the needs and concerns of all community members are addressed.

In marginalized communities, civic engagement can be a powerful tool for promoting social equity. As defined by Merriam-Webster, marginalized refers to those who are put or kept in a powerless or unimportant position within a society or group. Studies have shown that community service and civic engagement can enhance understanding and relationships within diverse communities, fostering a sense of empowerment and participation among marginalized individuals[^1^].

For instance, a study by Hope and Jagers found that black youth who experience racial discrimination are often motivated to participate in politics[^2^]. Similarly, Chan's research on at-risk youth such as African-Americans and Latinos from low-income families in inner-city neighborhoods found that early participation in civic engagement activities can inspire these individuals to set ambitious goals for their futures[^3^].

However, it is important to note that while civic engagement can foster a sense of empowerment and participation among marginalized individuals, it does not automatically translate into long-term changes in social equity. For instance, despite the increase in the number of Hispanics eligible to vote, there is still a lack of active engagement in addressing issues such as immigration[^4^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement can be a powerful tool for promoting social equity. By involving all stakeholders in the decision-making process, it ensures that the benefits of redevelopment are equitably distributed and that the needs and concerns of all community members are addressed. This can be seen in the case studies of Chicago, San Francisco, and Detroit, where community engagement has been a central part of their brownfield redevelopment strategies[^5^].

In conclusion, community engagement plays a crucial role in promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment. It provides a platform for marginalized communities to voice their concerns and participate in the decision-making process, ensuring that the benefits of redevelopment are equitably distributed.

[^1^]: Merriam-Webster. (n.d.). Definition of MARGINALIZE. In Merriam-Webster.com dictionary. Retrieved from https://www.merriam-webster.com/dictionary/marginalize

[^2^]: Hope, E., & Jagers, R. (2014). The Role of Sociopolitical Attitudes and Civic Education in the Civic Engagement of Black Youth. Journal of Research on Adolescence, 24(3), 460-470.

[^3^]: Chan, W. (2015). The Impact of Civic Engagement on At-Risk Youth. Journal of Youth Studies, 18(9), 1238-1252.

[^4^]: New York Times. (2012). Hispanic Voter Registration Lags. Retrieved from https://www.nytimes.com/2012/09/24/us/politics/hispanic-voter-registration-lags-behind-growth-in-population.html

[^5^]: Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment, Chapter 12: Brownfield Redevelopment and Social Equity, Section 12.2c Case Studies in Social Equity Strategies.

#### 12.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Social Equity

Engaging the community in social equity during brownfield redevelopment requires a multi-faceted approach. The following techniques can be employed to ensure active community participation and promote social equity:

1. **Community Meetings and Workshops:** Regular community meetings and workshops provide a platform for all stakeholders to voice their concerns, ideas, and suggestions. These meetings should be inclusive and accessible to all community members, including marginalized individuals. They should also be conducted in a manner that encourages open dialogue and constructive feedback[^5^].

2. **Participatory Mapping:** Participatory mapping is a technique that involves community members in the process of mapping their local environment. This can be particularly useful in brownfield redevelopment as it allows community members to identify areas of concern, potential hazards, and opportunities for redevelopment[^6^].

3. **Community Surveys:** Surveys can be used to gather information about community members' perceptions, needs, and priorities. This data can then be used to inform decision-making processes and ensure that redevelopment plans align with community needs[^7^].

4. **Collaborative Planning:** Collaborative planning involves community members in the planning and decision-making processes. This can help to ensure that redevelopment plans are equitable and meet the needs of all community members[^8^].

5. **Capacity Building:** Capacity building involves providing community members with the skills and knowledge they need to actively participate in redevelopment processes. This can include training in areas such as environmental management, project management, and community organizing[^9^].

6. **Use of Technology:** Technology can be used to facilitate community engagement and participation. For example, online platforms can be used to share information, gather feedback, and facilitate discussions. This can be particularly useful in reaching community members who may not be able to attend in-person meetings or workshops[^10^].

In conclusion, community engagement is a critical component of promoting social equity in brownfield redevelopment. By employing these techniques, it is possible to ensure that all community members have the opportunity to participate in the decision-making process and that the benefits of redevelopment are equitably distributed.

#### 12.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Social Equity

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the importance of community engagement and social equity in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The Think Resilience Initiative

The Think Resilience Initiative by the Post Carbon Institute is an excellent example of how online platforms can be used to engage communities in environmental management[^10^]. This online course provides community members with the knowledge and skills they need to understand and respond to complex environmental challenges. It also encourages community members to actively participate in building community resilience, thereby promoting social equity.

The Think Resilience Initiative demonstrates the effectiveness of using technology to facilitate community engagement and capacity building. It also highlights the importance of providing community members with the knowledge and skills they need to actively participate in environmental management processes[^10^].

##### Case Study 2: Indigenous Education and Community Engagement

Indigenous American communities provide a unique perspective on community engagement and social equity. In these communities, children are included in communal activities from a young age, which helps them develop a sense of belonging and motivates them to engage with their social world[^11^].

This approach to education, known as Learning by Observing and Pitching In (LOPI), supports informal learning and promotes self-sovereignty. It also emphasizes the importance of collaboration and mutual responsibility within the community[^11^].

This case study highlights the importance of engaging all community members, including children, in environmental management processes. It also demonstrates the effectiveness of using participatory methods, such as LOPI, to promote social equity and community engagement[^11^].

##### Case Study 3: Community Engagement in Brownfield Redevelopment in Mexico

A study conducted with children who had immigrated from Indigenous communities in rural Mexico provides valuable insights into community engagement and social equity in brownfield redevelopment[^12^]. 

In this study, children were found to view activities that Westernized culture regards as "chores" as meaningful and productive work. They also emphasized the importance of collaboration and mutual responsibility within the community[^12^].

This case study demonstrates the importance of understanding and respecting cultural differences when engaging communities in brownfield redevelopment. It also highlights the effectiveness of using participatory methods, such as community surveys and collaborative planning, to ensure that redevelopment plans align with community needs and promote social equity[^12^].

In conclusion, these case studies highlight the importance of community engagement and social equity in brownfield redevelopment. They also provide practical examples of how the techniques discussed in the previous section can be applied in real-world scenarios.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields is not just an environmental issue but also a social equity issue. The process of brownfield redevelopment can have significant impacts on local communities, particularly those that are economically disadvantaged or marginalized. Therefore, it is crucial to ensure that these communities are not only protected from potential environmental hazards but also benefit from the redevelopment process. This can be achieved through inclusive planning processes, equitable distribution of resources, and the creation of opportunities for local residents. 

The chapter has highlighted the importance of integrating social equity considerations into brownfield redevelopment projects. It has also underscored the need for comprehensive and inclusive planning processes that take into account the needs and aspirations of all community members. Furthermore, the chapter has emphasized the role of policy and regulatory frameworks in ensuring that brownfield redevelopment contributes to social equity. 

In essence, brownfield redevelopment presents both challenges and opportunities for social equity. While it can lead to displacement and gentrification if not properly managed, it can also contribute to community revitalization, job creation, and improved quality of life if done in an equitable and inclusive manner. Therefore, it is imperative for environmental managers, urban planners, policymakers, and other stakeholders to adopt a social equity lens in brownfield redevelopment.

## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Environmental Justice:

### Introduction

The intersection of brownfield redevelopment and environmental justice is a critical area of study in the field of environmental management. This chapter delves into the complex relationship between these two concepts, exploring how they influence each other and the implications for communities and policy-making.

Brownfields, defined as abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use, often pose significant environmental, legal, and financial challenges. However, they also present opportunities for urban revitalization, economic development, and environmental cleanup. On the other hand, environmental justice is a principle asserting that all people and communities are entitled to equal protection of environmental and public health laws and regulations.

The redevelopment of brownfields has the potential to address environmental justice issues by transforming contaminated sites into productive community assets. However, it can also exacerbate social inequalities if not managed carefully. This chapter will explore these dynamics, providing a comprehensive understanding of the challenges and opportunities at the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and environmental justice.

We will delve into the historical context of brownfields and environmental justice, examine case studies of successful and unsuccessful redevelopment projects, and discuss strategies for ensuring that brownfield redevelopment contributes to environmental justice. This chapter aims to equip readers with the knowledge and tools to navigate the complexities of brownfield redevelopment in a way that promotes environmental justice and sustainable development.

### Section: 13.1 Environmental Justice and Brownfields:

#### 13.1a Definition of Environmental Justice

Environmental justice is a multifaceted concept that encompasses several dimensions of justice, including distributive justice, procedural justice, and recognition justice. Distributive justice refers to the equitable distribution of environmental risks and benefits. This means that no group of people, whether defined by race, class, or any other characteristic, should bear a disproportionate share of the negative environmental consequences resulting from industrial, governmental, and commercial operations or policies. 

Procedural justice, on the other hand, emphasizes the fair and meaningful participation of all people in decision-making processes related to the environment. This involves ensuring that all individuals and communities have an equal opportunity to participate in decisions about activities that may affect their environment or health. 

Recognition justice is about acknowledging and addressing the historical and ongoing oppression and marginalization of certain groups in environmental decision-making processes and outcomes. This dimension of environmental justice recognizes that some communities, particularly those that are economically disadvantaged or racially marginalized, have been disproportionately affected by environmental degradation and pollution.

The United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) defines environmental justice as "the fair treatment and meaningful involvement of all people regardless of race, color, national origin, or income, with respect to the development, implementation, and enforcement of environmental laws, regulations, and policies." This definition encapsulates the three dimensions of environmental justice mentioned above.

Environmental justice is also closely related to the concepts of environmental racism and environmental inequality, which refer to the disproportionate impact of environmental hazards on people of color and low-income communities, respectively. These concepts highlight the systemic nature of environmental injustice and the need for comprehensive solutions that address the root causes of these disparities.

Joan Martinez-Alier's concept of the environmentalism of the poor underscores the importance of including the perspectives and needs of marginalized communities in environmental decision-making. This concept also introduces the idea of "ecological distribution conflicts," which are disputes over access to and control of natural resources and the environmental impacts that result from their use. These conflicts are often rooted in social and economic inequalities, further highlighting the intersection of environmental and social justice issues.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, environmental justice is a critical consideration. Brownfields, which are abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use, often pose significant environmental, legal, and financial challenges. However, they also present opportunities for urban revitalization, economic development, and environmental cleanup. The redevelopment of brownfields has the potential to address environmental justice issues by transforming contaminated sites into productive community assets. However, if not managed carefully, it can also exacerbate social inequalities. Therefore, it is crucial to approach brownfield redevelopment with a commitment to environmental justice, ensuring that the benefits of redevelopment are equitably distributed and that all communities have a meaningful say in the redevelopment process.

#### 13.1b Role of Environmental Justice in Brownfield Redevelopment

Environmental justice plays a crucial role in the redevelopment of brownfields. Brownfields are abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use. Expansion or redevelopment of such sites may be complicated by real or perceived environmental contaminations. These sites are often located in economically disadvantaged or racially marginalized communities, which have historically borne the brunt of environmental degradation and pollution. 

The redevelopment of brownfields presents an opportunity to address environmental justice issues. By cleaning up and redeveloping these sites, we can reduce environmental hazards, improve public health, create new jobs, and enhance the quality of life in these communities. However, it is essential to ensure that the benefits of brownfield redevelopment are equitably distributed and that the affected communities are meaningfully involved in the decision-making processes.

The Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) has established the Brownfields Program to provide grants and technical assistance for brownfield assessment, cleanup, and redevelopment. The program emphasizes the importance of community involvement and public participation. It also encourages the use of cleanup technologies and practices that are sustainable and beneficial to the community.

However, there are also concerns about environmental gentrification, which refers to the process where the cleanup and redevelopment of brownfields lead to an increase in property values, making the area unaffordable for the original residents. This can result in the displacement of low-income residents and exacerbate social inequalities. Therefore, it is crucial to incorporate affordable housing and anti-displacement strategies in brownfield redevelopment plans.

In conclusion, environmental justice is a key consideration in brownfield redevelopment. It requires a careful balance between environmental cleanup, economic development, and social equity. By involving the affected communities in the decision-making processes and ensuring the equitable distribution of benefits, we can make brownfield redevelopment a tool for promoting environmental justice.

#### 13.1c Case Studies in Environmental Justice and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore several case studies that highlight the intersection of environmental justice and brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide real-world examples of the challenges and opportunities that arise in the process of brownfield redevelopment, and how they relate to issues of environmental justice.

##### Case Study 1: The Gowanus Canal, Brooklyn, New York

The Gowanus Canal in Brooklyn, New York, is a notorious example of a brownfield site. For over a century, the canal was a dumping ground for industrial waste, leading to severe contamination. The surrounding community, which is predominantly low-income and racially diverse, has suffered from the environmental and health impacts of this pollution (Bullard, 1993).

In 2010, the canal was designated a Superfund site by the EPA, initiating a long-term cleanup process. The redevelopment plan includes the creation of affordable housing, public parks, and job opportunities. However, the process has been fraught with controversy, with concerns about gentrification and displacement of the existing community (Gleeson and Low, 1999).

This case highlights the importance of community involvement in brownfield redevelopment. The Gowanus Canal cleanup has been marked by strong community activism, with local residents and organizations advocating for a redevelopment plan that addresses their needs and concerns.

##### Case Study 2: The Packard Plant, Detroit, Michigan

The Packard Plant in Detroit, Michigan, once a symbol of the city's industrial might, has been abandoned since the 1950s. The site is heavily contaminated and poses significant environmental and health risks to the surrounding community, which is predominantly African American and economically disadvantaged (Bullard, 1993).

In 2013, a developer purchased the site with plans to transform it into a mixed-use development with housing, retail, and office space. The redevelopment plan includes environmental cleanup, job creation, and community benefits. However, there are concerns about whether the benefits will reach the local community and whether the redevelopment will lead to gentrification and displacement (Harvey, 1996).

This case underscores the need for equitable distribution of the benefits of brownfield redevelopment. It also highlights the potential for brownfield redevelopment to contribute to economic revitalization and job creation in disadvantaged communities.

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the complex interplay between environmental justice and brownfield redevelopment. They underscore the importance of community involvement, equitable benefit distribution, and anti-displacement strategies in the process of brownfield redevelopment. They also highlight the potential for brownfield redevelopment to address environmental injustices and contribute to community revitalization.

### Section: 13.2 Environmental Justice Strategies:

#### 13.2a Overview of Environmental Justice Strategies

Environmental justice strategies are crucial in addressing the disparities in environmental burdens among different communities. These strategies aim to ensure that all communities, regardless of their racial, ethnic, or socioeconomic status, are equally protected from environmental hazards and equally involved in decision-making processes related to their environment. 

There are several key strategies that can be employed to promote environmental justice in the context of brownfield redevelopment:

1. **Community Engagement:** As seen in the case of the Gowanus Canal, community involvement is crucial in brownfield redevelopment. This can be achieved through public meetings, workshops, and other forms of community consultation. It is important to ensure that these processes are inclusive and accessible to all community members, including those who may face barriers due to language, disability, or other factors (Bullard, 1993).

2. **Equitable Planning and Development:** Brownfield redevelopment should aim to benefit the existing community, particularly those who have been most affected by the site's contamination. This can include the creation of affordable housing, job opportunities, and public amenities. It is also important to consider potential negative impacts of redevelopment, such as gentrification and displacement, and to take steps to mitigate these risks (Gleeson and Low, 1999).

3. **Regulatory Enforcement:** The enforcement of environmental laws and regulations is a key aspect of environmental justice. This includes ensuring that companies are held accountable for pollution and contamination, and that cleanup and redevelopment processes adhere to environmental standards. Regulatory agencies, such as the EPA, play a crucial role in this process.

4. **Education and Capacity Building:** Education and capacity building can empower communities to advocate for their environmental rights and to participate effectively in decision-making processes. This can include providing information about environmental risks and rights, training in advocacy and negotiation skills, and support for community-based environmental monitoring and research.

5. **Policy Advocacy:** Advocacy for policies that promote environmental justice is another important strategy. This can include policies that require equitable distribution of environmental benefits and burdens, that prioritize the cleanup and redevelopment of brownfields in disadvantaged communities, and that ensure meaningful community participation in environmental decision-making.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these strategies, providing examples of how they have been applied in practice and discussing their potential benefits and challenges.

#### 13.2b Selection of Environmental Justice Strategies

The selection of environmental justice strategies for brownfield redevelopment should be guided by the specific needs and characteristics of the affected community, as well as the nature of the brownfield site itself. Here are some considerations to guide the selection process:

1. **Community Needs and Characteristics:** The selection of strategies should be informed by a thorough understanding of the community's needs, characteristics, and capacities. This includes factors such as the community's demographic makeup, socioeconomic status, health status, and cultural practices. For instance, communities with a high proportion of non-English speakers may require additional efforts to ensure meaningful participation in decision-making processes (Bullard, 1993).

2. **Site Characteristics:** The nature of the brownfield site, including the type and extent of contamination, the site's size and location, and its potential for redevelopment, should also guide the selection of strategies. For example, sites with severe contamination may require more stringent regulatory enforcement and oversight, while sites with high redevelopment potential may benefit from strategies aimed at equitable development and community benefits (Gleeson and Low, 1999).

3. **Existing Policies and Regulations:** The selection of strategies should also take into account existing policies and regulations at the local, state, and federal levels. For instance, the presence of strong environmental regulations may necessitate a focus on regulatory enforcement, while the absence of such regulations may call for advocacy and policy change efforts.

4. **Partnerships and Collaborations:** The selection of strategies should consider the potential for partnerships and collaborations with other stakeholders, such as local government agencies, non-profit organizations, and private sector entities. Such partnerships can enhance the effectiveness of environmental justice strategies by leveraging resources, expertise, and influence.

5. **Evaluation and Adaptation:** Finally, the selection of strategies should include mechanisms for ongoing evaluation and adaptation. This can help ensure that the strategies are effective in promoting environmental justice and can be adjusted as needed in response to changing circumstances or new information.

In conclusion, the selection of environmental justice strategies for brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires careful consideration of a range of factors. By taking a thoughtful and informed approach, it is possible to select strategies that effectively promote environmental justice and contribute to the creation of healthier, more equitable communities.

#### 13.2c Case Studies in Environmental Justice Strategies

In this section, we will explore several case studies that illustrate the application of environmental justice strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how these strategies can be implemented in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The Hunters Point Shipyard Redevelopment in San Francisco, USA

The Hunters Point Shipyard in San Francisco, once a naval shipyard, is one of the largest brownfield sites in the United States. The site was contaminated with a variety of hazardous substances, including heavy metals, petroleum products, and radioactive materials (Chapple et al., 2011). The redevelopment of the site has been a contentious issue, with community groups advocating for a thorough cleanup and equitable development.

The community's environmental justice strategies included forming a coalition of local residents, community organizations, and environmental groups to advocate for their interests. They also engaged in extensive community outreach and education efforts to raise awareness about the risks associated with the site and the potential benefits of redevelopment. Additionally, they utilized legal strategies, filing lawsuits to challenge the adequacy of the environmental impact report and the cleanup plan (Chapple et al., 2011).

##### Case Study 2: The Vila Autódromo Community in Rio de Janeiro, Brazil

The Vila Autódromo community in Rio de Janeiro was located on a brownfield site that was slated for redevelopment in preparation for the 2016 Olympics. The community, which was largely composed of low-income residents, faced displacement as a result of the redevelopment plans.

The community's environmental justice strategies included forming a residents' association to represent their interests and negotiate with the city government. They also partnered with local universities and non-profit organizations to develop alternative redevelopment plans that would allow the community to remain in place. Despite facing significant challenges, the community was able to secure a number of concessions, including the construction of new housing for some of the displaced residents (Freeman, 2017).

These case studies illustrate the importance of community engagement, advocacy, and partnerships in achieving environmental justice in brownfield redevelopment. They also highlight the need for strategies that are tailored to the specific needs and characteristics of the community and the brownfield site.

### Section: 13.3 Environmental Justice and Community Engagement:

#### 13.3a Role of Community Engagement in Environmental Justice

Community engagement plays a pivotal role in environmental justice, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It is through active community participation that the principles of environmental justice can be upheld, ensuring that all residents, regardless of their socio-economic status, race, or ethnicity, have the opportunity to participate in decisions about a healthy environment in which they live, work, and play (Bullard, 1993).

Community engagement in environmental justice involves a variety of activities, including but not limited to:

1. **Public Participation:** This involves the active involvement of community members in decision-making processes related to environmental planning and brownfield redevelopment. Public participation can take various forms, such as public hearings, community meetings, and participatory mapping exercises. It ensures that the voices of all community members, particularly those who are most vulnerable to environmental hazards, are heard and considered in decision-making processes (Arnstein, 1969).

2. **Community Education and Awareness:** This involves raising awareness among community members about environmental issues, risks, and rights. It can be achieved through various means, such as community workshops, educational campaigns, and the distribution of informational materials. Community education and awareness are crucial for empowering community members to advocate for their environmental rights and to participate effectively in decision-making processes (Bullard, 1993).

3. **Community Advocacy:** This involves community members advocating for their environmental rights and interests. Community advocacy can take various forms, such as lobbying, litigation, and protest. It is a powerful tool for achieving environmental justice, as it enables community members to challenge unjust environmental policies and practices and to demand equitable and sustainable solutions (Bullard, 1993).

4. **Partnerships and Collaborations:** This involves the formation of partnerships and collaborations between community members and other stakeholders, such as government agencies, non-profit organizations, and academic institutions. These partnerships and collaborations can enhance the capacity of community members to engage in environmental decision-making processes and to advocate for their environmental rights and interests (Bullard, 1993).

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement is particularly important. Brownfield sites, due to their history of industrial use and contamination, often pose significant environmental and health risks to surrounding communities. Therefore, it is crucial that community members are actively involved in decision-making processes related to the cleanup and redevelopment of these sites. This not only ensures that the redevelopment process is equitable and responsive to the needs and interests of the community, but also that it contributes to the overall improvement of the local environment and the health and well-being of community members (De Sousa, 2003).

In the next section, we will explore some practical examples of how community engagement can be integrated into brownfield redevelopment processes to achieve environmental justice.

#### References:

- Arnstein, S. R. (1969). A Ladder of Citizen Participation. Journal of the American Institute of Planners, 35(4), 216–224.
- Bullard, R. D. (1993). Confronting Environmental Racism: Voices from the Grassroots. South End Press.
- De Sousa, C. A. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181–198.

#### 13.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Environmental Justice

Engaging the community in environmental justice is a multifaceted process that requires a combination of strategies and techniques. Here are some effective techniques for engaging the community in environmental justice:

1. **Community-Based Participatory Research (CBPR):** CBPR is a research approach that involves community members in all aspects of the research process, from defining the research question to collecting and analyzing data, and disseminating the results (Israel et al., 1998). This approach ensures that the research is relevant to the community and that the results are used to address the community's environmental concerns.

2. **Stakeholder Engagement:** This involves identifying and engaging all individuals, groups, or organizations that have a stake in the environmental issue at hand. Stakeholder engagement can be achieved through various means, such as stakeholder meetings, focus groups, and surveys. It ensures that all perspectives are considered in decision-making processes (Reed, 2008).

3. **Capacity Building:** This involves strengthening the ability of community members to participate effectively in environmental decision-making processes. Capacity building can be achieved through various means, such as training workshops, mentoring, and the provision of resources. It empowers community members to advocate for their environmental rights and to hold decision-makers accountable (Chaskin, 2001).

4. **Collaborative Problem Solving:** This involves working together with community members to identify, analyze, and solve environmental problems. Collaborative problem solving can be achieved through various means, such as brainstorming sessions, consensus-building exercises, and action planning workshops. It fosters a sense of ownership and commitment among community members, thereby enhancing the sustainability of environmental solutions (Gray, 1989).

5. **Digital Engagement:** With the rise of digital technology, online platforms can be used to engage community members in environmental justice. This can include social media campaigns, online surveys, virtual town hall meetings, and digital storytelling. Digital engagement can reach a wider audience and facilitate more inclusive participation (Seltzer and Mahmoudi, 2013).

These techniques are not mutually exclusive and can be used in combination to enhance community engagement in environmental justice. It is important to note that the choice of techniques should be guided by the specific context and needs of the community.

#### 13.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Environmental Justice

In this section, we will delve into two case studies that highlight the importance of community engagement in environmental justice and the successful outcomes that can be achieved when communities are actively involved in environmental decision-making processes.

##### Case Study 1: Community-Based Participatory Research as a Tool for Policy Change

The first case study involves the collaboration between Communities for a Better Environment (CBE), Liberty Hill Foundation, and an academic research team from the University of California, Santa Cruz, Brown University, and Occidental College. The collaboration aimed to review Rule 1402, a regulation included in the South Coast Air Quality Management District that was initially set to reduce public health risk from cancerous and noncancerous emissions by large industries (CBE, n.d.).

The initial maximum individual cancer risk was set at 100 cancer risks per million. However, due to the collaborative efforts of CBE and its partners, the risk was reduced to 25 cancer risks per million six years later, representing a reduction in acceptable risk levels to 75% (CBE, n.d.). This collaboration not only led to a significant reduction in the allowable risk level faced by various communities but also encouraged the California Environmental Protection Agency to broaden their view on issues such as these, to put more emphasis on risk exposure when making new policies.

##### Case Study 2: Linking Exposure Assessment Science With Policy

The second case study is yet to be detailed but will focus on the linkage between exposure assessment science and policy. This case study will explore how scientific research can inform policy decisions and how community engagement can play a crucial role in this process.

In both case studies, community engagement played a pivotal role in achieving environmental justice. The active involvement of community members in the decision-making process not only ensured that their concerns were heard and addressed but also led to more effective and sustainable solutions to environmental problems.

In the next section, we will explore the challenges and barriers to community engagement in environmental justice and discuss strategies for overcoming these challenges.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the chapter has provided a comprehensive overview of brownfield redevelopment and its intersection with environmental justice. We have explored the concept of brownfields, the environmental, social, and economic implications of their existence, and the potential benefits of their redevelopment. We have also delved into the concept of environmental justice, examining how it relates to brownfield redevelopment and the importance of ensuring equitable outcomes in these projects.

The chapter has underscored the importance of integrating environmental justice principles into brownfield redevelopment projects. This is not only to ensure that the benefits of such projects are equitably distributed, but also to prevent the exacerbation of existing environmental inequalities. The chapter has also highlighted the role of various stakeholders, including government agencies, community organizations, and private sector entities, in facilitating successful and equitable brownfield redevelopment.

While brownfield redevelopment presents numerous challenges, it also offers significant opportunities for environmental improvement, economic revitalization, and the promotion of environmental justice. By adopting a holistic and inclusive approach to brownfield redevelopment, we can transform these underutilized and often contaminated sites into productive and sustainable spaces that benefit all members of the community.
