## Chapter: Chapter 14: Brownfield Redevelopment and Green Infrastructure

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure are two critical aspects of environmental management. This chapter will delve into the intersection of these two areas, exploring how they can work together to create sustainable, healthy, and vibrant communities.

Brownfields, or previously developed land that may be contaminated with hazardous substances, pollutants, or contaminants, present both a challenge and an opportunity. The challenge lies in the potential health and environmental risks these sites pose. The opportunity, however, is in their potential for redevelopment. By redeveloping these sites, we can not only mitigate the risks they pose, but also create new opportunities for economic development and community revitalization.

Green infrastructure, on the other hand, refers to the use of natural systems and processes to provide essential services, such as stormwater management, air quality improvement, and climate regulation. By integrating green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment, we can create developments that are not only economically viable, but also environmentally sustainable and beneficial to local communities.

This chapter will explore the principles and practices of brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure, and how they can be integrated to create sustainable, resilient, and livable communities. We will look at case studies of successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have incorporated green infrastructure, and discuss the lessons learned from these projects. We will also explore the challenges and opportunities in integrating green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment, and discuss strategies for overcoming these challenges.

In this chapter, we will not only learn about the technical aspects of brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure, but also about the broader social, economic, and environmental implications of these practices. By the end of this chapter, you will have a deeper understanding of the role of brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure in environmental management, and be equipped with the knowledge and tools to apply these concepts in your own work.

### Section: 14.1 Green Infrastructure and Brownfields

#### 14.1a Definition of Green Infrastructure

Green infrastructure, also referred to as blue-green infrastructure, is a network that utilizes natural elements to address urban and climatic challenges. This approach incorporates various components such as stormwater management, climate adaptation, reduction of heat stress, biodiversity enhancement, food production, air quality improvement, sustainable energy production, clean water provision, and healthy soil maintenance. It also caters to anthropocentric functions such as enhancing the quality of life through recreational activities and providing shade and shelter in urban areas.

Green infrastructure is a subset of "Sustainable and Resilient Infrastructure", as defined in standards like SuRe, the Standard for Sustainable and Resilient Infrastructure. It can also refer to "low-carbon infrastructure" such as renewable energy infrastructure and public transportation systems. Blue-green infrastructure can be a component of "sustainable drainage systems" or "sustainable urban drainage systems" (SuDS or SUDS) designed to manage water quantity and quality, while providing improvements to biodiversity and amenity.

In a more practical sense, green infrastructure refers to a decentralized network of stormwater management practices, which includes green roofs, trees, bioretention, and infiltration. It uses plant or soil systems to restore some of the natural processes needed to manage water and create healthier urban environments.

Green infrastructure serves to provide an ecological framework for the social, economic, and environmental health of the surroundings. More recently, scholars and activists have called for green infrastructure that promotes social inclusion and equality rather than reinforcing pre-existing structures of unequal access to nature-based services.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, green infrastructure can play a crucial role in transforming contaminated and underutilized lands into sustainable and resilient spaces that not only mitigate environmental risks but also contribute to economic development and community revitalization. The integration of green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment can lead to developments that are economically viable, environmentally sustainable, and beneficial to local communities.

#### 14.1b Role of Green Infrastructure in Brownfield Redevelopment

Green infrastructure plays a pivotal role in the redevelopment of brownfields. Brownfields, which are abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use, often pose environmental, legal, and financial burdens. However, they also present opportunities for sustainable redevelopment. Green infrastructure can be a key component in transforming these sites into productive and sustainable landscapes.

The integration of green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment can provide numerous benefits. These include:

1. **Stormwater Management:** Green infrastructure can help manage stormwater runoff, a common issue in brownfield sites. Techniques such as green roofs, bioretention, and permeable pavements can absorb and filter stormwater, reducing runoff and improving water quality. This can help mitigate the risk of flooding and water pollution, which are often associated with brownfield sites.

2. **Climate Adaptation:** Green infrastructure can help brownfield sites adapt to climate change. For instance, tree planting can provide shade and reduce heat stress, while green roofs can reduce the urban heat island effect. These measures can help make brownfield sites more resilient to extreme weather events and changing climate conditions.

3. **Biodiversity Enhancement:** Green infrastructure can help enhance biodiversity in brownfield sites. By creating habitats for various species, it can contribute to urban biodiversity and ecosystem health. This can also provide recreational opportunities for local communities, enhancing the social value of brownfield sites.

4. **Carbon Sequestration:** Green infrastructure can help sequester carbon, contributing to climate change mitigation. For instance, trees and vegetation can absorb carbon dioxide, reducing greenhouse gas emissions. This can also improve air quality, providing health benefits for local communities.

5. **Economic Benefits:** Green infrastructure can provide economic benefits. It can increase property values, attract investment, and create jobs in construction, maintenance, and other related sectors. It can also reduce costs associated with stormwater management and climate adaptation.

In conclusion, green infrastructure can play a crucial role in transforming brownfields into sustainable and resilient landscapes. By integrating green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment, we can address environmental challenges, enhance social benefits, and create economic opportunities. However, it requires careful planning, design, and management to ensure its effectiveness and sustainability. Future research and practice should continue to explore innovative approaches and strategies for integrating green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment.

#### 14.1c Case Studies in Green Infrastructure and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the successful integration of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment. These examples demonstrate the potential of green infrastructure to transform brownfields into sustainable and productive landscapes.

##### The Steel Yard, Providence, Rhode Island

The Steel Yard is a 3.8-acre brownfield site in Providence, Rhode Island, that was once home to the Providence Steel and Iron Company. The site was contaminated with heavy metals and petroleum, posing significant environmental and health risks. However, through a comprehensive redevelopment plan that incorporated green infrastructure, the site was transformed into a vibrant community space.

Green infrastructure played a crucial role in managing stormwater runoff at the site. A 10,000 square foot green roof was installed, along with a rain garden and a permeable pavement system. These features help to absorb and filter stormwater, reducing runoff and improving water quality. The site now serves as a community arts center, providing space for artists and craftsmen, as well as hosting events and educational programs[^1^].

##### The Menomonee Valley, Milwaukee, Wisconsin

The Menomonee Valley in Milwaukee, Wisconsin, was once a bustling industrial hub, home to many factories and mills. However, by the late 20th century, many of these industries had closed down, leaving behind a swath of brownfield sites. The city embarked on an ambitious redevelopment plan, with green infrastructure at its core.

The redevelopment included the creation of three miles of riverwalk, with native plantings to absorb stormwater and provide habitat for wildlife. A 24-acre park was also created, featuring wetlands, prairies, and an urban orchard. The park not only provides recreational space for the community but also serves as a stormwater management system, absorbing runoff and improving water quality. The redevelopment has also attracted new businesses to the area, demonstrating the economic potential of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment[^2^].

These case studies illustrate the transformative potential of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment. By integrating green infrastructure, these sites have not only addressed environmental and health risks but also created vibrant, sustainable spaces that benefit the community and the local economy.

[^1^]: The Steel Yard. (n.d.). Case Study: The Steel Yard. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/green-infrastructure/case-study-steel-yard
[^2^]: Menomonee Valley Partners. (n.d.). Menomonee Valley - From Brownfield to Greenfield. Retrieved from https://www.menomoneevalley-fromthegroundup.com/

### Section: 14.2 Green Infrastructure Strategies:

#### 14.2a Overview of Green Infrastructure Strategies

Green infrastructure strategies are a set of practices that aim to mimic natural processes to manage water and create healthier urban environments. These strategies are often used in brownfield redevelopment to manage stormwater runoff, improve air quality, reduce energy consumption, and sequester carbon. This section will provide an overview of some of the most common green infrastructure strategies, including green roofs, tree planting, bioretention and infiltration, and permeable pavement.

##### Green Roofs

Green roofs are a type of green infrastructure that involves the installation of a layer of vegetation on a building's roof. This vegetation layer, which is planted over a waterproof membrane, can provide a range of benefits, including reducing stormwater runoff, reducing energy usage, and sequestering carbon[^2^].

Green roofs can be categorized into two types: extensive and intensive. Extensive green roofs have a growing media depth ranging from two to six inches, while intensive green roofs have a growing media depth greater than six inches. The type of green roof chosen for a project will depend on the specific goals of the project, as well as the structural capacity of the building[^2^].

##### Tree Planting

Tree planting is another common green infrastructure strategy. Trees can provide a range of ecological, social, and economic benefits, including improving air quality, reducing stormwater runoff, providing habitat for wildlife, and enhancing the aesthetic appeal of an area[^3^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, tree planting can be used to help remediate contaminated soils. Certain tree species are capable of uptaking and storing contaminants in their tissues, a process known as phytoremediation[^4^]. This can help to reduce the concentration of contaminants in the soil, making the site safer for future use.

##### Bioretention and Infiltration

Bioretention and infiltration are green infrastructure strategies that involve the use of vegetation and soil to capture and treat stormwater runoff. Bioretention areas, also known as rain gardens, are shallow depressions planted with vegetation that can absorb and filter stormwater. Infiltration practices, on the other hand, involve directing stormwater to areas where it can infiltrate into the ground, replenishing groundwater supplies[^5^].

Both bioretention and infiltration can be effective strategies for managing stormwater runoff on brownfield sites. They can help to reduce the volume of runoff, improve water quality, and provide habitat for wildlife[^5^].

##### Permeable Pavement

Permeable pavement is a type of pavement that allows water to pass through it, reducing stormwater runoff and recharging groundwater supplies. This can be particularly beneficial on brownfield sites, where impervious surfaces such as buildings and pavement can contribute to high levels of runoff[^6^].

Permeable pavement can be made from a variety of materials, including porous asphalt, pervious concrete, and permeable interlocking pavers. The choice of material will depend on the specific requirements of the project, including the expected traffic load and the desired aesthetic appearance[^6^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these strategies, discussing their benefits, challenges, and potential applications in brownfield redevelopment.

[^2^]: Getter, K. L., & Rowe, D. B. (2006). The role of extensive green roofs in sustainable development. HortScience, 41(5), 1276-1285.
[^3^]: Nowak, D. J., Crane, D. E., & Stevens, J. C. (2006). Air pollution removal by urban trees and shrubs in the United States. Urban Forestry & Urban Greening, 4(3-4), 115-123.
[^4^]: Pilon-Smits, E. (2005). Phytoremediation. Annual Review of Plant Biology, 56, 15-39.
[^5^]: Dietz, M. E. (2007). Low impact development practices: A review of current research and recommendations for future directions. Water, Air, and Soil Pollution, 186(1-4), 351-363.
[^6^]: Brattebo, B. O., & Booth, D. B. (2003). Long-term stormwater quantity and quality performance of permeable pavement systems. Water Research, 37(18), 4369-4376.

#### 14.2b Selection of Green Infrastructure Strategies

The selection of green infrastructure strategies for brownfield redevelopment is a critical step that requires careful consideration of various factors. These factors include the specific environmental challenges of the site, the goals of the redevelopment project, the available budget, and the local climate and ecology[^5^].

##### Green Roofs

The selection of a green roof as a green infrastructure strategy will depend on several factors. First, the structural capacity of the building must be sufficient to support the weight of the green roof, especially in the case of intensive green roofs, which have a greater growing media depth and thus a higher weight[^2^]. Second, the local climate must be suitable for the growth of the selected vegetation. Finally, the cost of installing and maintaining a green roof must be within the project's budget[^6^].

The benefits of green roofs, such as reduced stormwater runoff and energy usage, and carbon sequestration, must be weighed against these considerations. In some cases, incentives or subsidies may be available to offset the cost of green roof installation[^6^].

##### Tree Planting

The selection of tree planting as a green infrastructure strategy will depend on the availability of suitable land, the local climate and ecology, and the specific goals of the project. For example, if the goal is to remediate contaminated soils, tree species capable of phytoremediation should be selected[^4^].

The benefits of tree planting, such as improved air quality, reduced stormwater runoff, and enhanced aesthetic appeal, must be weighed against the cost of tree planting and maintenance, as well as potential challenges such as the need for irrigation or protection from pests[^7^].

##### Bioretention and Infiltration

The selection of bioretention and infiltration as a green infrastructure strategy will depend on the specific environmental challenges of the site, such as the need to manage stormwater runoff or improve water quality. The local soil conditions, including permeability and depth to groundwater, must also be considered[^8^].

The benefits of bioretention and infiltration, such as improved water quality and reduced stormwater runoff, must be weighed against the cost of installation and maintenance, as well as potential challenges such as the need for regular inspection and cleaning[^8^].

In conclusion, the selection of green infrastructure strategies for brownfield redevelopment requires a careful evaluation of the specific conditions and goals of the project. By considering the benefits and challenges of each strategy, as well as the local context, it is possible to select the most effective and sustainable solutions for each site[^5^].

#### 14.2c Case Studies in Green Infrastructure Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the successful implementation of green infrastructure strategies in brownfield redevelopment projects. These case studies will provide practical examples of how green roofs, tree planting, and bioretention and infiltration strategies can be effectively utilized.

##### Case Study 1: Green Roofs in Chicago

The City of Chicago has been a pioneer in the implementation of green roofs as a part of its urban development strategy[^8^]. One notable example is the Chicago City Hall, which installed a 20,300 square foot green roof in 2001 as part of a broader initiative to test different types of green roofs and their benefits[^8^]. The green roof has been successful in reducing stormwater runoff, lowering roof surface temperature, and improving air quality[^8^]. This case study demonstrates the potential of green roofs as a viable green infrastructure strategy for brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 2: Tree Planting in Detroit

The Greening of Detroit is a non-profit organization that has been instrumental in implementing tree planting strategies in Detroit's urban redevelopment efforts[^9^]. Since its inception in 1989, the organization has planted over 130,000 trees in the city[^9^]. The tree planting initiatives have not only improved the city's aesthetic appeal but also contributed to improved air quality, reduced stormwater runoff, and enhanced community engagement[^9^]. This case study underscores the potential of tree planting as a green infrastructure strategy in brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 3: Bioretention and Infiltration in Portland

The City of Portland, Oregon, has been a leader in implementing bioretention and infiltration strategies in its urban development projects[^10^]. The city's Green Streets Program, launched in 2007, has incorporated bioretention facilities into street design to manage stormwater runoff[^10^]. These facilities have been effective in reducing the volume of stormwater entering the sewer system, improving water quality, and enhancing the aesthetic appeal of the city's streets[^10^]. This case study highlights the potential of bioretention and infiltration as a green infrastructure strategy in brownfield redevelopment.

These case studies demonstrate the potential of green infrastructure strategies in brownfield redevelopment. However, it is important to note that the success of these strategies depends on careful planning, consideration of local environmental conditions, and adequate funding[^11^].

[^8^]: City of Chicago. (2001). Chicago City Hall Green Roof. Retrieved from https://www.chicago.gov/city/en/depts/dgs/supp_info/chicago_city_hallgreenroof.html
[^9^]: The Greening of Detroit. (2020). Our Impact. Retrieved from https://www.greeningofdetroit.com/our-impact
[^10^]: City of Portland. (2019). Green Streets Program. Retrieved from https://www.portlandoregon.gov/bes/44422
[^11^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2018). Green Infrastructure in Parks: A Guide to Collaboration, Funding, and Community Engagement. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2018-05/documents/final_gi_parks_guide_5.2.18.pdf

### Section: 14.3 Green Infrastructure and Community Engagement:

#### 14.3a Role of Community Engagement in Green Infrastructure

Community engagement plays a pivotal role in the successful implementation of green infrastructure in brownfield redevelopment. It is through the active participation of the community that sustainable urban infrastructure can be realized and maintained. 

Community engagement in green infrastructure involves the active participation of community members in the planning, design, implementation, and maintenance of green infrastructure projects. This can take the form of public meetings, workshops, and other participatory processes that allow community members to voice their opinions and contribute their ideas[^11^].

The benefits of community engagement in green infrastructure are manifold. Firstly, it ensures that the needs and preferences of the community are taken into account in the design and implementation of green infrastructure projects. This can lead to greater acceptance and use of the infrastructure, and ultimately, to its success[^12^].

Secondly, community engagement can foster a sense of ownership and responsibility among community members. When people feel that they have a stake in the infrastructure, they are more likely to take care of it and ensure its longevity[^13^].

Thirdly, community engagement can facilitate the sharing of knowledge and skills. Community members can learn about the benefits of green infrastructure, how it works, and how to maintain it. This can empower them to take an active role in the stewardship of their environment[^14^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement can be particularly important. Brownfields are often located in urban areas with dense populations, and their redevelopment can have significant impacts on the surrounding community. By involving the community in the planning and implementation of green infrastructure, it is possible to ensure that the redevelopment benefits the community and contributes to the sustainability of the urban environment[^15^].

In the following sections, we will explore some practical examples of how community engagement has contributed to the success of green infrastructure projects in brownfield redevelopment.

[^11^]: Arnstein, S. R. (1969). A Ladder of Citizen Participation. Journal of the American Institute of Planners, 35(4), 216–224. https://doi.org/10.1080/01944366908977225
[^12^]: Choguill, C. L. (1996). A ladder of community participation for underdeveloped countries. Habitat International, 20(3), 431–444. https://doi.org/10.1016/0197-3975(96)00020-9
[^13^]: Pretty, J. N. (1995). Participatory learning for sustainable agriculture. World Development, 23(8), 1247–1263. https://doi.org/10.1016/0305-750X(95)00046-F
[^14^]: Wondolleck, J. M., & Yaffee, S. L. (2000). Making Collaboration Work: Lessons from Innovation in Natural Resource Management. Island Press.
[^15^]: De Sousa, C. A. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181–198. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0169-2046(02)00149-2

#### 14.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Green Infrastructure

Engaging the community in green infrastructure projects requires a strategic approach that is inclusive, transparent, and empowering. Here are some techniques that can be used to facilitate community engagement in green infrastructure:

1. **Public Meetings and Workshops**: These are traditional methods of community engagement that provide a platform for community members to voice their opinions and contribute their ideas[^15^]. They can be used to present plans, gather feedback, and facilitate discussions about green infrastructure projects. 

2. **Surveys and Questionnaires**: These tools can be used to gather information about the community's needs, preferences, and perceptions of green infrastructure. They can be distributed in person, by mail, or online[^16^].

3. **Participatory Mapping**: This technique involves the use of maps to facilitate discussions about the spatial aspects of green infrastructure. Community members can identify areas of concern, suggest locations for green infrastructure, and visualize the potential impacts of different scenarios[^17^].

4. **Citizen Science**: This involves engaging community members in the collection and analysis of data related to green infrastructure. For example, community members could monitor the performance of green infrastructure, such as the amount of stormwater captured by a rain garden[^18^].

5. **Community Design Charrettes**: These are intensive, collaborative design sessions that bring together community members, designers, and other stakeholders to develop solutions for green infrastructure. They can foster creativity, build consensus, and generate a sense of ownership among community members[^19^].

6. **Digital Engagement Tools**: These include websites, social media, online forums, and other digital platforms that can be used to share information, gather feedback, and facilitate discussions about green infrastructure. They can reach a wider audience and provide more flexible and convenient ways for community members to participate[^20^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, these techniques can be adapted to address the specific challenges and opportunities of the site. For example, participatory mapping could be used to identify areas of contamination, citizen science could be used to monitor the remediation process, and community design charrettes could be used to envision the future use of the site[^21^].

By using these techniques, it is possible to engage the community in the planning, design, implementation, and maintenance of green infrastructure in a way that is inclusive, transparent, and empowering. This can lead to more successful and sustainable outcomes for brownfield redevelopment[^22^].

#### 14.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Green Infrastructure

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the successful implementation of green infrastructure through community engagement. These case studies provide practical examples of how communities have leveraged various techniques of engagement to promote sustainable practices and green infrastructure.

1. **The High Line, New York City, USA**: The High Line is a 1.45-mile-long elevated linear park, greenway and rail trail created on a former New York Central Railroad spur on the west side of Manhattan in New York City[^20^]. The project was initiated by community residents who did not want the disused railway line to be demolished. Through public meetings, workshops, and design charrettes, the community was able to voice their ideas and contribute to the design of the park. The High Line is now a testament to the power of community engagement in green infrastructure projects[^21^].

2. **The Green Alley Program, Chicago, USA**: The Green Alley program is an initiative by the City of Chicago to retrofit its vast network of service alleys with sustainable materials and technologies[^22^]. The program uses permeable pavements, reflective surfaces, and other green infrastructure to manage stormwater, reduce heat island effect, and improve overall sustainability. Community members were involved in the planning and implementation process through public meetings and participatory mapping exercises[^23^].

3. **The Sponge City Initiative, China**: The Sponge City Initiative is a nationwide program in China aimed at enhancing urban resilience through the use of green infrastructure[^24^]. The initiative encourages cities to absorb, clean, and use rainwater by incorporating natural ecosystems into urban planning. Community engagement has been a key component of the initiative, with citizens involved in monitoring the performance of green infrastructure and providing feedback through digital engagement tools[^25^].

4. **The Green Belt Movement, Kenya**: The Green Belt Movement is a grassroots non-governmental organization based in Kenya that empowers communities, particularly women, to conserve the environment and improve livelihoods[^26^]. Through community learning and citizen science, the movement has successfully planted over 51 million trees and trained over 30,000 women in forestry, food processing, beekeeping, and other trades that help protect the environment[^27^].

These case studies demonstrate the potential of community engagement in promoting green infrastructure and sustainable practices. They show that when communities are involved in the planning, design, and implementation of green infrastructure projects, the results can be transformative and long-lasting.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields and the integration of green infrastructure is a critical aspect of environmental management. It not only aids in the restoration of degraded lands but also contributes to the creation of sustainable urban environments. The process of brownfield redevelopment involves a series of steps, including site assessment, remediation, and redevelopment, which require careful planning and execution. 

The integration of green infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects provides numerous environmental, social, and economic benefits. It helps in managing stormwater, reducing heat island effect, improving air and water quality, enhancing biodiversity, and providing recreational spaces for communities. Moreover, it contributes to the reduction of greenhouse gas emissions and adaptation to climate change. 

However, the successful implementation of brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure projects requires overcoming various challenges, such as technical, regulatory, financial, and social issues. It necessitates the involvement of multiple stakeholders, including government agencies, private sector, communities, and non-governmental organizations, and the use of innovative approaches and technologies. 

In the future, the role of brownfield redevelopment and green infrastructure in environmental management is expected to increase, given the growing pressures of urbanization, climate change, and resource scarcity. Therefore, it is essential to continue research and practice in this field and to share knowledge and experiences among different countries and regions.

## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Renewable Energy

### Introduction

The fifteenth chapter of "Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment" delves into the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and renewable energy. Brownfields, as we have learned in previous chapters, are abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use. Expansion or redevelopment of such a property may be complicated by real or perceived environmental contaminations.

In this chapter, we will explore how these brownfields can be transformed into sites for renewable energy production. This approach not only addresses the environmental concerns associated with brownfields but also contributes to the global shift towards sustainable energy sources. 

We will discuss the potential of various renewable energy technologies such as solar, wind, and biomass, in the context of brownfield redevelopment. The chapter will also highlight the environmental, economic, and social benefits of this innovative approach, including job creation, increased property values, and reduced greenhouse gas emissions.

Moreover, we will delve into the challenges and barriers that may arise in the process of converting brownfields into renewable energy sites. These may include technical, regulatory, financial, and community acceptance issues. 

This chapter aims to provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment, offering insights into the opportunities and challenges it presents. By the end of this chapter, readers should be able to appreciate the potential of brownfield sites in contributing to a sustainable and energy-efficient future.

### Section: 15.1 Renewable Energy and Brownfields:

#### 15.1a Definition of Renewable Energy

Renewable energy is derived from resources that are naturally replenished on a human timescale. These resources include sunlight, wind, the movement of water (hydro), and geothermal heat. While most renewable energy sources are sustainable, some, such as certain biomass sources, may not be sustainable at current rates of exploitation[^1^].

Renewable energy is often used for electricity generation, heating, and cooling. It is typically deployed in large-scale projects, but it is also well-suited to rural and remote areas and developing countries, where energy is often crucial for human development[^1^]. 

From 2011 to 2021, the share of renewable energy in the global electricity supply increased from 20% to 28%. The use of fossil energy decreased from 68% to 62%, and nuclear from 12% to 10%. The share of hydropower decreased from 16% to 15% while power from sun and wind increased from 2% to 10%. Biomass and geothermal energy grew from 2% to 3%[^1^].

Renewable energy industries are associated with over 10 million jobs globally, with solar photovoltaics being the largest renewable employer[^1^]. In most countries, photovoltaic solar or onshore wind are the cheapest new-build electricity[^1^].

#### 15.1b Renewable Energy and Brownfields

Brownfields, as defined in previous chapters, are abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use. The redevelopment of these properties may be complicated by real or perceived environmental contaminations[^2^].

These brownfields can be transformed into sites for renewable energy production. This approach not only addresses the environmental concerns associated with brownfields but also contributes to the global shift towards sustainable energy sources[^2^].

The potential of various renewable energy technologies such as solar, wind, and biomass, in the context of brownfield redevelopment, is significant. The environmental, economic, and social benefits of this innovative approach include job creation, increased property values, and reduced greenhouse gas emissions[^2^].

However, there are challenges and barriers that may arise in the process of converting brownfields into renewable energy sites. These may include technical, regulatory, financial, and community acceptance issues[^2^].

[^1^]: "Renewable energy." Wikipedia, The Free Encyclopedia. Wikipedia, The Free Encyclopedia, 28 Feb. 2022. Web. 1 Mar. 2022.
[^2^]: "Brownfield Redevelopment and Renewable Energy." Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment. MIT Press, 2022. Print.

#### 15.1b Role of Renewable Energy in Brownfield Redevelopment

The role of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment is multifaceted and offers several benefits. It not only aids in the remediation of contaminated sites but also provides a sustainable solution for energy production[^3^].

##### 15.1b.1 Environmental Benefits

The use of renewable energy technologies in brownfield redevelopment can significantly reduce the environmental impact of these sites. For instance, solar panels installed on brownfields can generate clean energy while reducing greenhouse gas emissions. Similarly, wind turbines can harness wind energy, providing a sustainable energy source and reducing reliance on fossil fuels[^3^].

Moreover, the use of renewable energy technologies can also help in the remediation of contaminated sites. For example, certain types of biomass can absorb heavy metals and other contaminants from the soil, effectively cleaning up the site while producing energy[^3^].

##### 15.1b.2 Economic Benefits

The redevelopment of brownfields into renewable energy sites can also bring about significant economic benefits. It can lead to job creation in the renewable energy sector, which is one of the fastest-growing industries globally[^1^]. 

Moreover, the production of renewable energy can also generate revenue. For instance, electricity generated from solar panels or wind turbines can be sold to the grid, providing a steady income stream[^3^].

##### 15.1b.3 Social Benefits

The redevelopment of brownfields into renewable energy sites can also have social benefits. It can lead to the revitalization of abandoned or underused areas, improving the quality of life for local residents[^3^]. 

Furthermore, the use of renewable energy can also contribute to energy security. By diversifying the energy mix and reducing reliance on imported fossil fuels, renewable energy can enhance energy independence[^3^].

In conclusion, the role of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment is significant. It offers environmental, economic, and social benefits, making it a sustainable and viable solution for the remediation of contaminated sites.

[^1^]: International Renewable Energy Agency (IRENA). (2021). Renewable Energy and Jobs – Annual Review 2021. IRENA, Abu Dhabi.
[^2^]: United States Environmental Protection Agency (EPA). (2021). Brownfields and Land Revitalization. EPA, Washington, D.C.
[^3^]: National Renewable Energy Laboratory (NREL). (2012). Renewable Energy on Contaminated Land: Tools for Local Governments. NREL, Golden, CO.

#### 15.1c Case Studies in Renewable Energy and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore several case studies that highlight the successful integration of renewable energy technologies in brownfield redevelopment. These examples demonstrate the potential for renewable energy to transform contaminated sites into productive and sustainable spaces.

##### 15.1c.1 Brightfields Initiative - Massachusetts, USA

The Brightfields Initiative in Massachusetts, USA, is a prime example of the successful integration of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment[^4^]. The initiative, launched by the Massachusetts Department of Energy Resources, encourages the installation of solar photovoltaic (PV) systems on brownfields and other underutilized sites. 

One notable project under this initiative is the redevelopment of the former landfill in Brockton into a 1.75 MW solar PV facility[^4^]. The project not only transformed a previously contaminated site into a productive space but also generates enough electricity to power approximately 175 homes annually[^4^].

##### 15.1c.2 Steel Winds Project - Lackawanna, New York, USA

The Steel Winds Project in Lackawanna, New York, is another excellent example of renewable energy and brownfield redevelopment. The project involved the transformation of a former steel mill site into a wind farm[^5^]. 

The 35-acre site, which was once heavily contaminated with industrial waste, now hosts eight wind turbines with a combined capacity of 20 MW[^5^]. The project has not only revitalized the site but also provides a significant portion of the city's electricity needs[^5^].

##### 15.1c.3 EKO-Park - Ljubljana, Slovenia

The EKO-Park in Ljubljana, Slovenia, is a unique example of brownfield redevelopment that combines renewable energy production with sustainable urban planning[^6^]. The park, built on a former industrial site, features a biomass power plant, solar PV installations, and a geothermal heating system[^6^].

The EKO-Park not only generates renewable energy but also provides recreational spaces for the local community, demonstrating the potential for brownfield redevelopment to enhance both environmental sustainability and quality of life[^6^].

In conclusion, these case studies illustrate the potential for renewable energy to play a pivotal role in brownfield redevelopment. By transforming contaminated sites into productive spaces, renewable energy technologies can contribute to environmental sustainability, economic growth, and social well-being[^3^].

[^4^]: Massachusetts Department of Energy Resources. (2018). Brightfields Initiative. Retrieved from https://www.mass.gov/service-details/brightfields-initiative
[^5^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (2012). Steel Winds: A Creative Approach to Redeveloping a Brownfield. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/sites/production/files/2015-04/documents/steel-winds.pdf
[^6^]: European Commission. (2016). EKO-Park: A model for sustainable urban living. Retrieved from https://ec.europa.eu/regional_policy/en/projects/slovenia/eko-park-a-model-for-sustainable-urban-living

### Section: 15.2 Renewable Energy Strategies:

#### 15.2a Overview of Renewable Energy Strategies

Renewable energy strategies are crucial for the successful redevelopment of brownfields. These strategies involve the use of renewable energy technologies, such as solar, wind, and geothermal energy, to transform contaminated sites into sustainable and productive spaces. The choice of renewable energy technology depends on various factors, including the geographical location, availability of resources, and the specific needs of the redevelopment project.

##### 15.2a.1 Solar Energy

Solar energy is a popular choice for brownfield redevelopment, especially in regions with high solar irradiance. Solar photovoltaic (PV) systems can be installed on brownfields to generate electricity, as demonstrated by the Brightfields Initiative in Massachusetts, USA[^4^]. The use of solar energy not only reduces reliance on fossil fuels but also contributes to the mitigation of greenhouse gas emissions.

##### 15.2a.2 Wind Energy

Wind energy is another viable option for brownfield redevelopment. Wind turbines can be installed on brownfields to generate electricity, as exemplified by the Steel Winds Project in Lackawanna, New York, USA[^5^]. Wind energy is a clean and renewable source of energy that can significantly reduce the carbon footprint of a redevelopment project.

##### 15.2a.3 Geothermal Energy

Geothermal energy is a sustainable and reliable source of energy that can be used in brownfield redevelopment. This form of energy harnesses the heat from the earth's interior to generate electricity or provide heating and cooling solutions. The EKO-Park in Ljubljana, Slovenia, is a prime example of the use of geothermal energy in brownfield redevelopment[^6^].

##### 15.2a.4 Renewable Energy Modeling Tools

Modeling tools such as SIREN and SWITCH can be used to plan and optimize the use of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment. SIREN, for instance, allows users to explore the location and scale of renewable energy sources to meet a specified electricity demand[^7^]. SWITCH, on the other hand, is an optimal planning model for power systems with large shares of renewable energy[^8^]. These tools can be instrumental in the successful integration of renewable energy technologies in brownfield redevelopment.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these renewable energy strategies and explore how they can be effectively implemented in brownfield redevelopment.

[^4^]: Massachusetts Department of Energy Resources. (n.d.). Brightfields Initiative. Retrieved from [website link]
[^5^]: Steel Winds Project. (n.d.). Retrieved from [website link]
[^6^]: EKO-Park. (n.d.). Retrieved from [website link]
[^7^]: Sustainable Energy Now. (n.d.). SIREN. Retrieved from [website link]
[^8^]: Department of Electrical Engineering, University of Hawaiʻi at Mānoa. (n.d.). SWITCH. Retrieved from [website link]

#### 15.2b Selection of Renewable Energy Strategies

The selection of renewable energy strategies for brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors. These factors include the geographical location, availability of resources, and the specific needs of the redevelopment project. Additionally, the selection process also involves the use of energy modeling tools such as URBS and SIREN.

##### 15.2b.1 URBS

URBS, a linear programming model developed by the Institute for Renewable and Sustainable Energy Systems, Technical University of Munich, Germany, is particularly suited for distributed energy systems (DES). It is an energy modeling framework that attempts to minimize the total discounted cost of the system[^7^].

URBS selects from a set of technologies to meet a predetermined electricity demand. It uses a time resolution of one hour and the spatial resolution is model-defined. The decision variables are the capacities for the production, storage, and transport of electricity and the time scheduling for their operation[^7^].

The software has been used to explore cost-optimal extensions to the European transmission grid using projected wind and solar capacities for 2020[^7^]. It has also been used to explore energy systems spanning Europe, the Middle East, and North Africa (EUMENA) and Indonesia, Malaysia, and Singapore[^7^].

##### 15.2b.2 SIREN

SIREN, or SEN Integrated Renewable Energy Network Toolkit, is another useful tool for selecting renewable energy strategies. Run by Sustainable Energy Now, an NGO based in Perth, Australia, SIREN uses the SAM model (System Advisor Model) from the US National Renewable Energy Laboratory to perform energy calculations[^8^].

SIREN uses hourly datasets to model a given geographic region. Users can use the software to explore the location and scale of renewable energy installations, making it a valuable tool for planning and optimizing the use of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment[^8^].

##### 15.2b.3 Factors Influencing the Selection of Renewable Energy Strategies

The selection of renewable energy strategies is influenced by a variety of factors. These include the geographical location of the brownfield site, the availability of renewable energy resources, the specific needs of the redevelopment project, and the potential environmental and economic benefits of the selected strategies.

For instance, solar energy may be a suitable choice for brownfield sites located in regions with high solar irradiance, while wind energy may be more appropriate for sites located in windy areas. Geothermal energy, on the other hand, may be a viable option for sites located in regions with high geothermal potential[^9^].

In conclusion, the selection of renewable energy strategies for brownfield redevelopment requires a comprehensive understanding of the site's characteristics, the available renewable energy resources, and the potential environmental and economic benefits of the selected strategies. Tools like URBS and SIREN can aid in this process by providing a framework for exploring and optimizing the use of renewable energy technologies.

#### 15.2b.3 SWITCH

Another tool that can be used in the selection of renewable energy strategies for brownfield redevelopment is SWITCH, an acronym for Solar, Wind, Conventional and Hydroelectric generation, and Transmission[^9^]. Developed by the Department of Electrical Engineering, University of Hawaiʻi at Mānoa, SWITCH is an optimal planning model for power systems with large shares of renewable energy[^9^].

SWITCH is a power system model that focuses on the integration of renewables. It identifies which generator and transmission projects to build in order to satisfy electricity demand at the lowest cost over a specified period[^9^]. This makes it a valuable tool for planning and optimizing the use of renewable energy in brownfield redevelopment projects.

The model is written in Pyomo, an optimization components library programmed in Python, and can use either the open source GLPK solver or the commercial CPLEX solver[^9^]. This flexibility allows for a wide range of optimization strategies to be explored, depending on the specific needs and constraints of the redevelopment project.

SWITCH's codebase and datasets are hosted on GitHub, making it easily accessible for researchers and practitioners alike[^9^]. This open-source approach encourages collaboration and the sharing of knowledge, which can lead to more effective and efficient renewable energy strategies for brownfield redevelopment.

In conclusion, the selection of renewable energy strategies for brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors and the use of sophisticated energy modeling tools. Tools like URBS, SIREN, and SWITCH can provide valuable insights and help guide decision-making processes, ultimately leading to more sustainable and cost-effective redevelopment projects.

[^9^]: Department of Electrical Engineering, University of Hawaiʻi at Mānoa. (n.d.). SWITCH. Retrieved from https://github.com/switch-model/switch

### Section: 15.3 Renewable Energy and Community Engagement:

#### 15.3a Role of Community Engagement in Renewable Energy

Community engagement plays a crucial role in the successful implementation of renewable energy projects, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. The concept of energy democracy, which emphasizes the involvement of local communities in the planning and execution of renewable energy projects, is a key aspect of community renewable energy efforts[^10^].

Community-based renewable energy projects are expected to yield a variety of positive social impacts, such as increased acceptance of renewable energy developments, heightened awareness of renewable and sustainable energy technologies, and the adoption of low carbon technologies[^10^]. However, it is important to note that while there is often widespread support for the implementation of such projects, there is often a lack of desire for personal involvement, which can hinder the progress of these initiatives[^10^].

The success of community renewable energy projects is often contingent on institutional support. In the European Union and the United Kingdom, for example, there is a high prevalence of institutional support for community-based renewable energy, which has led to a greater number of successful projects compared to the United States[^10^]. In the U.S., market-based investments in community-based solar and wind projects are more common, with the private sector showing increased interest in community solar energy projects in recent years[^10^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement can be particularly beneficial. By involving local communities in the planning and implementation of renewable energy projects, it is possible to address local needs and concerns, while also promoting the adoption of renewable energy technologies. This can lead to more sustainable and cost-effective redevelopment projects, while also fostering a sense of ownership and pride within the community.

In conclusion, community engagement is a critical component of renewable energy projects, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. By involving local communities in the planning and implementation process, it is possible to foster a sense of ownership and pride, while also promoting the adoption of renewable energy technologies. This can lead to more sustainable and cost-effective redevelopment projects, while also yielding a variety of positive social impacts.

[^10^]: Walker, G., Devine-Wright, P., Hunter, S., High, H., & Evans, B. (2010). Trust and community: Exploring the meanings, contexts and dynamics of community renewable energy. Energy Policy, 38(6), 2655-2663.

#### 15.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Renewable Energy

Engaging the community in renewable energy projects is a multifaceted process that requires a strategic approach. Here are some techniques that can be employed to foster community engagement in renewable energy projects:

1. **Education and Awareness Campaigns**: One of the most effective ways to engage the community in renewable energy projects is through education and awareness campaigns[^11^]. These campaigns can be used to inform the community about the benefits of renewable energy, the importance of energy democracy, and the potential social impacts of community-based renewable energy projects[^11^]. 

2. **Public Meetings and Workshops**: Public meetings and workshops provide a platform for community members to voice their opinions, ask questions, and learn more about renewable energy projects[^12^]. These events can also be used to gather feedback and suggestions from the community, which can be invaluable in the planning and implementation stages of a project[^12^].

3. **Participatory Planning Processes**: Participatory planning processes involve the community in the decision-making process, fostering a sense of ownership and commitment to the project[^13^]. This can be particularly effective in overcoming the lack of desire for personal involvement often observed in community renewable energy projects[^13^].

4. **Community Shares and Co-operatives**: Community shares and co-operatives can be used to finance renewable energy projects, providing community members with a financial stake in the project[^14^]. This not only helps to raise funds for the project, but also fosters a sense of ownership and commitment among community members[^14^].

5. **Partnerships with Local Institutions**: Partnerships with local institutions, such as schools, universities, and local government agencies, can provide valuable support for renewable energy projects[^15^]. These partnerships can help to secure funding, provide technical expertise, and foster community support[^15^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, these techniques can be particularly effective. By engaging the community in the planning and implementation of renewable energy projects, it is possible to address local needs and concerns, promote the adoption of renewable energy technologies, and foster a sense of ownership and commitment to the project. This can lead to more sustainable and cost-effective redevelopment projects, while also contributing to the broader goals of energy democracy and community resilience[^16^].

[^11^]: Post Carbon Institute. (2018). Think Resilience. Retrieved from https://www.postcarbon.org/program/think-resilience/
[^12^]: Walker, G., Devine-Wright, P., Hunter, S., High, H., & Evans, B. (2010). Trust and community: Exploring the meanings, contexts and dynamics of community renewable energy. Energy Policy, 38(6), 2655-2663.
[^13^]: Seyfang, G., Park, J. J., & Smith, A. (2013). A thousand flowers blooming? An examination of community energy in the UK. Energy Policy, 61, 977-989.
[^14^]: Walker, G., & Devine-Wright, P. (2008). Community renewable energy: What should it mean? Energy Policy, 36(2), 497-500.
[^15^]: Warren, C. R., & McFadyen, M. (2010). Does community ownership affect public attitudes to wind energy? A case study from south-west Scotland. Land Use Policy, 27(2), 204-213.
[^16^]: Seyfang, G., Hielscher, S., Hargreaves, T., Martiskainen, M., & Smith, A. (2014). A grassroots sustainable energy niche? Reflections on community energy in the UK. Environmental Innovation and Societal Transitions, 13, 21-44.

#### 15.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Renewable Energy

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the role of community engagement in renewable energy projects. These case studies will provide practical examples of the techniques discussed in the previous section.

1. **The Kodiak Island, Alaska**: The Kodiak Island in Alaska is a prime example of a successful community-based renewable energy project[^16^]. The Kodiak Electric Association (KEA), a member-owned rural electric cooperative, set a goal in 2007 to produce 95% of the island's energy from renewable sources by 2020[^16^]. Through a combination of wind and hydroelectric power, the KEA achieved this goal five years ahead of schedule[^16^]. The success of this project can be attributed to the active involvement of the community, which was achieved through regular public meetings, education and awareness campaigns, and a participatory planning process[^16^].

2. **The Kodiak Island, Alaska**: The Kodiak Island in Alaska is a prime example of a successful community-based renewable energy project[^16^]. The Kodiak Electric Association (KEA), a member-owned rural electric cooperative, set a goal in 2007 to produce 95% of the island's energy from renewable sources by 2020[^16^]. Through a combination of wind and hydroelectric power, the KEA achieved this goal five years ahead of schedule[^16^]. The success of this project can be attributed to the active involvement of the community, which was achieved through regular public meetings, education and awareness campaigns, and a participatory planning process[^16^].

3. **The Findhorn Ecovillage, Scotland**: The Findhorn Ecovillage in Scotland is another example of a successful community-based renewable energy project[^17^]. The ecovillage is powered by four wind turbines, which were financed through a community shares scheme[^17^]. The project has not only reduced the community's carbon footprint but also provides a regular income for the community[^17^]. The success of this project can be attributed to the community's financial stake in the project, which fostered a sense of ownership and commitment[^17^].

4. **The University of California, Davis**: The University of California, Davis, has partnered with the local community to develop a solar power project[^18^]. The project, which is one of the largest of its kind in the United States, was made possible through a partnership with the local utility company[^18^]. The university's commitment to renewable energy and its partnership with the local community serve as a model for other institutions looking to develop similar projects[^18^].

These case studies highlight the importance of community engagement in the success of renewable energy projects. They also underscore the need for institutional support, whether it be from local government agencies, universities, or utility companies. As we move towards a more sustainable future, it is crucial that we continue to foster community engagement and institutional support for renewable energy projects.

### Conclusion

Throughout this chapter, we have explored the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and renewable energy. We have seen how these two areas, often considered separately, can be integrated to create sustainable solutions for environmental management. Brownfield sites, once seen as liabilities, can be transformed into assets that contribute to a community's renewable energy portfolio. 

We have discussed the potential of brownfield sites to host renewable energy facilities, such as solar and wind farms, and the benefits this can bring. These include reducing greenhouse gas emissions, creating jobs, and revitalizing communities. We have also examined the challenges involved in this process, such as site contamination issues and the need for regulatory approvals. 

In conclusion, brownfield redevelopment for renewable energy is a promising area of environmental management. It offers a way to turn neglected and contaminated sites into productive and sustainable assets. However, it requires careful planning, collaboration among various stakeholders, and adherence to environmental regulations. As we move towards a more sustainable future, the role of brownfield redevelopment in our energy strategy will likely become increasingly important.
