## Chapter: Chapter 16: Brownfield Redevelopment and Adaptive Reuse:

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment and adaptive reuse are two critical aspects of environmental management. They represent a sustainable approach to land use and urban development, transforming neglected, abandoned, or underutilized sites into productive community assets. This chapter will delve into the intricacies of these processes, providing a comprehensive understanding of their role in environmental management.

Brownfield sites, often former industrial or commercial properties, may have real or perceived contamination that hinders their redevelopment. The process of brownfield redevelopment involves assessing and remediating these sites, preparing them for new uses. This not only mitigates environmental risks but also stimulates economic growth and revitalizes communities.

Adaptive reuse, on the other hand, is the practice of repurposing buildings for uses other than those initially intended. This strategy can breathe new life into historic structures, conserve resources, and contribute to community character and vitality. It is a testament to the concept of sustainability, demonstrating that the greenest building is often the one already built.

This chapter will explore the principles, practices, and benefits of brownfield redevelopment and adaptive reuse. It will also discuss the challenges involved and strategies to overcome them. By the end of this chapter, readers should have a solid understanding of these processes and their significance in environmental management.

### Section: 16.1 Adaptive Reuse and Brownfields:

#### 16.1a Definition of Adaptive Reuse

Adaptive reuse refers to the process of repurposing buildings or sites for uses other than those initially intended while retaining their historic or architectural value. This practice is often applied to brownfield sites, which are abandoned, idled, or underused industrial and commercial properties where expansion or redevelopment is complicated by real or perceived environmental contamination.

The concept of adaptive reuse is akin to the software industry's practice of code reuse, where existing code is utilized to perform common operations, thereby increasing productivity and improving quality. Similarly, adaptive reuse in the context of brownfield redevelopment involves leveraging existing structures and sites, thereby conserving resources, reducing waste, and contributing to sustainable urban development.

The process of adaptive reuse can be likened to using a software library, where authors of new programs can use the code in a software library to perform common tasks, instead of writing new code. In the same vein, adaptive reuse allows developers to utilize existing structures or sites, instead of constructing new ones, thereby saving time, resources, and reducing environmental impact.

However, just as software reuse implementation is dependent on the context in which it is implemented, adaptive reuse also depends on various factors such as the condition of the existing structure, the potential for contamination, the feasibility of the proposed new use, and the regulatory and economic environment.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the process of adaptive reuse in the context of brownfield redevelopment, discussing its benefits, challenges, and strategies for successful implementation.

#### 16.1b Role of Adaptive Reuse in Brownfield Redevelopment

Adaptive reuse plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment, offering a sustainable and cost-effective solution to the problem of abandoned or underused industrial and commercial properties. This section will explore the role of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment, focusing on its environmental, economic, and social benefits.

##### Environmental Benefits

Adaptive reuse contributes significantly to environmental sustainability. By repurposing existing structures, it reduces the need for new construction materials, thereby conserving natural resources and reducing waste. This is particularly important in the context of brownfield redevelopment, where the presence of hazardous substances, pollutants, or contaminants can complicate the construction process and pose risks to the environment.

Moreover, adaptive reuse can help mitigate the environmental impact of brownfields. By transforming these sites into productive uses, it can reduce the amount of pollutants that can contaminate the soil and water around the abandoned building. This is particularly beneficial when repurposing buildings which are near schools, residential neighborhoods, or other workplaces by mitigating occupational hazards from such contaminants commonly found in construction.

##### Economic Benefits

From an economic perspective, adaptive reuse can offer significant cost savings. The cost of producing new materials and the services that come with manufacturing these products are much more costly than repurposing existing buildings. Moreover, adaptive reuse can also lead to indirect cost savings by reducing the time and resources required for new construction.

In addition, adaptive reuse can contribute to economic revitalization by transforming brownfields into productive uses. This can stimulate local economies, create jobs, and increase property values, thereby benefiting both the local community and the broader economy.

##### Social Benefits

Adaptive reuse can also have significant social benefits. By transforming brownfields into productive uses, it can improve the quality of life in surrounding communities. This can be particularly beneficial in urban areas, where brownfields often occupy prime locations that could be used for housing, commercial, or recreational purposes.

Furthermore, adaptive reuse can help preserve the architectural and historical value of existing structures, contributing to the cultural heritage of a community. This can enhance the sense of place and identity in a community, fostering social cohesion and community pride.

In conclusion, adaptive reuse plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment, offering a sustainable, cost-effective, and socially beneficial solution to the problem of abandoned or underused industrial and commercial properties. However, its successful implementation requires careful planning and consideration of various factors, including the condition of the existing structure, the potential for contamination, the feasibility of the proposed new use, and the regulatory and economic environment.

#### 16.1c Case Studies in Adaptive Reuse and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore several case studies that highlight the successful implementation of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the principles and benefits discussed in the previous sections have been applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: The High Line, New York City

The High Line in New York City is a prime example of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. Once a derelict elevated railway line, the High Line was transformed into a 1.45-mile-long public park, which opened in 2009. The project involved the remediation of the brownfield site, the preservation of the existing railway infrastructure, and the creation of a new public space that includes walkways, seating areas, and a variety of plantings.

The High Line project has brought significant environmental, economic, and social benefits. It has created a green space in an urban environment, contributing to biodiversity and improving air quality. Economically, the High Line has stimulated local economic development, with property values in the surrounding area increasing significantly. Socially, it has created a new public space for residents and visitors to enjoy, promoting community cohesion and improving quality of life.

##### Case Study 2: The Brewery, Los Angeles

The Brewery in Los Angeles is another example of successful adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. Once the Pabst Blue Ribbon brewery, the site was transformed into a live-work complex for artists in the 1980s. The project involved the remediation of the brownfield site and the conversion of the existing industrial buildings into residential and studio spaces.

The Brewery has brought significant environmental benefits by reusing the existing buildings and reducing the need for new construction materials. Economically, it has provided affordable housing and workspace for artists, contributing to the local creative economy. Socially, it has created a vibrant community of artists, contributing to the cultural life of the city.

These case studies demonstrate the potential of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. By repurposing existing structures and transforming brownfield sites into productive uses, adaptive reuse can bring significant environmental, economic, and social benefits.

### Section: 16.2 Adaptive Reuse Strategies:

#### 16.2a Overview of Adaptive Reuse Strategies

Adaptive reuse strategies are a critical component of brownfield redevelopment. These strategies involve repurposing existing structures or sites for new uses, thereby reducing the need for new construction materials and minimizing the environmental impact. This section will provide an overview of various adaptive reuse strategies, drawing parallels from the software industry's code reuse strategies.

##### Software Libraries as a Model

In the software industry, a common example of code reuse is the use of software libraries. These libraries contain pre-written code that can be used to perform common operations, thereby saving time and resources. Similarly, in brownfield redevelopment, existing structures can be seen as 'libraries' of materials and spaces that can be reused in new ways. For instance, an old factory building might be repurposed as a residential complex, or a disused railway line might be transformed into a public park, as seen in the case of the High Line in New York City.

##### Design Patterns as a Model

Another example from the software industry is the use of design patterns. These are general solutions to recurring problems that can be modified to fit specific needs. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, design patterns might involve common strategies for site remediation, building conversion, or community engagement. For instance, a common design pattern might involve the remediation of contaminated soil, the preservation of historic building features, and the creation of new public spaces, as seen in the case of the Brewery in Los Angeles.

##### Systematic Reuse

Systematic reuse is a strategy that aims to increase productivity and improve quality by reusing components in a systematic way. In the context of brownfield redevelopment, this might involve the systematic reuse of building materials, infrastructure, or land. For instance, bricks from a demolished building might be reused in new construction, or a disused industrial site might be repurposed as a renewable energy facility.

In conclusion, adaptive reuse strategies in brownfield redevelopment can draw inspiration from the software industry's code reuse strategies. By reusing existing structures and materials in new ways, these strategies can help to minimize environmental impact, stimulate economic development, and improve community cohesion. However, as with software reuse, the successful implementation of adaptive reuse strategies in brownfield redevelopment depends on the specific context and requires careful planning and management.

#### 16.2b Selection of Adaptive Reuse Strategies

The selection of adaptive reuse strategies in brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors. These factors include the condition of the existing structures, the extent of environmental contamination, the needs of the local community, and the potential for economic development. 

##### Condition of Existing Structures

The condition of existing structures is a critical factor in the selection of adaptive reuse strategies. Structures that are in good condition can be repurposed with minimal modifications, thereby saving resources and reducing environmental impact. For instance, an old warehouse in good condition might be converted into a retail space or a community center. On the other hand, structures that are in poor condition might require extensive renovations or even demolition, which can increase costs and environmental impact. 

##### Extent of Environmental Contamination

The extent of environmental contamination is another important factor. Sites with severe contamination might require extensive remediation before they can be reused, which can be costly and time-consuming. In such cases, it might be more feasible to opt for strategies that involve minimal disturbance of the contaminated soil, such as the construction of parking lots or green spaces. On the other hand, sites with minimal contamination might be suitable for more intensive uses, such as residential or commercial development.

##### Community Needs and Economic Development

The needs of the local community and the potential for economic development are also crucial factors. Adaptive reuse strategies should aim to meet the needs of the local community, whether that involves providing affordable housing, creating job opportunities, or enhancing public spaces. At the same time, these strategies should also aim to stimulate economic development, as this can help to offset the costs of redevelopment and ensure the long-term sustainability of the project.

##### Adaptive Reuse and Software Reuse

Drawing parallels from the software industry, the selection of adaptive reuse strategies can be likened to the selection of software reuse strategies. Just as software developers must consider factors such as the functionality of existing code, the requirements of the project, and the potential for future reuse when selecting software reuse strategies, so too must urban planners and developers consider the condition of existing structures, the extent of environmental contamination, the needs of the local community, and the potential for economic development when selecting adaptive reuse strategies.

In conclusion, the selection of adaptive reuse strategies is a complex process that requires a holistic approach. By considering a range of factors and drawing on lessons from other fields such as software development, it is possible to select strategies that not only minimize environmental impact but also meet the needs of the local community and stimulate economic development.

#### 16.2c Case Studies in Adaptive Reuse Strategies

In this section, we will explore several case studies that illustrate the application of adaptive reuse strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the factors discussed in the previous section - condition of existing structures, extent of environmental contamination, community needs, and economic development - can influence the selection and implementation of adaptive reuse strategies.

##### Case Study 1: The High Line, New York City

The High Line in New York City is a prime example of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. Originally a freight rail line elevated above the streets of Manhattan, the High Line was abandoned in the 1980s and slated for demolition. However, a community-led effort to preserve and repurpose the structure resulted in its transformation into a public park.

The condition of the existing structure was a key factor in this project. Despite years of neglect, the elevated rail line was structurally sound and could be repurposed with minimal modifications. The extent of environmental contamination was also relatively low, as the site had been largely unused since the cessation of rail operations.

The High Line project has met the needs of the local community by providing a unique public space, and it has stimulated economic development by attracting tourists and encouraging investment in the surrounding area. This case study demonstrates how adaptive reuse can transform a derelict structure into a valuable community asset.

##### Case Study 2: The Lachine Canal, Montreal

The Lachine Canal in Montreal is another example of successful brownfield redevelopment through adaptive reuse. Once a major industrial corridor, the canal and its surrounding lands fell into disuse in the mid-20th century. The site was heavily contaminated due to its industrial past.

The redevelopment of the Lachine Canal involved extensive environmental remediation to address the contamination. The existing structures, many of which were historic buildings, were repurposed into residential, commercial, and recreational spaces. The project has met the needs of the local community by providing housing and public spaces, and it has stimulated economic development by attracting new businesses to the area.

These case studies illustrate the potential of adaptive reuse strategies in brownfield redevelopment. By considering the condition of existing structures, the extent of environmental contamination, the needs of the local community, and the potential for economic development, it is possible to transform brownfield sites into vibrant, sustainable communities.

### Section: 16.3 Adaptive Reuse and Community Engagement:

#### 16.3a Role of Community Engagement in Adaptive Reuse

Community engagement plays a crucial role in the adaptive reuse of brownfields. It is a process that involves the active participation of community members in the planning, design, and implementation of redevelopment projects. This engagement can take various forms, from public meetings and focus groups to online platforms and crowd-funded models.

The concept of open-source architecture provides a useful framework for understanding the role of community engagement in adaptive reuse. Open-source architecture is a democratic approach to design that values the input of multiple users at every stage of a project. It recognizes the importance of community participation in shaping the built environment and harnesses the power of network effects to scale systems effectively.

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, open-source architecture can facilitate a more inclusive and responsive approach to adaptive reuse. By involving community members in the decision-making process, developers can ensure that the reuse of brownfields meets the needs and aspirations of the local community. This can result in more successful and sustainable redevelopment projects that enhance the quality of life for residents and contribute to the economic vitality of the area.

However, community engagement in adaptive reuse also presents certain challenges. Traditional engagement programmes often rely on blunt tools such as focus groups, which can result in a lack of representation and input. Moreover, the open-source nature of community engagement can lead to organizational drawbacks, such as the forking of projects, abandoned projects, and the emergence of cliques. Therefore, it is important to establish clear guidelines and open standards of collaboration to manage these challenges effectively.

#### 16.3b Case Study: The High Line, New York City

The High Line in New York City provides a compelling example of the role of community engagement in adaptive reuse. This project involved the transformation of an abandoned freight rail line into a public park, a process that was driven by a community-led effort to preserve and repurpose the structure.

The success of the High Line project can be attributed in large part to the active involvement of the local community. Through public meetings, online platforms, and other forms of engagement, community members were able to voice their ideas and concerns, influence the design of the park, and contribute to its ongoing management and maintenance. This case study demonstrates the potential of community engagement to facilitate adaptive reuse that is responsive to the needs and aspirations of the local community.

In conclusion, community engagement is a critical component of adaptive reuse in brownfield redevelopment. By involving community members in the planning, design, and implementation of redevelopment projects, developers can ensure that the reuse of brownfields is sustainable, inclusive, and beneficial to the local community.

#### 16.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Adaptive Reuse

Engaging the community in adaptive reuse projects is a multifaceted process that requires a strategic approach. Here are some techniques that can be used to foster community engagement in adaptive reuse:

1. **Public Meetings and Workshops:** These are traditional methods of community engagement that provide a platform for residents to voice their opinions and concerns. They can be used to present redevelopment plans, gather feedback, and facilitate discussions about the project.

2. **Online Platforms:** With the rise of digital technology, online platforms have become an effective tool for community engagement. They can be used to share information, solicit feedback, and facilitate discussions in a more accessible and convenient manner. Examples include project websites, social media pages, and online forums.

3. **Crowdfunding Models:** Crowdfunding models can be used to raise funds for redevelopment projects and engage the community in the process. They can foster a sense of ownership and investment in the project among community members.

4. **Participatory Design Workshops:** These workshops involve community members in the design process, allowing them to contribute ideas and feedback directly. This can result in designs that better reflect the needs and aspirations of the community.

5. **Surveys and Questionnaires:** These tools can be used to gather quantitative data on community preferences and opinions. They can provide valuable insights into the community's needs and aspirations, which can inform the planning and design process.

6. **Community Advisory Committees:** These committees consist of community members who provide ongoing input and feedback on the project. They can serve as a liaison between the project team and the community, facilitating communication and collaboration.

7. **Open-Source Architecture:** As mentioned in the previous section, open-source architecture is a democratic approach to design that values the input of multiple users. It can be used to engage the community in the design process and foster a sense of ownership and investment in the project.

These techniques can be used individually or in combination, depending on the specific context and objectives of the project. However, it is important to note that community engagement is not a one-size-fits-all approach. The techniques used should be tailored to the specific needs and characteristics of the community, and should be implemented in a transparent and inclusive manner.

#### 16.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Adaptive Reuse

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the role of community engagement in adaptive reuse and brownfield redevelopment projects. These case studies will provide practical insights into the techniques discussed in the previous section.

##### Case Study 1: The High Line, New York City

The High Line in New York City is a prime example of successful adaptive reuse facilitated by strong community engagement. This 1.45-mile-long elevated linear park was created on a former New York Central Railroad spur on the west side of Manhattan. The project was initiated by two local residents who formed the non-profit organization 'Friends of the High Line' to advocate for the High Line's preservation and reuse as a public park[^1^].

Community engagement played a crucial role in the project's success. Public meetings were held to gather feedback and ideas, and a design competition was organized to solicit innovative design proposals. The project also utilized online platforms to share information and updates, and to facilitate discussions among community members[^1^].

##### Case Study 2: The Lachine Canal, Montreal

The Lachine Canal in Montreal is another example of successful brownfield redevelopment and adaptive reuse. The canal, which had been abandoned for several decades, was redeveloped into a multi-use urban park that includes recreational facilities, residential areas, and commercial spaces[^2^].

Community engagement was a key component of the redevelopment process. A community advisory committee was established to provide ongoing input and feedback. Participatory design workshops were held to involve community members in the design process. Surveys and questionnaires were also used to gather data on community preferences and opinions[^2^].

These case studies demonstrate the importance of community engagement in adaptive reuse and brownfield redevelopment projects. They show how various techniques can be used to involve community members in the process, and how this involvement can contribute to the success of the project.

[^1^]: Friends of the High Line. (n.d.). The High Line. Retrieved from https://www.thehighline.org/
[^2^]: Parks Canada. (n.d.). Lachine Canal National Historic Site. Retrieved from https://www.pc.gc.ca/en/lhn-nhs/qc/canallachine

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields and their adaptive reuse is a complex but rewarding process. It involves a comprehensive understanding of environmental management practices, regulatory frameworks, and innovative remediation technologies. The transformation of brownfields into productive and sustainable land uses not only mitigates environmental hazards but also promotes economic growth and community development. 

The adaptive reuse of brownfields is a testament to the power of innovation and resilience in the face of environmental challenges. It underscores the importance of sustainable practices in urban planning and development. The process of brownfield redevelopment is a practical example of how environmental management can be integrated into urban development strategies to create sustainable and resilient cities.

The journey from identifying and assessing a brownfield site to its successful redevelopment and adaptive reuse is a multidisciplinary endeavor. It requires the collaboration of environmental scientists, urban planners, policymakers, and community stakeholders. This collaborative approach ensures that the redevelopment process is not only environmentally sound but also socially equitable and economically viable.

## Chapter: Chapter 17: Brownfield Redevelopment and Historic Preservation

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation are two critical aspects of environmental management that often intersect. Brownfields, defined as abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use, often exist within historic districts or contain historic structures. Their redevelopment can contribute to economic revitalization, environmental cleanup, and the preservation of historic and cultural resources. However, the process is fraught with challenges, including contamination issues, regulatory complexities, and the need to balance preservation with modernization.

This chapter delves into the intricate relationship between brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation. It explores the opportunities and challenges that arise when these two areas intersect, and provides practical guidance for managing these complex projects. The chapter also highlights case studies that illustrate successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have also preserved historic structures and contributed to community revitalization.

While the chapter does not provide a one-size-fits-all solution, it offers a comprehensive understanding of the issues at hand and equips readers with the knowledge and tools to navigate the complexities of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation. Whether you are an environmental manager, a city planner, a developer, or a community advocate, this chapter will provide valuable insights into this important aspect of environmental management.

### Section: 17.1 Historic Preservation and Brownfields

#### 17.1a Definition of Historic Preservation

Historic preservation, also known as built heritage preservation or built heritage conservation, is a practice that aims to protect, conserve, and preserve buildings, objects, landscapes, or other artifacts of historical significance. This concept, which gained popularity in the twentieth century, posits that cities, as products of centuries of development, have a responsibility to protect their patrimonial legacy. The term specifically refers to the preservation of the built environment, excluding, for example, the preservation of primeval forests or wilderness[^1^].

Historic preservation is a professional field with various practice areas, including regulatory compliance, architecture and construction, historic sites/museums, advocacy, and downtown revitalization/rejuvenation. Each of these areas requires a different set of skills, knowledge, and abilities[^1^].

In the United States, approximately 90% of professional, paid practice in historic preservation is in the area of regulatory compliance. This is driven by laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels. Key federal regulations include the National Historic Preservation Act of 1966, Section 106 (54 USC 306108, formerly known as 36 CFR 800), National Register of Historic Places (54 USC 302101-302108), and the Secretary of the Interior's Standards (36 CFR 67). Many states have laws that reference these federal regulations or create parallel regulations. At the local level, preservation laws and regulations are known as "preservation ordinances" and require private property owners to seek a "certificate of appropriateness" when making modifications to existing buildings listed in a local historical register[^1^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, historic preservation plays a crucial role. Brownfields, often located within historic districts or containing historic structures, present unique opportunities for economic revitalization, environmental cleanup, and the preservation of historic and cultural resources. However, the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation also presents challenges, including contamination issues, regulatory complexities, and the need to balance preservation with modernization[^2^].

[^1^]: (source: related context)
[^2^]: (source: last textbook section content)

#### 17.1b Role of Historic Preservation in Brownfield Redevelopment

Historic preservation plays a significant role in brownfield redevelopment. This role is multifaceted and includes aspects such as environmental sustainability, economic viability, and community revitalization.

##### Environmental Sustainability

Historic preservation contributes to environmental sustainability in brownfield redevelopment in several ways. Firstly, it promotes the adaptive reuse of existing buildings, which is a form of land recycling. This practice is more environmentally sustainable than constructing new buildings as it reduces the need for new materials and decreases site disruptions[^2^]. 

Secondly, historic preservation can help mitigate the environmental risks associated with brownfields. Many brownfields are contaminated with hazardous substances, pollutants, or contaminants[^3^]. By preserving and rehabilitating these sites, we can reduce the risk of these harmful substances spreading to the surrounding environment.

##### Economic Viability

Historic preservation can also enhance the economic viability of brownfield redevelopment. The adaptive reuse of existing buildings can lead to significant cost savings. The cost of producing new materials and the services associated with manufacturing these products are often much higher than the cost of repurposing existing buildings[^2^]. 

Moreover, historic preservation can increase the property value of brownfields. Historic buildings often have unique architectural features that are highly valued in the real estate market. By preserving these features, we can enhance the marketability of these properties.

##### Community Revitalization

Lastly, historic preservation can contribute to community revitalization. Brownfields are often located in historic districts or contain historic structures. By preserving these historic elements, we can help maintain the cultural identity of these communities. This can foster a sense of pride and belonging among community members, which can in turn stimulate community engagement and revitalization[^4^].

In conclusion, historic preservation plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. It promotes environmental sustainability, enhances economic viability, and contributes to community revitalization. Therefore, it is essential to incorporate historic preservation principles into brownfield redevelopment strategies.

[^2^]: "Adaptive building", Land recycling, Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment.
[^3^]: "Brownfield redevelopment", Land recycling, Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment.
[^4^]: "Historic Preservation and Brownfields", Section 17.1, Environmental Management Practicum: Brownfield Redevelopment.

#### 17.1c Case Studies in Historic Preservation and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the successful integration of historic preservation and brownfield redevelopment. These examples demonstrate the potential benefits of this approach, including environmental sustainability, economic viability, and community revitalization.

##### The Pearl District, Portland, Oregon

The Pearl District in Portland, Oregon, is a prime example of successful brownfield redevelopment combined with historic preservation[^4^]. Once an industrial area with numerous brownfields, the district has been transformed into a vibrant, mixed-use neighborhood. 

The redevelopment process involved the adaptive reuse of several historic buildings, which helped to maintain the district's unique architectural character. This not only reduced the need for new construction materials but also enhanced the marketability of the properties. 

Moreover, the redevelopment project included extensive environmental remediation efforts to address the contamination issues associated with the brownfields. This helped to mitigate the environmental risks and improve the overall environmental quality of the district[^4^].

##### The American Tobacco Historic District, Durham, North Carolina

The American Tobacco Historic District in Durham, North Carolina, is another noteworthy example. This district was once home to the American Tobacco Company, one of the largest tobacco manufacturers in the United States. After the company closed its operations, the district was left with numerous brownfields and abandoned historic buildings[^5^].

The redevelopment of the district involved the adaptive reuse of these historic buildings, turning them into offices, restaurants, and residential units. This not only preserved the district's historic character but also provided significant cost savings compared to new construction.

In addition, the redevelopment project included extensive environmental remediation efforts to address the contamination issues associated with the brownfields. This helped to improve the environmental quality of the district and reduce the risk of exposure to hazardous substances[^5^].

These case studies demonstrate the potential benefits of integrating historic preservation and brownfield redevelopment. They show that this approach can lead to environmental, economic, and community benefits, making it a viable strategy for managing brownfields.

[^4^]: "The Pearl District: A Case Study of Brownfield Redevelopment." Oregon Metro. https://www.oregonmetro.gov/sites/default/files/2014/05/07/pearl_district_case_study.pdf
[^5^]: "American Tobacco Historic District: A Case Study of Brownfield Redevelopment." City of Durham. https://durhamnc.gov/3102/American-Tobacco-Historic-District

### Section: 17.2 Historic Preservation Strategies:

#### 17.2a Overview of Historic Preservation Strategies

Historic preservation strategies are crucial in brownfield redevelopment projects, especially when these sites contain buildings or structures of historical significance. These strategies aim to protect and conserve the historical value of these sites while facilitating their redevelopment for modern use[^6^]. 

There are several strategies that can be employed in the context of brownfield redevelopment, each with its own set of benefits and challenges. These strategies can be broadly categorized into three main types: regulatory compliance, architectural conservation, and community engagement[^7^].

##### Regulatory Compliance

Regulatory compliance is a critical aspect of historic preservation. In the United States, about 90% of professional, paid practice in historic preservation is in the area of regulatory compliance[^8^]. This involves adhering to laws, regulations, and guidelines at the federal, state, and local levels that are designed to protect historic sites and buildings. 

For instance, the National Historic Preservation Act (NHPA) requires federal agencies to take into account the effects of their undertakings on historic properties[^9^]. This means that any brownfield redevelopment project that involves federal funding or permits must comply with the NHPA.

##### Architectural Conservation

Architectural conservation involves preserving and restoring the architectural features of historic buildings. This can include structural repairs, facade restoration, and interior renovations. The goal is to maintain the architectural integrity of the building while making it suitable for modern use[^10^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, architectural conservation can often involve adaptive reuse, which is the process of repurposing buildings for uses other than what they were originally designed for. This strategy not only preserves the historic character of the building but also reduces the need for new construction materials, contributing to environmental sustainability[^11^].

##### Community Engagement

Community engagement is a key strategy in historic preservation. This involves involving the local community in the preservation process, from planning and decision-making to implementation and maintenance[^12^]. 

Engaging the community can help to ensure that the redevelopment project aligns with the community's values and needs. It can also foster a sense of ownership and pride among community members, which can contribute to the long-term success of the project[^13^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these strategies, providing practical examples and case studies to illustrate their application in brownfield redevelopment projects.

[^6^]: National Trust for Historic Preservation. (2017). Preservation Leadership Forum. Retrieved from https://forum.savingplaces.org/learn/fundamentals/preservation-basics
[^7^]: National Park Service. (2018). Historic Preservation Professional Practice. Retrieved from https://www.nps.gov/tps/education/professional-practice.htm
[^8^]: Ibid.
[^9^]: Advisory Council on Historic Preservation. (2018). National Historic Preservation Act. Retrieved from https://www.achp.gov/digital-library-section-106-landing/national-historic-preservation-act

[^13^]: Ibid.

#### 17.2b Selection of Historic Preservation Strategies

Selecting the appropriate historic preservation strategy for a brownfield redevelopment project requires careful consideration of several factors. These include the historical significance of the site, the condition of the existing structures, the intended future use of the site, and the regulatory requirements that apply to the project[^11^].

##### Historical Significance

The historical significance of a site can greatly influence the choice of preservation strategy. Sites with high historical significance may warrant more intensive preservation efforts, such as full restoration or reconstruction. On the other hand, sites with lower historical significance may be suitable for more flexible strategies, such as adaptive reuse or facadism[^12^].

##### Condition of Existing Structures

The physical condition of the existing structures on a brownfield site is another important factor to consider. Structures that are in good condition may be suitable for preservation or adaptive reuse. However, structures that are in poor condition may require more extensive interventions, such as stabilization or reconstruction[^13^].

##### Intended Future Use

The intended future use of the site can also influence the choice of preservation strategy. For example, if the site is to be used for commercial purposes, strategies that allow for modifications to the interior of the buildings, such as adaptive reuse, may be appropriate. However, if the site is to be used for educational or cultural purposes, strategies that preserve the original features of the buildings, such as restoration or preservation, may be more suitable[^14^].

##### Regulatory Requirements

Finally, the regulatory requirements that apply to the project can also influence the choice of preservation strategy. For example, if the site is listed on the National Register of Historic Places, certain preservation strategies may be required by law[^15^]. Additionally, local zoning laws and building codes can also influence the choice of preservation strategy[^16^].

In conclusion, the selection of historic preservation strategies for brownfield redevelopment projects is a complex process that requires careful consideration of a variety of factors. By taking these factors into account, developers can choose strategies that not only preserve the historical value of the site, but also facilitate its redevelopment for modern use.

#### 17.2c Case Studies in Historic Preservation Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of different historic preservation strategies in brownfield redevelopment projects. These case studies will provide practical examples of how the principles and strategies discussed in the previous sections can be applied in real-world scenarios.

##### Case Study 1: Hewitt & Emerson Architectural Works

Hewitt & Emerson is a renowned architectural firm known for its commitment to preserving the historical integrity of the sites it redevelops[^16^]. One of their notable projects involved the adaptive reuse of a historic building in a brownfield site. The firm was able to maintain the architectural and heritage character of the building while transforming it into a modern commercial space. This project serves as an excellent example of how adaptive reuse can be used to promote sustainable historical and cultural development of urban areas[^17^].

##### Case Study 2: Ribeirinha (Horta)

Ribeirinha, a small town in Horta, has managed to preserve a group of architecturally significant buildings despite the challenges posed by earthquakes and inundation. The town's approach to historic preservation has involved a combination of strategies, including stabilization, restoration, and adaptive reuse. This case study highlights the importance of flexibility and adaptability in historic preservation, especially in areas prone to natural disasters[^18^].

##### Case Study 3: Indigenous Architecture in Canada

Contemporary indigenous Canadian and Métis architecture provides another interesting case study in historic preservation. Many indigenous communities have successfully integrated traditional architectural styles and techniques into modern building designs. This approach not only preserves the cultural heritage of these communities but also promotes sustainability and resilience[^19^].

In conclusion, these case studies demonstrate that there is no one-size-fits-all approach to historic preservation in brownfield redevelopment. The choice of strategy depends on a variety of factors, including the historical significance of the site, the condition of the existing structures, the intended future use of the site, and the applicable regulatory requirements. By carefully considering these factors, developers can select the preservation strategy that best meets the needs of the project and the community[^20^].

### Section: 17.3 Historic Preservation and Community Engagement:

#### 17.3a Role of Community Engagement in Historic Preservation

Community engagement plays a pivotal role in the process of historic preservation, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. The involvement of local communities is not only a matter of democratic participation but also a crucial factor in ensuring the success and sustainability of preservation efforts[^20^].

Local communities are often the primary stakeholders in heritage sites. Their intimate knowledge of the site, its history, and its cultural significance can provide invaluable insights that may not be apparent to external experts[^21^]. This is particularly true in the case of indigenous communities, who often have a deep cultural and spiritual connection to their ancestral lands[^22^].

The concept of "value-led conservation" has emerged as a key principle in heritage management. This approach emphasizes the importance of identifying and respecting the values associated with a heritage site, which are often best understood by the local community[^23^]. This approach was first formalized in the Burra Charter by ICOMOS Australia in 1979 and has since been developed further by the work of the GCI[^24^].

Community engagement can take many forms, including knowledge exchange, education, consultation, exhibitions, academic events, and publicity campaigns. These activities can help to foster a sense of ownership and stewardship among the local community, thereby enhancing the sustainability of preservation efforts[^25^].

For instance, in Homs, Syria, local charities have been working with local communities to protect their heritage. Similarly, a conservation programme in Dangeil, Sudan, has leveraged social and economic relationships with the community to ensure the long-term sustainability of the project[^26^].

In Australia, indigenous communities have increasingly taken on the stewardship of conservation and management programs to care for, monitor, and maintain their cultural heritage places and landscapes[^27^].

Participatory action research is another method that has been used to engage communities in archaeology projects. This approach involves the community in the research process, thereby ensuring that the research is relevant and beneficial to the community[^28^].

In conclusion, community engagement is a crucial component of historic preservation, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. By involving local communities in the preservation process, we can ensure that preservation efforts are sustainable, culturally sensitive, and beneficial to the community[^29^].

In the next section, we will explore some practical strategies for engaging communities in historic preservation efforts.

#### 17.3b Strategies for Community Engagement in Historic Preservation

Engaging communities in historic preservation efforts requires a thoughtful and strategic approach. Here are some strategies that have proven effective in various contexts[^30^]:

1. **Consultation**: This involves seeking the input and feedback of the local community at various stages of the preservation process. Consultation can take many forms, including public meetings, surveys, and focus groups[^31^].

2. **Education and Awareness**: This involves educating the local community about the importance of historic preservation and the specific cultural and historical significance of the site. This can be achieved through workshops, exhibitions, and educational programs[^32^].

3. **Participation**: This involves actively involving the local community in the preservation process. This can include volunteer programs, community archaeology projects, and participatory action research[^33^].

4. **Partnerships**: This involves forming partnerships with local organizations, such as charities, schools, and community groups. These partnerships can provide additional resources and support for preservation efforts[^34^].

In the following sections, we will explore each of these strategies in more detail, providing practical examples and case studies to illustrate their application in real-world scenarios.

#### 17.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Historic Preservation

Engaging the community in historic preservation can be achieved through various techniques. These techniques aim to involve the community in the preservation process, ensuring their values and perspectives are considered, and fostering a sense of ownership and stewardship[^27^].

##### Participatory Action Research

Participatory action research (PAR) is a method that has been used in community archaeology projects[^28^]. This approach involves the community in the research process, allowing them to contribute their knowledge and perspectives. It is a collaborative process that encourages active participation and learning, fostering a sense of ownership and responsibility among community members[^29^].

##### Digital Archaeology

Digital archaeology is another technique that can be used to engage the community in historic preservation. The use of digital technology allows for the analysis, documentation, and reconstruction of historical sites and artifacts through non-intrusive methods[^30^]. This can include the use of 3D modeling technology to accurately model archaeological sites, providing further information to formulate archaeological perspectives[^31^].

Digital archaeology can also facilitate the dissemination of archaeological data to the public, promoting communication between the cultural heritage of archaeological sites and the public population[^32^]. This can help to foster a sense of connection and engagement with the heritage site, enhancing the sustainability of preservation efforts[^33^].

##### Community Archaeology

Community archaeology is a technique that involves the community in the archaeological process. This can include involving community members in archaeological excavations, research, and interpretation[^34^]. This approach can help to foster a sense of ownership and stewardship among the community, enhancing the sustainability of preservation efforts[^35^].

##### Think Resilience

Think Resilience is an online course that can be used to engage the community in historic preservation. The course provides information on how to build community resilience, which can be applied to the context of historic preservation[^36^]. By equipping community members with the knowledge and skills to build resilience, they can be better prepared to face the challenges associated with historic preservation[^37^].

In conclusion, engaging the community in historic preservation is crucial for the success and sustainability of preservation efforts. Various techniques can be used to achieve this, including participatory action research, digital archaeology, community archaeology, and resilience education. These techniques can help to foster a sense of ownership and stewardship among the community, ensuring their values and perspectives are considered in the preservation process[^38^].

#### 17.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Historic Preservation

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the successful integration of community engagement and historic preservation.

##### Case Study 1: Jonathan Creek Archaeological Site

The Jonathan Creek archaeological site is a prime example of community engagement in historic preservation. This Mississippian-era town and mound center site shows a history of growth and rebuilding[^36^]. The community was involved in the excavation process, contributing their knowledge and perspectives to the interpretation of the site[^37^].

The use of digital archaeology was also prominent in this project. 3D modeling technology was used to accurately model the archaeological site, providing further information to formulate archaeological perspectives[^38^]. This digital data was disseminated to the public, promoting communication between the cultural heritage of the site and the public population[^39^].

##### Case Study 2: Center for Public History and Digital Humanities

The Center for Public History and Digital Humanities is another example of successful community engagement in historic preservation. The center uses digital technology to document and analyze historical sites and artifacts[^40^]. This includes the use of 3D modeling technology to accurately model archaeological sites[^41^].

The center also involves the community in the research process through participatory action research. This approach allows the community to contribute their knowledge and perspectives, fostering a sense of ownership and responsibility among community members[^42^].

##### Case Study 3: Contemporary Indigenous Canadian and Métis Architecture

Contemporary Indigenous Canadian and Métis architecture projects are notable examples of community archaeology. These projects involve the community in the archaeological process, from excavation to research and interpretation[^43^]. This approach fosters a sense of ownership and stewardship among the community, enhancing the sustainability of preservation efforts[^44^].

In conclusion, these case studies highlight the importance of community engagement in historic preservation. Through techniques such as participatory action research, digital archaeology, and community archaeology, communities can contribute their knowledge and perspectives, fostering a sense of ownership and stewardship. This not only enhances the sustainability of preservation efforts but also promotes communication between the cultural heritage of archaeological sites and the public population[^45^].

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation presents a unique challenge in environmental management. The process of transforming contaminated, underutilized sites into productive community assets requires a careful balance of environmental remediation, economic revitalization, and cultural preservation. 

The redevelopment of brownfields can serve as a catalyst for community revitalization, creating jobs, increasing property values, and improving the quality of life. However, it is essential to ensure that these projects do not compromise the historical integrity of the sites. Historic preservation is not just about preserving buildings and landscapes; it's about preserving the stories, memories, and cultural heritage that these sites represent. 

Incorporating historic preservation into brownfield redevelopment projects requires a multidisciplinary approach, involving environmental scientists, urban planners, architects, historians, and community stakeholders. It requires a deep understanding of the site's history, its environmental challenges, and its potential for future use. 

In the end, the goal of brownfield redevelopment and historic preservation is to create sustainable, vibrant communities that respect their past while looking forward to the future. It's about creating places where people want to live, work, and play, and where the environment, economy, and culture are in harmony.
