## Chapter: Brownfield Redevelopment and Food Systems

### Introduction

The intersection of brownfield redevelopment and food systems presents a unique opportunity to address environmental, economic, and social challenges in urban areas. Brownfields, defined as abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities available for re-use, often pose environmental risks due to potential contamination. However, their redevelopment can lead to significant benefits, including the creation of local jobs, increased property values, and improved environmental conditions.

In this chapter, we will explore the role of food systems in brownfield redevelopment. Food systems encompass all the stages of food production, from cultivation and harvesting to distribution and consumption. They are integral to the health and well-being of communities, and their integration into brownfield redevelopment projects can contribute to food security, community resilience, and sustainable urban development.

We will delve into the potential benefits and challenges of integrating food systems into brownfield redevelopment, including the potential for urban agriculture, the creation of food hubs, and the development of local food economies. We will also discuss the environmental considerations associated with these projects, such as soil remediation and water management.

This chapter will provide a comprehensive overview of the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and food systems, offering insights into the potential for these projects to contribute to sustainable urban development and community resilience. Through a combination of case studies, theoretical discussions, and practical examples, we aim to provide a thorough understanding of this complex and evolving field.

### Section: 18.1 Food Systems and Brownfields:

#### 18.1a Definition of Food Systems

A food system encompasses the entire range of activities and processes involved in the production, processing, marketing, consumption, and disposal of food and food-related items. This includes the growing, harvesting, packaging, transporting, marketing, consumption, distribution, and disposal of food and food-related items. It also includes the inputs needed and outputs generated at each of these steps. Food systems fall within agri-food systems, which include the entire range of actors and their interlinked value-adding activities in the primary production of food and non-food agricultural products, as well as in food storage, aggregation, post-harvest handling, transportation, processing, distribution, marketing, disposal, and consumption[^1^].

Food systems operate within and are influenced by various contexts, including social, political, economic, technological, and environmental. They require human resources that provide labor, research, and education. Food systems can be categorized as either conventional or alternative, depending on their model of food lifespan from origin to plate. They are dependent on a multitude of ecosystem services, such as natural pest regulations, microorganisms providing nitrogen-fixation, and pollinators[^1^].

The global food system, including all of the various industries involved in sustainable and conventional food systems, provides employment for 1 billion people[^1^]. However, it is facing a number of challenges created by impeding global food security issues created by climate change and non-climate change stresses on the system. About 34% of total greenhouse gas emissions are attributable to the global food system[^1^]. In 2020, an EU evidence review found that food system gas emissions are on course to increase by 30–40% by 2050 due to population growth and dietary change[^1^]. According to the Food and Agriculture Organization (FAO), it is crucial to build the resilience of agri-food systems[^1^].

[^1^]: IPCC, 2019. Climate Change and Land: an IPCC special report on climate change, desertification, land degradation, sustainable land management, food security, and greenhouse gas fluxes in terrestrial ecosystems. Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change.

#### 18.1b Role of Food Systems in Brownfield Redevelopment

Food systems play a significant role in the redevelopment of brownfields. Brownfields, as defined by the EPA, are properties that may be complicated by the presence or potential presence of a hazardous substance, pollutant, or contaminant[^2^]. These sites, once rehabilitated, can provide valuable space for urban agriculture and local food systems, contributing to both environmental sustainability and food security.

Urban agriculture, a component of local food systems, involves the cultivation, processing, and distribution of food in or around urban areas[^3^]. This practice can be particularly beneficial in brownfield redevelopment as it can transform contaminated, unused land into productive spaces that provide fresh, local produce to communities. This not only contributes to food security but also promotes community engagement and revitalization[^3^].

The redevelopment of brownfields into urban agricultural spaces can also contribute to environmental sustainability. For instance, the process of phytoremediation, which involves the use of plants to remove, stabilize, or break down contaminants in the soil, can be employed in these sites[^4^]. This not only cleans up the contaminated land but also prepares it for agricultural use. Moreover, urban agriculture can contribute to reducing the carbon footprint of food systems by minimizing the distance food travels from farm to plate, thereby reducing greenhouse gas emissions associated with food transportation[^5^].

However, the redevelopment of brownfields for food systems is not without challenges. These include potential risks to food safety due to residual contamination, regulatory barriers, and the need for technical expertise in both brownfield remediation and urban agriculture[^6^]. Therefore, careful planning, community involvement, and collaboration among various stakeholders, including environmental professionals, urban farmers, and local government, are crucial for the successful integration of food systems into brownfield redevelopment.

In conclusion, food systems can play a pivotal role in brownfield redevelopment, contributing to environmental sustainability, food security, and community revitalization. However, to fully realize these benefits, it is essential to address the challenges associated with this integration and foster collaboration among various stakeholders.

[^2^]: EPA. (2021). Brownfields Overview. U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. https://www.epa.gov/brownfields/brownfields-overview
[^3^]: Mougeot, L. J. A. (2000). Urban agriculture: Definition, presence, potentials and risks. In N. Bakker, M. Dubbeling, S. Gündel, U. Sabel-Koschella, & H. de Zeeuw (Eds.), Growing cities, growing food: Urban agriculture on the policy agenda (pp. 1–42). German Foundation for International Development (DSE).
[^4^]: Pilon-Smits, E. (2005). Phytoremediation. Annual Review of Plant Biology, 56, 15–39. https://doi.org/10.1146/annurev.arplant.56.032604.144214
[^5^]: Weber, C. L., & Matthews, H. S. (2008). Food-miles and the relative climate impacts of food choices in the United States. Environmental Science & Technology, 42(10), 3508–3513. https://doi.org/10.1021/es702969f
[^6^]: De Sousa, C. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181–198. https://doi.org/10.1016/S0169-2046(02)00149-2

#### 18.1c Case Studies in Food Systems and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the intersection of food systems and brownfield redevelopment. These examples will illustrate the potential benefits and challenges of integrating urban agriculture into brownfield redevelopment projects.

##### Case Study 1: The Plant, Chicago, Illinois

The Plant is a former meatpacking facility in Chicago that has been transformed into a vertical farm and food business operation[^7^]. The 93,500 square foot facility now houses a variety of food-related businesses, including a brewery, a bakery, and several indoor farms. The Plant is also home to a large aquaponics system, which combines aquaculture (raising fish) with hydroponics (growing plants in water) in a symbiotic environment[^7^].

The Plant exemplifies the potential of brownfield redevelopment for urban agriculture. It not only provides fresh, local produce to the community but also creates jobs and serves as a model for sustainable food production. However, the project also faced significant challenges, including the high cost of retrofitting the old building and the need for specialized knowledge in aquaponics[^7^].

##### Case Study 2: The Michigan Urban Farming Initiative, Detroit, Michigan

The Michigan Urban Farming Initiative (MUFI) is a non-profit organization that uses agriculture as a platform to promote education, sustainability, and community in an urban environment[^8^]. MUFI has transformed a two-square-block area in Detroit's North End, which was previously filled with vacant lots and abandoned buildings, into a thriving urban agrihood[^8^].

MUFI's urban farm produces over 300 varieties of vegetables, which are provided free to the community. The project has not only improved food security in the area but also revitalized the neighborhood and provided educational opportunities for local residents[^8^]. However, MUFI also faced challenges, including soil contamination and the need for ongoing funding to support its operations[^8^].

These case studies illustrate the potential of brownfield redevelopment for urban agriculture, as well as the challenges that such projects may face. They underscore the need for careful planning, community involvement, and collaboration among various stakeholders to ensure the success of these initiatives.

[^7^]: The Plant Chicago. (n.d.). About. Retrieved from https://www.plantchicago.org/about
[^8^]: Michigan Urban Farming Initiative. (n.d.). About Us. Retrieved from http://www.miufi.org/about-us

### Section: 18.2 Food Systems Strategies:

#### 18.2a Overview of Food Systems Strategies

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, food systems strategies play a crucial role in ensuring the resilience and sustainability of agrifood systems. These strategies are designed to address the challenges posed by disruptions, such as those caused by environmental changes or economic shocks, and to enhance the capacity of the food system to absorb, adapt, and transform in response to these disruptions[^9^].

##### Diverse Food Sourcing

One key strategy is the diversification of food sourcing. This involves sourcing food from a variety of domestic and international suppliers, which can help buffer the food supply against shocks and stresses[^9^]. For instance, if a domestic crop fails due to a natural disaster, having diverse international trade partners can ensure the continued supply of that crop. However, this strategy also exposes the food system to external shocks, such as changes in international market prices or political instability in exporting countries[^9^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, diverse food sourcing can be facilitated by creating spaces for urban agriculture, which can supplement the food supply with locally grown produce. This not only enhances the diversity of the food supply but also reduces the environmental impact of food transportation[^10^].

##### Diverse Food Supply Chain Types

Another important strategy is the diversification of food supply chain types. This involves supporting a mix of traditional, transitional, and modern food supply chains, each of which has different vulnerabilities and resilience capacities[^9^]. For example, traditional food supply chains, which often involve small-scale producers and small and medium agrifood enterprises (SMAEs), may be more vulnerable to disruptions due to their limited resources. However, they can also be more resilient in some respects, as they are often more embedded in local communities and can adapt more quickly to changes in local conditions[^9^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, supporting diverse food supply chain types can involve creating spaces for different types of food businesses, from small-scale urban farms to large-scale food processing facilities. This not only enhances the resilience of the food system but also creates jobs and supports local economic development[^10^].

In the following sections, we will explore these strategies in more detail and discuss how they can be implemented in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

[^9^]: FAO. (2021). The State of Food and Agriculture 2021. Rome. https://doi.org/10.4060/cb4474en
[^10^]: De Zeeuw, H., & Dubbeling, M. (2009). Cities, food and agriculture: Challenges and the way forward. RUAF Foundation.

#### 18.2b Selection of Food Systems Strategies

The selection of food systems strategies in the context of brownfield redevelopment requires careful consideration of the local context, including the available resources, the characteristics of the local food system, and the potential impacts of different strategies[^11^].

##### Diverse Food Sourcing

In selecting diverse food sourcing strategies, it is important to consider the potential benefits and risks associated with different sources of food. For instance, while international trade can buffer against domestic shocks, it can also increase exposure to external shocks[^9^]. Therefore, it may be beneficial to balance international trade with domestic production and local food sourcing strategies, such as urban agriculture[^10^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, urban agriculture can be a particularly effective strategy. By transforming brownfields into productive urban farms, it is possible to increase the diversity of the local food supply, reduce the environmental impact of food transportation, and contribute to local economic development[^12^].

##### Diverse Food Supply Chain Types

In selecting diverse food supply chain strategies, it is important to consider the different vulnerabilities and resilience capacities of traditional, transitional, and modern food supply chains[^9^]. For instance, while traditional food supply chains may be more vulnerable to disruptions due to their limited resources, they can also be more resilient in some respects, as they are often more embedded in local communities[^9^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, supporting a mix of food supply chain types can help to build a more resilient and sustainable food system. For instance, by providing support for small-scale producers and small and medium agrifood enterprises (SMAEs), it is possible to enhance the resilience of the local food system, while also contributing to local economic development[^13^].

In conclusion, the selection of food systems strategies in the context of brownfield redevelopment requires a careful balance of diverse food sourcing and diverse food supply chain types, taking into account the local context and the potential impacts of different strategies[^11^]. By doing so, it is possible to build a more resilient and sustainable food system that can absorb, adapt, and transform in response to disruptions[^9^].

#### 18.2c Case Studies in Food Systems Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of diverse food sourcing and diverse food supply chain strategies in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 1: Urban Agriculture in Detroit, USA

Detroit, a city that has faced significant economic challenges, has turned to urban agriculture as a strategy for brownfield redevelopment[^14^]. The city has a large number of vacant lots, many of which are brownfields, that have been transformed into urban farms. These farms not only provide a source of fresh, locally grown food, but also create jobs and contribute to the revitalization of neighborhoods[^14^].

The urban agriculture movement in Detroit has been supported by a mix of public, private, and non-profit organizations. For instance, the Detroit Black Community Food Security Network operates D-Town Farm, a seven-acre organic farm located on a former brownfield site[^15^]. The farm produces a diverse range of crops, contributing to the resilience of the local food system.

##### Case Study 2: Small and Medium Agrifood Enterprises (SMAEs) in Nairobi, Kenya

In Nairobi, Kenya, SMAEs play a crucial role in the local food system. Many of these enterprises operate in informal markets, which are often located on brownfield sites[^16^]. These markets provide a source of affordable food for low-income residents and create employment opportunities.

However, these enterprises face significant challenges, including limited access to capital and vulnerability to disruptions. To address these challenges, the Kenyan government and non-profit organizations have implemented programs to provide financial and technical support to SMAEs[^16^]. These programs have helped to enhance the resilience of the local food system and contribute to economic development.

These case studies illustrate the potential of diverse food sourcing and diverse food supply chain strategies to contribute to the resilience and sustainability of food systems in the context of brownfield redevelopment. However, they also highlight the importance of supportive policies and investments to ensure the success of these strategies.

In the next section, we will discuss the role of policy and investment in supporting food systems strategies in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

[^14^]: Pothukuchi, K. (2013). The Detroit Food System: A Handbook for Urban Food Systems. Wayne State University.
[^15^]: Detroit Black Community Food Security Network. (2020). D-Town Farm.
[^16^]: Neven, D., Odera, M. M., Reardon, T., & Wang, H. (2009). Kenyan Supermarkets, Emerging Middle-Class Horticultural Farmers, and Employment Impacts on the Rural Poor. World Development, 37(11), 1802-1811.

### Section: 18.3 Food Systems and Community Engagement:

#### 18.3a Role of Community Engagement in Food Systems

Community engagement plays a crucial role in the development and sustainability of local food systems, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It is through the active participation of community members that brownfield sites can be transformed into productive spaces that contribute to local food security, environmental sustainability, and economic development[^17^].

Community engagement in local food systems can take many forms. It can involve participation in farmers' markets, community-supported agriculture (CSA) programs, and urban farming initiatives. It can also involve advocacy for policies that support local food production and distribution[^18^].

##### Benefits of Community Engagement in Local Food Systems

Community engagement in local food systems can yield a range of benefits. These include:

###### Social Benefits

Community engagement can foster social cohesion and build relationships among community members[^19^]. For instance, farmers' markets can serve as social hubs, encouraging interaction among shoppers, farmers, and employees[^20^]. Such interactions can strengthen community ties and promote a sense of belonging.

###### Environmental Benefits

Community engagement in local food systems can contribute to environmental sustainability. Local food systems typically require less energy for storage and transport, potentially reducing greenhouse gas emissions[^21^]. Moreover, community members can play a role in ensuring that local food production practices are environmentally sustainable, for instance, by advocating for organic farming practices[^22^].

###### Economic Benefits

Community engagement can also yield economic benefits. Local food systems can create jobs and stimulate local economies[^23^]. For instance, a study in Iowa found that the introduction of farmers' markets created 576 jobs and led to a significant increase in output and income[^24^].

###### Nutritional Benefits

Finally, community engagement in local food systems can contribute to improved nutrition. Local foods often travel shorter distances and may require less processing or packaging, potentially resulting in fresher, more nutritious food options[^25^].

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, community engagement can be particularly important. It can help to ensure that redevelopment efforts are responsive to community needs and priorities, and that they contribute to the creation of sustainable, resilient local food systems[^26^]. In the next section, we will explore some strategies for fostering community engagement in local food systems.

#### 18.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Food Systems

Engaging the community in food systems is a multifaceted process that requires a combination of strategies to be effective. Here are some techniques that have proven successful in various contexts:

##### Community Meetings and Workshops

Community meetings and workshops provide a platform for community members to learn about local food systems, express their concerns, and contribute their ideas[^24^]. These meetings can be organized by local government agencies, non-profit organizations, or community groups. They can cover a range of topics, from the basics of urban farming to the intricacies of food policy[^25^].

##### Collaborative Planning and Decision-Making

Involving community members in the planning and decision-making processes of local food systems can foster a sense of ownership and commitment[^26^]. This can be achieved through participatory planning methods, such as community visioning exercises, design charrettes, and consensus-building workshops[^27^].

##### Community-Based Research

Community-based research involves partnering with community members to conduct research on local food systems. This approach not only generates valuable data but also builds capacity within the community[^28^]. Community members can be involved in various stages of the research process, from identifying research questions to collecting and analyzing data[^29^].

##### Volunteer and Internship Opportunities

Offering volunteer and internship opportunities in local food systems can provide community members with hands-on experience and practical skills[^30^]. These opportunities can be offered by urban farms, farmers' markets, and other food-related organizations[^31^].

##### Public Awareness Campaigns

Public awareness campaigns can be used to educate the community about the importance of local food systems and the role they can play in supporting them[^32^]. These campaigns can utilize various media, including social media, newsletters, and local newspapers[^33^].

In conclusion, engaging the community in food systems requires a multifaceted approach that combines education, participation, research, and awareness-raising. By leveraging these techniques, communities can play an active role in shaping their local food systems, contributing to food security, environmental sustainability, and economic development[^34^].

#### 18.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Food Systems

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the role of community engagement in food systems, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 1: The Activating Change Together for Community Food Security (ACT for CFS) Project, Nova Scotia, Canada

The ACT for CFS project in Nova Scotia, Canada, is an excellent example of how participatory action research (PAR) can be used to address community food security (CFS)[^33^]. The project involved community members in the research process, empowering them to identify and address issues related to CFS. The data collected through this project was used to influence policy and promote social change[^34^]. This case study demonstrates the potential of PAR as a tool for community engagement in food systems.

##### Case Study 2: The Role of Farmers' Markets in Promoting Local Food, USA

Farmers' markets in the United States have played a significant role in promoting local food and fostering community interaction[^35^]. These markets have been found to encourage more sociable behavior compared to supermarkets, with 75% of shoppers at farmers' markets arriving in groups compared to 16% at supermarkets[^36^]. This case study highlights the potential of farmers' markets as a platform for community engagement in local food systems.

##### Case Study 3: The Role of Community-Based Research in Urban Agriculture, Detroit, USA

In Detroit, USA, community-based research has been used to engage community members in urban agriculture[^37^]. Community members were involved in various stages of the research process, from identifying research questions to collecting and analyzing data. This approach not only generated valuable data but also built capacity within the community[^38^]. This case study illustrates the potential of community-based research as a tool for community engagement in food systems.

These case studies underscore the importance of community engagement in food systems, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. They highlight the potential of various strategies, including participatory action research, farmers' markets, and community-based research, in fostering community engagement and promoting local food systems.

[^33^]: http://foodarc.ca/actforcfs
[^34^]: http://foodarc.ca/project-pages/results-publications/

[^38^]: Pothukuchi, K. (2009). Community and Regional Food Planning: Building Institutional Support in the United States. International Planning Studies, 14(4), 349-367.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields into productive food systems is a multifaceted process that requires careful planning and management. It involves the identification and assessment of brownfields, the remediation of contaminated sites, and the implementation of sustainable agricultural practices. The transformation of brownfields into food systems not only contributes to environmental sustainability but also promotes food security and community development. However, it is important to note that the success of brownfield redevelopment projects largely depends on the collaboration and commitment of various stakeholders, including government agencies, private sector entities, and local communities. Therefore, effective communication and public participation are crucial in ensuring the successful implementation and sustainability of brownfield redevelopment projects.

## Chapter: Chapter 19: Brownfield Redevelopment and Housing

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment represents a significant opportunity to transform underutilized and often contaminated sites into productive community assets. This chapter, "Brownfield Redevelopment and Housing", delves into the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and housing, exploring how these abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities can be repurposed to meet housing needs.

The chapter will examine the potential benefits and challenges of brownfield redevelopment for housing, including the potential for increased housing supply, improved neighborhood aesthetics, and the mitigation of environmental hazards. It will also discuss the regulatory and policy frameworks that guide brownfield redevelopment, and how these can either facilitate or hinder the transformation of these sites into housing.

The chapter will also explore case studies of successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have resulted in the creation of housing, providing practical insights into how these projects can be effectively managed. These case studies will highlight the importance of stakeholder engagement, comprehensive planning, and innovative financing strategies in ensuring the success of brownfield redevelopment for housing.

In essence, this chapter aims to provide a comprehensive overview of the role of brownfield redevelopment in addressing housing needs, and the environmental management practices that can ensure these projects are both sustainable and beneficial for communities. It is hoped that this chapter will serve as a valuable resource for practitioners, policymakers, and students interested in the intersection of environmental management and housing.

### Section: 19.1 Housing and Brownfields:

#### 19.1a Definition of Housing

Housing, in the broadest sense, refers to the provision of shelter - a fundamental human need. It encompasses the construction and utilization of structures, either individually or collectively, for the purpose of providing a dwelling place. Housing plays a pivotal role in determining the quality of life for individuals, families, and communities. It is not just about having a roof over one's head; it is about having a safe, affordable, and stable place to live. 

Housing can be categorized into two types: market housing and non-market housing. Market housing includes apartments, condominiums, and private housing, which are bought and sold on the open market. The prices and rents for these types of housing are determined by supply and demand, and are typically owned by private individuals or corporations. Non-market housing, on the other hand, is provided and managed by the government or non-profit organizations, with the goal of providing affordable housing options to those who cannot afford market rates.

#### 19.1b Housing and Brownfields: An Overview

Brownfields, defined as abandoned or underused industrial and commercial facilities, often pose significant environmental and health risks due to the presence of hazardous substances or contaminants. However, these sites also represent untapped potential for housing development. Through brownfield redevelopment, these sites can be transformed into valuable community assets, providing much-needed housing in areas where land may be scarce or expensive.

The intersection of brownfield redevelopment and housing is a complex one, with numerous potential benefits and challenges. On the one hand, brownfield redevelopment can increase the supply of housing, improve neighborhood aesthetics, and mitigate environmental hazards. On the other hand, the process of redeveloping these sites can be complicated and costly, requiring extensive environmental cleanup and compliance with regulatory frameworks.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the potential benefits and challenges of brownfield redevelopment for housing, explore the regulatory and policy frameworks that guide this process, and examine case studies of successful brownfield redevelopment projects that have resulted in the creation of housing. Through this exploration, we aim to provide a comprehensive understanding of the role of brownfield redevelopment in addressing housing needs, and the environmental management practices that can ensure these projects are both sustainable and beneficial for communities.

#### 19.1b Role of Housing in Brownfield Redevelopment

Housing plays a significant role in brownfield redevelopment. The transformation of brownfields into residential areas can contribute to the revitalization of communities, providing affordable and accessible housing options, and promoting sustainable urban development. 

##### 19.1b.i Housing as a Catalyst for Brownfield Redevelopment

Housing development can serve as a catalyst for brownfield redevelopment. The demand for housing, particularly in urban areas, can drive the cleanup and redevelopment of brownfield sites. The Small Business Liability Relief and Brownfields Revitalization Act provides funding for brownfield assessment, cleanup, and redevelopment, which can be leveraged to create housing opportunities. 

The redevelopment of brownfields for housing can also stimulate economic growth by attracting investment and creating jobs. It can lead to increased property values in the surrounding area, generating additional tax revenue for local governments. Furthermore, the process of brownfield redevelopment can provide opportunities for environmental job training, contributing to workforce development in the community.

##### 19.1b.ii Housing and Community Engagement in Brownfield Redevelopment

Community engagement is a critical component of successful brownfield redevelopment. Local residents, particularly those who may be directly affected by the redevelopment, should be involved in the planning and decision-making process. This can help to ensure that the redevelopment meets the needs of the community, including the need for affordable housing.

Public-private partnerships can play a key role in facilitating community engagement in brownfield redevelopment. These partnerships can bring together local governments, private developers, community organizations, and residents to collaborate on the redevelopment process. They can help to leverage resources, share expertise, and build consensus around the vision for the redevelopment.

##### 19.1b.iii Housing and Environmental Justice in Brownfield Redevelopment

Brownfield redevelopment for housing must also consider issues of environmental justice. Brownfields are often located in low-income communities and communities of color, which have historically borne a disproportionate burden of environmental hazards. The redevelopment of these sites for housing should aim to mitigate these environmental injustices, by ensuring that the benefits of redevelopment, such as improved housing and environmental conditions, are equitably distributed.

In conclusion, housing plays a pivotal role in brownfield redevelopment, serving as a catalyst for economic growth, community engagement, and environmental justice. However, the process of redeveloping brownfields for housing can be complex and challenging, requiring careful planning, collaboration, and a commitment to environmental and social sustainability.

#### 19.1c Case Studies in Housing and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the successful redevelopment of brownfields into housing projects. These examples demonstrate the potential benefits of brownfield redevelopment, including environmental remediation, community revitalization, and economic growth.

##### 19.1c.i The Brewery Project, Los Angeles

The Brewery Project in Los Angeles, California, is a prime example of adaptive building and brownfield redevelopment. The site, which was once the Pabst Blue Ribbon Brewery, was abandoned in the 1970s and remained vacant for over a decade. In the 1980s, the property was purchased by a private developer who transformed the industrial complex into a residential and commercial space.

The Brewery Project now houses over 300 live/work units, providing affordable housing for artists and creative professionals. The redevelopment of the site has also stimulated economic growth in the area, attracting new businesses and creating jobs. Furthermore, the project has contributed to the revitalization of the surrounding community, transforming a once-blighted area into a vibrant cultural hub.

##### 19.1c.ii The Mueller Community, Austin

The Mueller Community in Austin, Texas, is another example of successful brownfield redevelopment. The site was formerly the Robert Mueller Municipal Airport, which was decommissioned in 1999. The City of Austin, in partnership with a private developer, embarked on a large-scale redevelopment project to transform the 700-acre brownfield site into a mixed-use urban village.

The Mueller Community now features over 5,000 homes, a third of which are reserved for affordable housing. The redevelopment has also included the construction of retail spaces, office buildings, and public parks. The project has not only remediated a large brownfield site but also created a sustainable and inclusive community.

##### 19.1c.iii The Stapleton Development, Denver

The Stapleton Development in Denver, Colorado, is one of the largest brownfield redevelopment projects in the United States. The site was formerly the Stapleton International Airport, which was decommissioned in 1995. The City of Denver, in partnership with a private developer, undertook a massive redevelopment project to transform the 4,700-acre site into a sustainable urban community.

The Stapleton Development now features over 8,000 homes, along with retail spaces, office buildings, and public parks. The project has also included the restoration of natural habitats and the creation of a 1,100-acre regional park. The redevelopment has not only remediated a vast brownfield site but also contributed to the economic and social revitalization of the surrounding area.

These case studies illustrate the potential of brownfield redevelopment as a strategy for housing development, environmental remediation, and community revitalization. They highlight the importance of public-private partnerships, community engagement, and sustainable design in the successful redevelopment of brownfield sites.

### Section: 19.2 Housing Strategies:

#### 19.2a Overview of Housing Strategies

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, housing strategies play a crucial role in transforming these underutilized or abandoned sites into vibrant, livable communities. These strategies can range from the creation of affordable housing units to the development of mixed-use properties that combine residential, commercial, and public spaces. 

##### Affordable Housing

One of the primary housing strategies in brownfield redevelopment is the provision of affordable housing. This approach not only addresses the pressing need for affordable homes in many urban areas but also promotes social inclusion and diversity. For instance, the Mueller Community in Austin, Texas, reserved a third of its 5,000 homes for affordable housing, creating a mixed-income community that caters to a diverse range of residents.

##### Mixed-Use Development

Mixed-use development is another common strategy in brownfield redevelopment. This approach involves the integration of residential, commercial, and public spaces within a single development. The Brewery Project in Los Angeles, California, exemplifies this strategy, with its transformation of an abandoned brewery into a residential and commercial space. This not only provided affordable housing for artists and creative professionals but also stimulated economic growth by attracting new businesses.

##### Energy Efficiency and Sustainability

In recent years, there has been a growing emphasis on energy efficiency and sustainability in housing strategies for brownfield redevelopment. This includes the use of green building practices, renewable energy sources, and energy-efficient refurbishments. For example, some public schemes for energy-efficient refurbishment target public buildings, stimulating renovation demand and construction.

##### Innovative Housing Models

Innovative housing models, such as double family housing strategies, are also being explored in brownfield redevelopment. These models can offer unique solutions to housing challenges, providing more flexible and efficient use of space. The Double House in Utrecht, for instance, has been studied worldwide for its innovative approach to double family housing.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these housing strategies, exploring their benefits, challenges, and real-world applications in brownfield redevelopment.

#### 19.2b Selection of Housing Strategies

The selection of housing strategies in brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors. These factors include the specific needs of the community, the environmental conditions of the site, the available resources, and the long-term sustainability of the project. 

##### Community Needs

Understanding the needs of the community is a critical first step in selecting a housing strategy. This involves assessing the demand for different types of housing, such as affordable housing, family housing, or housing for specific demographic groups. For instance, if there is a high demand for affordable housing in the area, a strategy that prioritizes the creation of affordable units would be appropriate. 

##### Environmental Conditions

The environmental conditions of the brownfield site can also influence the selection of a housing strategy. For example, if the site is contaminated with hazardous substances, a strategy that includes extensive remediation efforts would be necessary. On the other hand, if the site is located in an area with high energy costs, a strategy that emphasizes energy efficiency and the use of renewable energy sources could be beneficial.

##### Available Resources

The resources available for the redevelopment project, including financial resources, technical expertise, and community support, can also impact the selection of a housing strategy. For instance, if there are significant financial resources available, it may be possible to pursue a more ambitious strategy, such as a mixed-use development. Conversely, if resources are limited, a more modest strategy, such as the refurbishment of existing buildings, may be more feasible.

##### Long-Term Sustainability

Finally, the long-term sustainability of the project should be considered when selecting a housing strategy. This involves evaluating the potential environmental, social, and economic impacts of the strategy over time. For example, a strategy that includes the use of green building practices can help to reduce the environmental impact of the development, while also potentially reducing energy costs for residents in the long term.

In conclusion, the selection of housing strategies in brownfield redevelopment is a multifaceted process that requires a thorough understanding of the community's needs, the site's environmental conditions, the available resources, and the long-term sustainability of the project. By carefully considering these factors, it is possible to select a strategy that not only transforms the brownfield site into a vibrant, livable community, but also contributes to the broader goals of environmental management and sustainable development.

#### 19.2c Case Studies in Housing Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the application of different housing strategies in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will provide practical insights into the challenges and opportunities associated with various strategies.

##### Case Study 1: Stamford Brook, United Kingdom

The Stamford Brook project in the United Kingdom is an excellent example of a housing strategy that prioritized energy efficiency. This project involved the construction of 700 dwellings built to high efficiency standards. However, the results of this project showed a significant gap between the expected energy use before construction and the actual energy use once the houses were occupied. This discrepancy was largely attributed to workmanship and the quality of building elements, particularly thermal bridges that were not considered in the initial calculations (Leeds Metropolitan, 2010). 

This case study underscores the importance of considering the quality of workmanship and building elements in the design and construction phases of a housing strategy. It also highlights the need for accurate energy use estimations that take into account the behavior of occupants and the properties of building elements.

##### Case Study 2: The Greening of Brownfields in Chicago, USA

In Chicago, USA, a housing strategy was implemented that focused on the remediation of brownfield sites and the creation of green spaces. This strategy was driven by the community's need for affordable housing and the city's commitment to environmental sustainability. The project involved the remediation of contaminated sites and the construction of energy-efficient homes. The strategy also included the creation of green spaces to improve the quality of life for residents and promote biodiversity (City of Chicago, 2012).

This case study demonstrates the potential of a housing strategy that balances the needs of the community with environmental sustainability. It also shows how brownfield redevelopment can contribute to urban greening and biodiversity conservation.

##### Case Study 3: The Revitalization of the Ruhr Area, Germany

The Ruhr Area in Germany is a former industrial region that has undergone significant brownfield redevelopment. The housing strategy implemented in this area involved the refurbishment of existing buildings and the construction of new ones. The strategy also emphasized the integration of renewable energy sources and energy-efficient technologies. This approach was made possible by the availability of significant financial resources and technical expertise (Ruhr Area, 2015).

This case study illustrates the potential of a mixed-use development strategy in brownfield redevelopment. It also highlights the importance of integrating renewable energy sources and energy-efficient technologies in housing strategies.

In conclusion, these case studies show that the selection of a housing strategy in brownfield redevelopment should be guided by the specific needs of the community, the environmental conditions of the site, the available resources, and the long-term sustainability of the project. They also underscore the importance of considering the quality of workmanship and building elements, the behavior of occupants, and the properties of building elements in the design and construction phases of a housing strategy.

### Section: 19.3 Housing and Community Engagement:

#### 19.3a Role of Community Engagement in Housing

Community engagement plays a pivotal role in the development of housing, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It is a process that involves the active participation of community members in decision-making processes and the implementation of strategies that directly affect their living conditions and environment. This engagement can take various forms, from public meetings and workshops to participatory design processes and community-led initiatives.

Community engagement is not just about involving residents in decision-making processes; it is also about empowering them to take an active role in shaping their communities. As Fraser & Nelson (2010) suggest, community empowerment should accompany mixed-income housing developments to foster neighborhood relations, identify key community assets and needs, and maximize the "neighborhood effects" of the new development. This empowerment can be facilitated by creating central, shared community spaces with interesting programming that provides opportunities for social interaction among diverse residents.

Community engagement is also crucial in ensuring that the needs and aspirations of lower-income residents are taken into account in the development process. As Vale (2013) argues, empowering lower-income residents to meaningfully engage in the management of their developments can foster positive outcomes for individuals and the neighborhood as a whole. This can be achieved through formal and informal social controls, as well as through the establishment of programmatic or governance structures that give lower-income residents control over management, maintenance, and subsequent transformation.

The role of community engagement in housing is not limited to the planning and management stages. It also extends to the implementation of strategies aimed at poverty alleviation. As the body of literature suggests, economically diverse and integrated neighborhoods can support residents' opportunities and life chances (Joseph, 2012). However, more research is needed to understand the mechanisms by which this happens. Community engagement can play a crucial role in this research by providing insights into the dynamics of neighbor relations and the impact of mixed-income housing on residents' opportunities and life chances.

In conclusion, community engagement is a critical component of housing development, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It is a process that involves the active participation of community members in decision-making processes and the implementation of strategies that directly affect their living conditions and environment. By fostering community empowerment and participation, we can create housing developments that are not only economically diverse and integrated but also responsive to the needs and aspirations of all residents.

#### 19.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Housing

Engaging the community in housing, particularly in brownfield redevelopment, requires a multi-faceted approach that combines various techniques and strategies. Here are some of the most effective techniques for fostering community engagement in housing:

**Public Meetings and Workshops:** These are traditional methods of community engagement that provide a platform for residents to voice their concerns, ideas, and aspirations. Public meetings and workshops can be used to gather feedback on proposed developments, discuss potential issues, and brainstorm solutions. They can also be used to educate residents about the redevelopment process and the potential benefits and challenges of mixed-income housing.

**Participatory Design Processes:** Participatory design is a method that involves residents in the design and planning stages of housing development. This can be done through design charrettes, where residents, architects, planners, and developers come together to create design solutions. Participatory design not only ensures that the housing development meets the needs and preferences of the community, but also fosters a sense of ownership and investment among residents.

**Community-Led Initiatives:** Empowering residents to lead initiatives related to housing development can foster a sense of agency and engagement. This could involve setting up resident committees or groups to oversee certain aspects of the development process, such as the design of community spaces or the implementation of social programs.

**Shared Community Spaces:** As suggested by Fraser & Nelson (2010), creating central, shared community spaces with interesting programming can provide opportunities for social interaction among diverse residents. These spaces can serve as hubs for community activities and events, fostering a sense of community and belonging.

**Programmatic or Governance Structures:** As Vale (2013) argues, establishing programmatic or governance structures that give lower-income residents control over management, maintenance, and subsequent transformation can foster positive outcomes. This could involve setting up resident councils or boards, or implementing participatory budgeting processes where residents have a say in how funds are allocated.

**Communication and Transparency:** Keeping residents informed about the progress of the redevelopment and the decisions being made is crucial for maintaining trust and engagement. This can be achieved through regular updates, newsletters, or a dedicated website.

In conclusion, engaging the community in housing requires a combination of techniques that foster participation, empowerment, and transparency. It is not a one-size-fits-all approach, but rather a process that needs to be tailored to the specific needs and characteristics of the community.

#### 19.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Housing

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the importance of community engagement in housing, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment and mixed-income housing.

**Case Study 1: The Chicago Housing Authority's Plan for Transformation**

In the late 1990s, the Chicago Housing Authority (CHA) embarked on an ambitious plan to transform its public housing. The Plan for Transformation aimed to replace high-rise public housing projects with mixed-income communities, with the goal of deconcentrating poverty and improving the quality of life for public housing residents (Joseph, 2006). 

Community engagement was a key component of the Plan for Transformation. The CHA held numerous public meetings and workshops to gather feedback from residents and the broader community. They also established Local Advisory Councils, made up of public housing residents, to provide input on the redevelopment process. Despite these efforts, the Plan for Transformation faced criticism for not adequately involving residents in decision-making processes and for displacing many low-income residents (Joseph, 2006).

**Case Study 2: The Regent Park Revitalization in Toronto**

Regent Park, Canada's oldest and largest public housing project, has been undergoing a revitalization process since 2005. The revitalization aims to transform the neighborhood into a mixed-income community, with a mix of rent-geared-to-income and market-rate units.

Community engagement has been a central part of the Regent Park revitalization. The Toronto Community Housing Corporation (TCHC) has used a variety of techniques to engage residents, including public meetings, design charrettes, and resident-led initiatives. The TCHC has also created shared community spaces, such as the Regent Park Aquatic Centre and the Daniels Spectrum, a cultural hub that offers programming for residents of all ages (James, 2014).

**Case Study 3: The HOPE VI Program in the United States**

The HOPE VI program, launched by the U.S. Department of Housing and Urban Development in 1992, aimed to revitalize severely distressed public housing projects into mixed-income communities. The program emphasized the importance of community engagement, requiring grantees to involve residents in the planning and implementation of redevelopment projects (Popkin et al., 2004).

Despite these requirements, studies have found that resident participation in HOPE VI projects was often limited and that many residents felt excluded from decision-making processes (Popkin et al., 2004). These findings underscore the challenges of ensuring meaningful community engagement in housing redevelopment projects.

These case studies highlight the importance of community engagement in housing redevelopment projects, particularly those aimed at creating mixed-income communities. They also underscore the challenges of ensuring meaningful participation and the potential consequences of failing to do so. As we continue to explore the topic of brownfield redevelopment and housing, we will delve deeper into these issues and explore potential strategies for addressing them.

### Conclusion

In conclusion, the redevelopment of brownfields into housing is a complex but rewarding process. It not only provides a solution to the housing crisis in many urban areas but also helps in the rehabilitation of contaminated lands. The process involves a series of steps including site assessment, remediation, planning, and construction. Each step requires careful consideration of environmental, economic, and social factors. 

The redevelopment process also involves various stakeholders including local communities, government agencies, developers, and environmental consultants. Their collaboration is crucial to the success of the project. The role of environmental management in this process cannot be overstated. It ensures that the redevelopment is done in a manner that is safe for the residents and sustainable for the environment.

Despite the challenges, brownfield redevelopment for housing has numerous benefits. It reduces urban sprawl, promotes economic development, and improves the quality of life in urban areas. It is a practical and sustainable solution to the housing needs of our growing urban populations.
