## Chapter: Chapter 20: Brownfield Redevelopment and Transportation

### Introduction

Brownfield redevelopment is a critical aspect of environmental management, particularly in urban areas where industrial or commercial sites have been abandoned or underutilized due to real or perceived environmental contamination. The redevelopment of these brownfield sites can provide numerous benefits, including environmental remediation, economic revitalization, and improved community health and wellbeing. However, the process of brownfield redevelopment is complex and multifaceted, requiring careful planning and coordination among various stakeholders.

One key aspect of brownfield redevelopment that is often overlooked is transportation. The location of brownfield sites, often in urban or peri-urban areas, means that transportation infrastructure and planning play a crucial role in the success of redevelopment projects. This includes considerations of accessibility, connectivity, and the potential impacts on local traffic patterns and transportation networks.

In this chapter, we will explore the intersection of brownfield redevelopment and transportation, examining how these two areas of environmental management are interconnected. We will delve into the challenges and opportunities that transportation considerations can present in the context of brownfield redevelopment, and discuss strategies for integrating transportation planning into the redevelopment process.

Whether you are an environmental manager, urban planner, or simply interested in the sustainable development of our cities, this chapter will provide you with a comprehensive understanding of the role of transportation in brownfield redevelopment. Through a combination of theory, case studies, and practical examples, we aim to equip you with the knowledge and tools to effectively incorporate transportation considerations into your own brownfield redevelopment projects.

### Section: 20.1 Transportation and Brownfields

#### 20.1a Definition of Transportation

Transportation, in the context of environmental management and brownfield redevelopment, refers to the movement of people and goods from one location to another. This movement can be facilitated through various means, including but not limited to, animals, vehicles, and automated systems. 

Animals, such as pack animals and riding animals, have been used for transportation for centuries, particularly in rural and remote areas. However, in the context of urban brownfield redevelopment, the use of animals for transportation is generally less relevant.

Vehicles, on the other hand, play a crucial role in urban transportation. A vehicle is a non-living device that is used to move people and goods. Vehicles can be powered by various means, including steam engines, combustion engines, electric motors, jet engines, or rockets. The energy is then converted into movement, most commonly through wheels, propellers, and pressure. Vehicles can be operated by a driver, or in some cases, they can be fully automated. This is particularly relevant in the context of the Fourth Industrial Revolution, which has brought about innovations such as Connected Vehicles and Autonomous Driving. However, these emerging technologies also raise concerns about safety and cybersecurity.

The operation of transportation systems can be either private or public. Private transport is subject to the owner of the vehicle, who operates the vehicle themselves. Public transport and freight transport, on the other hand, can be operated through private enterprise or by governments. The infrastructure and vehicles may be owned and operated by the same company, or they may be operated by different entities. 

In the context of brownfield redevelopment, transportation considerations are crucial. The location of brownfield sites, often in urban or peri-urban areas, means that transportation infrastructure and planning play a key role in the success of redevelopment projects. This includes considerations of accessibility, connectivity, and the potential impacts on local traffic patterns and transportation networks. In the following sections, we will delve deeper into these considerations and discuss strategies for integrating transportation planning into the brownfield redevelopment process.

#### 20.1b Role of Transportation in Brownfield Redevelopment

Transportation plays a pivotal role in the redevelopment of brownfield sites. The integration of transportation infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects can significantly enhance the sustainability and livability of urban areas. 

Firstly, transportation infrastructure can facilitate access to brownfield sites. Many brownfield sites are located in urban or peri-urban areas that are well-served by existing transportation networks. By leveraging these networks, developers can ensure that brownfield sites are easily accessible to workers, residents, and visitors. This can enhance the economic viability of brownfield redevelopment projects and contribute to urban revitalization efforts.

Secondly, transportation infrastructure can support the sustainable redevelopment of brownfield sites. For instance, the integration of public transportation networks into brownfield redevelopment projects can reduce reliance on private vehicles, thereby reducing greenhouse gas emissions and contributing to climate change mitigation efforts. Moreover, the use of sustainable transportation technologies, such as electric vehicles and self-healing roadway materials, can further enhance the environmental sustainability of brownfield redevelopment projects.

Thirdly, transportation infrastructure can contribute to the resilience of brownfield redevelopment projects. For instance, the integration of smart grid technologies into transportation networks can enhance the reliability and security of energy supply, thereby reducing the vulnerability of brownfield sites to power outages and other disruptions. Moreover, the use of renewable energy sources for transportation can enhance the self-sufficiency of brownfield sites, thereby increasing their resilience to external shocks.

However, the integration of transportation infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects also presents challenges. These include the need to coordinate with multiple stakeholders, the need to comply with complex regulatory requirements, and the need to secure adequate funding. Moreover, the use of emerging transportation technologies, such as autonomous vehicles, raises concerns about safety and cybersecurity.

In conclusion, transportation plays a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. By integrating transportation infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects, developers can enhance the sustainability, livability, and resilience of urban areas. However, this requires careful planning, coordination, and management.

#### 20.1c Case Studies in Transportation and Brownfield Redevelopment

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the role of transportation in brownfield redevelopment. These case studies will illustrate how transportation infrastructure can facilitate access to brownfield sites, support sustainable redevelopment, and contribute to resilience.

##### Case Study 1: The Atlanta BeltLine, Atlanta, USA

The Atlanta BeltLine is a redevelopment project that aims to transform a 22-mile historic rail corridor that encircles downtown Atlanta into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit. The project leverages existing transportation networks to facilitate access to brownfield sites and integrates sustainable transportation technologies, such as electric vehicles and self-healing roadway materials, to enhance environmental sustainability[^1^].

##### Case Study 2: The Zuidas District, Amsterdam, The Netherlands

The Zuidas District in Amsterdam is a brownfield redevelopment project that integrates a range of transportation infrastructure, including a new train station, a bus terminal, and a bicycle parking facility. The project also incorporates smart grid technologies to enhance the reliability and security of energy supply and uses renewable energy sources for transportation to increase resilience[^2^].

##### Case Study 3: The Docklands, Melbourne, Australia

The Docklands in Melbourne is a brownfield redevelopment project that has integrated a range of transportation infrastructure, including a new tram line and a pedestrian bridge. The project has also incorporated sustainable transportation technologies, such as electric vehicles and self-healing roadway materials, to enhance environmental sustainability[^3^].

These case studies illustrate the potential of integrating transportation infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects. However, they also highlight the challenges that can arise, such as the need for significant investment and the potential for increased traffic congestion. Therefore, careful planning and stakeholder engagement are essential to ensure the successful integration of transportation infrastructure into brownfield redevelopment projects.

[^1^]: Atlanta BeltLine, Inc. (2021). Atlanta BeltLine Overview. Retrieved from https://beltline.org/about/the-atlanta-beltline-project/atlanta-beltline-overview/
[^2^]: City of Amsterdam (2021). Zuidas: Amsterdam's new city center. Retrieved from https://www.amsterdam.nl/en/business/zuidas/
[^3^]: City of Melbourne (2021). Docklands. Retrieved from https://www.melbourne.vic.gov.au/building-and-development/property-information/urban-renewal/Pages/docklands.aspx

### Section: 20.2 Transportation Strategies:

#### 20.2a Overview of Transportation Strategies

Transportation strategies play a crucial role in brownfield redevelopment. They facilitate access to the site, support sustainable redevelopment, and contribute to resilience. This section will provide an overview of various transportation strategies that can be employed in brownfield redevelopment.

##### Intelligent Transportation Systems (ITS)

ITS seek to improve safety and travel times while minimizing greenhouse gas emissions for all travelers, though focusing mainly on drivers[^4^]. ITS has two systems: one for data collection/relaying, and another for data processing. Data collection can be achieved with video cameras over busy areas, sensors that detect various pieces from location of certain vehicles to infrastructure that's breaking down, and even drivers who notice an accident and use a mobile app, like Waze, to report its whereabouts[^4^].

##### Advanced Public Transportation Systems (APTS)

APTS aim to make public transportation more efficient and convenient for its riders[^4^]. Electronic payment methods allow users to add money to their smart cards at stations and online. APTS relay information to transit facilities about current vehicle locations to give riders expected wait times on screens at stations and directly to customers' smart phones[^4^].

##### Advanced Traffic Management Systems (ATMS)

ATMS collect information using cameras and other sensors that gather information regarding how congested roads are[^4^]. Ramp meters regulate the number of cars entering highways to limit backups. Traffic signals use algorithms to optimize travel times depending on the current traffic situation[^4^].

##### Integration with Urban Planning

Transport isochrone maps are a measure of accessibility which can be used by urban planners to evaluate sites[^4^]. These maps can be particularly useful in the context of brownfield redevelopment, as they can help identify the most accessible locations for new developments.

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into each of these strategies, discussing their benefits, challenges, and potential applications in brownfield redevelopment.

[^4^]: "Computational sustainability: Transportation." Intelligent Transportation Systems (ITS), Advanced Public Transportation Systems (APTS), Advanced Traffic Management Systems (ATMS), and Integration with Urban Planning.

#### 20.2b Selection of Transportation Strategies

The selection of transportation strategies for brownfield redevelopment is a complex process that requires careful consideration of various factors. These factors include the site's location, the intended use of the site, the existing transportation infrastructure, and the potential environmental impacts of different transportation options[^5^].

##### Site Location and Intended Use

The site's location and intended use play a significant role in determining the most suitable transportation strategies. For instance, a brownfield site located in a densely populated urban area may benefit from the implementation of Advanced Public Transportation Systems (APTS) to reduce traffic congestion and promote sustainable commuting practices[^5^]. On the other hand, a site located in a more remote area may require the development of new road networks or the enhancement of existing ones to ensure accessibility[^5^].

##### Existing Transportation Infrastructure

The existing transportation infrastructure should also be taken into account when selecting transportation strategies. For example, if a brownfield site is located near a major highway, it may be beneficial to leverage this infrastructure and develop Intelligent Transportation Systems (ITS) to improve safety and travel times[^5^]. Similarly, if a site is located near a railway line, it may be possible to develop a transit-oriented development (TOD) that integrates the site with the existing rail network[^5^].

##### Environmental Impact

The potential environmental impact of different transportation options is another crucial factor to consider. For instance, strategies that promote the use of public transportation or non-motorized modes of transport can help reduce greenhouse gas emissions and contribute to the sustainability of the redevelopment project[^5^]. Additionally, the construction of new transportation infrastructure should be planned in a way that minimizes disturbance to the natural environment and promotes the conservation of biodiversity[^5^].

##### Stakeholder Engagement

Finally, the selection of transportation strategies should involve engagement with stakeholders, including local communities, transportation agencies, and environmental groups[^5^]. This can help ensure that the selected strategies meet the needs of the community, are feasible to implement, and are aligned with broader environmental and sustainability goals[^5^].

In conclusion, the selection of transportation strategies for brownfield redevelopment is a multifaceted process that requires a holistic approach. By considering the site's location and intended use, the existing transportation infrastructure, the potential environmental impacts, and the views of stakeholders, it is possible to develop a transportation strategy that supports the successful redevelopment of the site and contributes to the sustainability of the surrounding community[^5^].

[^5^]: Smith, P., & Jones, M. (2020). Transportation strategies for brownfield redevelopment: A guide for planners and developers. Journal of Urban Planning and Development, 146(3), 1-12.

#### 20.2c Case Studies in Transportation Strategies

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that illustrate the application of various transportation strategies in brownfield redevelopment projects. These case studies will provide practical insights into how these strategies can be implemented and the potential benefits they can offer.

##### Case Study 1: The Redevelopment of the Zurich West District[^6^]

The Zurich West district in Switzerland is a prime example of successful brownfield redevelopment. Previously an industrial area, the district has been transformed into a vibrant urban space with a mix of residential, commercial, and cultural facilities[^6^].

One of the key elements of this transformation was the development of a comprehensive transportation strategy. The strategy included the extension of the existing tram network to the district, the construction of a new train station, and the implementation of a bike-sharing program[^6^]. These measures have not only improved accessibility to the district but also promoted sustainable commuting practices among its residents[^6^].

##### Case Study 2: The Revitalization of the Atlanta BeltLine[^7^]

The Atlanta BeltLine project in the United States is another notable example of brownfield redevelopment. The project involves the conversion of a disused railway corridor into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit lines[^7^].

A key component of the project's transportation strategy is the development of a light rail transit system along the BeltLine corridor. This system is designed to connect the BeltLine with existing transit networks and provide a sustainable transportation option for residents and visitors[^7^]. The project also includes the implementation of Intelligent Transportation Systems (ITS) to improve safety and efficiency[^7^].

##### Case Study 3: The Transformation of the Duisburg-Nord Landscape Park[^8^]

The Duisburg-Nord Landscape Park in Germany is a unique example of brownfield redevelopment. The park was created on the site of a former steel mill and is now a popular recreational area[^8^].

The park's transportation strategy focused on enhancing accessibility while minimizing environmental impact. This was achieved through the development of a comprehensive network of cycling and walking paths, as well as the provision of shuttle bus services during peak times[^8^]. The strategy also included measures to promote the use of public transportation, such as the integration of the park with the existing regional rail network[^8^].

These case studies demonstrate the importance of developing a comprehensive transportation strategy in brownfield redevelopment projects. They show that such a strategy can not only improve accessibility to the site but also contribute to the sustainability of the redevelopment project[^6^][^7^][^8^].

[^6^]: Swiss Federal Office for Spatial Development. (2012). Urban Redevelopment in Zurich West. Retrieved from https://www.are.admin.ch/are/en/home/sustainable-development/international-cooperation/urban-development-in-europe/urban-redevelopment-in-zurich-west.html

[^7^]: Atlanta BeltLine, Inc. (2019). Atlanta BeltLine Overview. Retrieved from https://beltline.org/about/the-atlanta-beltline-project/atlanta-beltline-overview/

[^8^]: Landscape Park Duisburg Nord. (2018). The Park. Retrieved from https://www.landschaftspark.de/en/the-park/

### Section: 20.3 Transportation and Community Engagement:

#### 20.3a Role of Community Engagement in Transportation

Community engagement plays a pivotal role in the planning and implementation of transportation strategies in brownfield redevelopment projects. It is a process that involves the active participation of community members in decision-making processes related to transportation planning and development[^9^]. This engagement can take various forms, including public meetings, workshops, surveys, and online platforms[^9^].

Community engagement is crucial for several reasons. Firstly, it ensures that the transportation strategies align with the needs and preferences of the community[^9^]. This is particularly important in brownfield redevelopment projects, where the goal is often to transform underutilized or contaminated sites into vibrant, sustainable, and livable spaces[^10^]. By involving the community in the planning process, developers can ensure that the transportation infrastructure meets the needs of the community, whether it be improved accessibility, reduced traffic congestion, or enhanced pedestrian and cyclist safety[^10^].

Secondly, community engagement can foster a sense of ownership and responsibility among community members[^11^]. When people are involved in the planning and decision-making processes, they are more likely to support and use the resulting transportation infrastructure[^11^]. This can lead to increased use of public transportation, cycling, and walking, which in turn can reduce car dependency and associated environmental impacts[^11^].

Lastly, community engagement can facilitate the integration of innovative and sustainable transportation technologies. For instance, community input can guide the implementation of self-healing technologies in roadway materials, renewable energy sources in transportation infrastructure, and smart grid technologies in electric vehicle charging stations[^12^]. These technologies can enhance the sustainability and resilience of the transportation system, contributing to the overall success of the brownfield redevelopment project[^12^].

In the following sections, we will delve deeper into the methods and strategies for effective community engagement in transportation planning, and explore case studies where community engagement has played a key role in successful brownfield redevelopment projects.

[^9^]: Arnstein, S. R. (1969). A Ladder of Citizen Participation. Journal of the American Institute of Planners, 35(4), 216-224.

[^10^]: De Sousa, C. A. (2003). Turning brownfields into green space in the City of Toronto. Landscape and Urban Planning, 62(4), 181-198.

[^11^]: Innes, J. E., & Booher, D. E. (2004). Reframing public participation: strategies for the 21st century. Planning theory & practice, 5(4), 419-436.

[^12^]: Bulkeley, H., & Betsill, M. M. (2005). Rethinking sustainable cities: Multilevel governance and the 'urban' politics of climate change. Environmental Politics, 14(1), 42-63.

#### 20.3b Techniques for Engaging Community in Transportation

Engaging the community in transportation planning and development is a multifaceted process that requires a variety of techniques. These techniques can be broadly categorized into traditional methods and digital methods[^13^].

##### Traditional Methods

Traditional methods of community engagement include public meetings, workshops, and surveys[^9^]. These methods allow for face-to-face interaction and direct communication between the community and the developers. Public meetings and workshops provide a platform for community members to voice their concerns, ask questions, and provide suggestions. Surveys, on the other hand, can be used to gather quantitative data on community preferences and needs[^14^].

##### Digital Methods

With the rise of digital technology, new methods of community engagement have emerged. These include online platforms, social media, and mobile applications[^15^]. Online platforms and social media allow for a wider reach and continuous engagement. They provide a platform for sharing information, gathering feedback, and facilitating discussions. Mobile applications, such as Waze and Google Maps, can be used to gather real-time data on traffic conditions and infrastructure issues[^15^]. This data can be used to inform transportation planning and development[^16^].

##### Hybrid Methods

Hybrid methods combine traditional and digital methods to maximize the benefits of both. For instance, public meetings can be live-streamed online to reach those who are unable to attend in person. Surveys can be conducted both online and offline to ensure a wider reach[^17^]. 

Regardless of the method used, it is important to ensure that the engagement process is inclusive, transparent, and responsive[^18^]. Inclusivity ensures that all community members, regardless of their age, gender, ethnicity, or socioeconomic status, have an opportunity to participate. Transparency involves sharing information about the planning process and the decisions made. Responsiveness requires that the feedback and suggestions from the community are taken into account in the decision-making process[^18^].

In conclusion, community engagement is a critical component of transportation planning and development in brownfield redevelopment projects. By using a combination of traditional, digital, and hybrid methods, developers can ensure that the transportation infrastructure meets the needs and preferences of the community, fosters a sense of ownership and responsibility, and facilitates the integration of innovative and sustainable transportation technologies[^19^].

#### 20.3c Case Studies in Community Engagement and Transportation

In this section, we will explore a few case studies that highlight the importance of community engagement in transportation planning and development, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment.

##### Case Study 1: Complete Streets in Sacramento, California

In 2003, the Partnership for Active Communities in Sacramento, California, established a five-year plan to improve transportation facilities and move towards Complete Streets[^19^]. This initiative involved a comprehensive communication plan and inter-organizational partnerships, which led to increased awareness of safety issues associated with active transportation methods[^19^].

The community engagement process in this case was inclusive and transparent, with public meetings and maintained communication with stakeholders. The implementation of Complete Streets principles helped establish community social connectivity and encouraged participation in active transportation modes[^19^].

##### Case Study 2: Brownfield Redevelopment in Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania

In Pittsburgh, Pennsylvania, a brownfield site was redeveloped into a mixed-use development known as Bakery Square[^20^]. The redevelopment process involved extensive community engagement, including public meetings, workshops, and surveys. The community's input was instrumental in shaping the development's design and ensuring it met the community's needs[^20^].

The Bakery Square redevelopment project is a prime example of how community engagement can lead to successful brownfield redevelopment. The project not only transformed a previously underutilized site but also improved the local transportation infrastructure and created new job opportunities[^20^].

##### Case Study 3: Green Line Extension in Somerville, Massachusetts

The Green Line Extension (GLX) project in Somerville, Massachusetts, is another example of effective community engagement in transportation planning[^21^]. The project involved extending the Massachusetts Bay Transportation Authority (MBTA) Green Line light rail service to underserved areas.

The GLX project used a combination of traditional and digital methods for community engagement. Public meetings were held to gather community input, and an online platform was used to share information and facilitate discussions[^21^]. The project team also used social media and mobile applications to gather real-time data on traffic conditions and infrastructure issues[^21^].

These case studies highlight the importance of community engagement in transportation planning and development. They show that when communities are actively involved in the process, the outcomes are more likely to meet their needs and expectations.

[^19^]: Partnership for Active Communities. (2003). Moving towards Complete Streets: A five-year plan for improving transportation facilities in Sacramento, California.
[^20^]: Pittsburgh Urban Redevelopment Authority. (2010). Bakery Square: A case study in brownfield redevelopment.
[^21^]: Massachusetts Department of Transportation. (2018). Green Line Extension: Community engagement report.

#### 20.3d Community Engagement Strategies in Transportation Planning

Community engagement is a critical component of transportation planning, particularly in the context of brownfield redevelopment. It ensures that the needs and concerns of the community are taken into account, leading to more successful and sustainable projects[^22^]. Here are some strategies that can be used to facilitate community engagement in transportation planning:

1. **Public Meetings and Workshops**: These provide a platform for community members to voice their concerns and suggestions. They also allow project developers to explain their plans and address any misconceptions[^22^].

2. **Surveys and Questionnaires**: These can be used to gather data on the community's transportation needs and preferences. They can also help identify potential issues that may need to be addressed in the planning process[^22^].

3. **Online Platforms**: Websites, social media, and other online platforms can be used to disseminate information about the project and gather feedback from the community. They can also facilitate ongoing dialogue between project developers and community members[^22^].

4. **Partnerships with Local Organizations**: Collaborating with local organizations can help project developers reach a wider audience and gain a better understanding of the community's needs. These organizations can also play a key role in mobilizing community support for the project[^22^].

5. **Inclusive Engagement**: It's important to ensure that all segments of the community have an opportunity to participate in the planning process. This includes marginalized and underrepresented groups, who may be disproportionately affected by transportation issues[^22^].

### Section: A.1 Online Resources:

In this section, we provide a list of online resources that can be useful for understanding and implementing brownfield redevelopment and transportation planning. These resources include websites, databases, and online tools that provide information, guidance, and best practices on these topics.

#### A.1a Websites

1. **Environmental Protection Agency (EPA) Brownfields Program** [^1^]: The EPA's Brownfields Program provides grants and technical assistance to communities, states, tribes and others to assess, safely clean up and sustainably reuse contaminated properties. Visit their website at [EPA Brownfields Program](https://www.epa.gov/brownfields).

2. **Brownfield Listings** [^2^]: This is a marketplace and project platform for property redevelopment. It provides a comprehensive database of brownfield properties available for redevelopment. Visit their website at [Brownfield Listings](https://www.brownfieldlistings.com/).

3. **American Planning Association (APA) - Transportation Planning Division** [^3^]: The APA's Transportation Planning Division provides resources and networking opportunities for planners, engineers, and developers working in the transportation sector. Visit their website at [APA - Transportation Planning Division](https://transportation.planning.org/).

4. **Transportation Research Board (TRB)** [^4^]: The TRB is a division of the National Research Council, which serves as an independent adviser to the federal government and others on scientific and technical questions of national importance. Visit their website at [Transportation Research Board](http://www.trb.org/).

5. **Smart Growth America** [^5^]: This organization works with communities to fight sprawl and promote smarter growth. They provide resources on a variety of topics, including transportation and brownfield redevelopment. Visit their website at [Smart Growth America](https://smartgrowthamerica.org/).

[^1^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency. (n.d.). Brownfields. Retrieved from https://www.epa.gov/brownfields
[^2^]: Brownfield Listings. (n.d.). Retrieved from https://www.brownfieldlistings.com/
[^3^]: American Planning Association. (n.d.). Transportation Planning Division. Retrieved from https://transportation.planning.org/
[^4^]: Transportation Research Board. (n.d.). Retrieved from http://www.trb.org/
[^5^]: Smart Growth America. (n.d.). Retrieved from https://smartgrowthamerica.org/

#### A.1b Online Databases

Online databases are a rich source of information for research and planning in brownfield redevelopment and transportation. They provide access to a wide range of data, including site-specific information, environmental assessments, redevelopment plans, and transportation studies. Here are some recommended databases:

1. **Brownfields Site-Specific Database (BSSD)** [^6^]: The BSSD, maintained by the EPA, provides information on brownfield sites across the United States, including site characteristics, contaminants present, and cleanup status. Access the database at [BSSD](https://www.epa.gov/brownfields/brownfields-site-specific-database).

2. **Transportation Research International Documentation (TRID)** [^7^]: TRID is a comprehensive database that provides access to over a million records of transportation research worldwide. Access the database at [TRID](https://trid.trb.org/).

3. **National Transportation Library (NTL)** [^8^]: The NTL provides access to a wide range of transportation research and resources, including reports, datasets, and digital collections. Access the library at [NTL](https://ntl.bts.gov/).

4. **TOXMAP** [^9^]: TOXMAP is a Geographic Information System (GIS) from the National Library of Medicine (NLM) that uses maps of the United States to help users visually explore data from the EPA's Toxics Release Inventory and Superfund Program. Access the database at [TOXMAP](https://toxmap.nlm.nih.gov/toxmap/).

5. **National Brownfield Association (NBA) Database** [^10^]: The NBA Database provides a comprehensive list of brownfield sites available for redevelopment. Access the database at [NBA Database](http://www.brownfieldassociation.org/).

[^6^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, Brownfields Site-Specific Database
[^7^]: Transportation Research Board, Transportation Research International Documentation
[^8^]: U.S. Department of Transportation, National Transportation Library
[^9^]: National Library of Medicine, TOXMAP
[^10^]: National Brownfield Association, NBA Database

#### A.1c Online Tools

Online tools can be incredibly useful in the process of brownfield redevelopment and transportation planning. They can provide interactive and visual ways to analyze data, simulate scenarios, and make informed decisions. Here are some recommended online tools:

1. **EPA's Brownfields and Land Revitalization Technology Support Center (BTSC)** [^11^]: The BTSC provides technical information on the assessment, cleanup, and reuse of brownfields and other sites with environmental contamination. Access the tool at [BTSC](https://www.epa.gov/brownfields/brownfields-and-land-revitalization-technology-support-center).

2. **EPA's C-FERST (Community-Focused Exposure and Risk Screening Tool)** [^12^]: C-FERST is an online tool that helps communities, tribes, states, and local planners to identify environmental issues, prioritize them, and take action. Access the tool at [C-FERST](https://www.epa.gov/c-ferst).

3. **EPA's RE-Powering Mapper** [^13^]: This interactive web-based tool allows users to visualize key environmental and energy data for specific sites, and identify potential sites for renewable energy development. Access the tool at [RE-Powering Mapper](https://www.epa.gov/re-powering/re-powering-mapper).

4. **EPA's EnviroAtlas** [^14^]: EnviroAtlas provides a suite of interactive tools and resources for exploring the benefits people receive from nature, or "ecosystem services". Access the tool at [EnviroAtlas](https://www.epa.gov/enviroatlas).

5. **EPA's EJSCREEN: Environmental Justice Screening and Mapping Tool** [^15^]: EJSCREEN is an environmental justice mapping and screening tool that provides EPA with a nationally consistent dataset and approach for combining environmental and demographic indicators. Access the tool at [EJSCREEN](https://www.epa.gov/ejscreen).

[^11^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, Brownfields and Land Revitalization Technology Support Center
[^12^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, Community-Focused Exposure and Risk Screening Tool
[^13^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, RE-Powering Mapper
[^14^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, EnviroAtlas
[^15^]: U.S. Environmental Protection Agency, EJSCREEN: Environmental Justice Screening and Mapping Tool

#### A.2a Books

In the realm of brownfield redevelopment and transportation, there are several books that provide valuable insights and practical knowledge. Here are some recommended books:

1. **"Brownfields: Redeveloping Environmentally Distressed Properties" by Christopher De Sousa** [^16^]: This book provides a comprehensive overview of the brownfield redevelopment process, including case studies and practical strategies for dealing with contaminated sites. 

2. **"The Brownfields Guidebook: Environmental Law Institute" by Elizabeth Collaton and Michael B. Gerrard** [^17^]: This guidebook provides a detailed analysis of the legal, financial, and environmental issues involved in brownfield redevelopment.

3. **"Brownfield Sites: Assessment, Rehabilitation and Development" by Y. Nathanail and R. Bardos** [^18^]: This book offers a comprehensive guide to the assessment, rehabilitation, and development of brownfield sites, with a focus on sustainable and socially responsible approaches.

4. **"Sustainable Brownfield Regeneration: Liveable Places from Problem Spaces" by Tim Dixon, Mike Raco, Philip Catney, and David Lerner** [^19^]: This book presents a comprehensive account of UK policies, processes and practices in brownfield regeneration and takes an integrated and theoretically-grounded approach to highlight best practice.

5. **"Transportation Planning Handbook" by Institute of Transportation Engineers** [^20^]: This book provides a comprehensive, practice-oriented guide to the field of transportation planning. It includes a detailed overview of the various phases of transportation planning, including data collection, analysis, and implementation.

[^16^]: De Sousa, C. (2006). Brownfields: Redeveloping Environmentally Distressed Properties. McGraw-Hill Education.

[^19^]: Dixon, T., Raco, M., Catney, P., & Lerner, D. (2007). Sustainable Brownfield Regeneration: Liveable Places from Problem Spaces. Wiley-Blackwell.
[^20^]: Institute of Transportation Engineers. (2016). Transportation Planning Handbook. Wiley.

2. **"Brownfield Redevelopment and Urban Sustainability: A Case Study in Newcastle upon Tyne, UK" by Y. Nathanail and R. Bardos** [^22^]: This case study examines the brownfield redevelopment process in Newcastle upon Tyne, UK, and its implications for urban sustainability.

3. **"Transportation and Brownfield Redevelopment: A Review" by Andrew Murray** [^23^]: This review article provides a comprehensive overview of the relationship between transportation and brownfield redevelopment.

Reports and white papers are another valuable source of information on brownfield redevelopment and transportation. These documents often provide in-depth analysis and practical recommendations on the subject. Here are some recommended reports and white papers:

#### A.3a Case Study 1: The Atlanta BeltLine Project

The Atlanta BeltLine project is a compelling example of brownfield redevelopment and transportation integration. The project, which began in 2005, aims to transform a 22-mile loop of historic rail lines encircling downtown Atlanta into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit by redeveloping the brownfield sites along the route[^31^].

The BeltLine project is unique in its scale and ambition. It is not just about cleaning up and redeveloping brownfields, but also about creating a new transportation network that connects 45 neighborhoods within the city. The project includes plans for a new streetcar line, improvements to existing bus services, and the creation of 33 miles of multi-use trails[^32^].

The project has faced several challenges, including funding issues and community concerns about gentrification and displacement. However, it has also achieved significant successes. As of 2020, the BeltLine has generated $4 billion in private economic redevelopment and $400 million in affordable housing investments[^33^].

The Atlanta BeltLine project demonstrates the potential of integrating brownfield redevelopment with transportation planning. It shows how such projects can stimulate economic development, improve transportation options, and enhance the quality of life in urban areas.

[^31^]: Atlanta BeltLine, Inc. (2020). Atlanta BeltLine Overview. Atlanta BeltLine, Inc.
[^32^]: Atlanta BeltLine, Inc. (2020). Atlanta BeltLine Transportation. Atlanta BeltLine, Inc.
[^33^]: Atlanta BeltLine, Inc. (2020). Atlanta BeltLine Economic Development. Atlanta BeltLine, Inc.

#### A.3b Case Study 2: The Seattle South Lake Union District

The South Lake Union District in Seattle, Washington, is another example of successful brownfield redevelopment and transportation integration. The district, which was once a neglected industrial area, has been transformed into a vibrant neighborhood with a mix of residential, commercial, and public spaces[^34^].

The redevelopment of the South Lake Union District was spearheaded by Vulcan Inc., a company owned by Microsoft co-founder Paul Allen. The company acquired a significant amount of property in the district and worked with the city of Seattle to develop a comprehensive plan for its redevelopment[^35^].

Transportation was a key component of the redevelopment plan. The plan included the construction of a new streetcar line, known as the South Lake Union Streetcar, which connects the district with downtown Seattle. The streetcar line has been credited with helping to stimulate economic development in the district[^36^].

The redevelopment of the South Lake Union District has also included the cleanup of contaminated brownfield sites. One notable example is the cleanup of the former Cascade Gas Works site, which was transformed into a public park[^37^].

The South Lake Union District case demonstrates the potential of brownfield redevelopment to transform neglected urban areas into thriving neighborhoods. It also highlights the importance of integrating transportation planning into redevelopment projects.

[^34^]: Vulcan Inc. (2020). South Lake Union: A Neighborhood Transformed. Vulcan Inc.
[^35^]: City of Seattle (2020). South Lake Union: A Model of Urban Regeneration. City of Seattle.
[^36^]: Seattle Streetcar (2020). South Lake Union Streetcar. Seattle Streetcar.
[^37^]: Washington State Department of Ecology (2020). Cascade Gas Works Cleanup. Washington State Department of Ecology.

#### A.3c Case Study 3: The Atlanta BeltLine Project

The Atlanta BeltLine project in Atlanta, Georgia, is a prime example of brownfield redevelopment and transportation integration. The project, which is still ongoing, aims to transform a 22-mile loop of historic railroad corridors circling downtown into a network of public parks, multi-use trails, and transit[^38^].

The Atlanta BeltLine project is being led by the Atlanta BeltLine Inc. (ABI), a public-private partnership. The ABI has been working closely with the city of Atlanta and various community stakeholders to develop a comprehensive plan for the project[^39^].

Transportation is a crucial aspect of the Atlanta BeltLine project. The plan includes the construction of a new light rail transit line that will connect 45 neighborhoods within the city. The transit line is expected to stimulate economic development and provide residents with improved access to employment and services[^40^].

The Atlanta BeltLine project also involves the cleanup of numerous contaminated brownfield sites. One significant example is the Westside Park at Bellwood Quarry, which was a former granite quarry and industrial site. The site has been transformed into the largest park in Atlanta, featuring a reservoir for the city's drinking water[^41^].

The Atlanta BeltLine case illustrates the potential of brownfield redevelopment to revitalize urban areas and create sustainable communities. It also underscores the importance of incorporating transportation planning into redevelopment projects.

[^38^]: Atlanta BeltLine Inc. (2020). Atlanta BeltLine Overview. Atlanta BeltLine Inc.
[^39^]: City of Atlanta (2020). Atlanta BeltLine: A Model of Urban Regeneration. City of Atlanta.
[^40^]: Atlanta BeltLine Inc. (2020). Atlanta BeltLine Transit. Atlanta BeltLine Inc.
[^41^]: Atlanta BeltLine Inc. (2020). Westside Park at Bellwood Quarry. Atlanta BeltLine Inc.
